The Bureau was involved in cultural exchanges (naturally), propaganda efforts (of course), and possibly intelligence-sharing (!).

This was not the extent of communist-nationalist cooperation in postimperial China. Until the purging of Shanghai communists under Chiang, the CCP together with the Nationalist ruling party formed a “United Front”—the first one. Together, they opposed local warlord action and unified China. Fast-forwarding a bit, Mao’s insurrection beginning in 1927 in response to Shanghai would not prove initially successful as envisioned. But with the invasion of China by imperial Japan in 1936, a temporary truce was called. Chiang’s own generals detained him to force him into the deal. A Second United Front between Nationalists and the CCP was formed. In the following years, including World War II, the communists fought two wars—a hot war against Japan and a cold one against their frenemy the Nationalists, expanding their influence across China and establishing CCP-allied guerrilla bases from which to operate openly against their own government when the time was right.

Communists cozy up to power, play nice as long as necessary, and backstab when and only when they have marked advantage. That was the case from 1945 through 1949. A world war had ended, and so would a regional one. Thanks to CCP OPE, it could only go one way.

There is an alternative path that Stage 3 can take, however, so it’s worth bringing up—it is the dual power strategy. This refers to the coexistence of institutions of the old world and those upstart institutions of the new order. For a time, there is a coexistence of ever-weakening institutions opposed to the revolutions—think Tsarist Russia and its military, banking, property, and other systems—and those gaining strength, in this case the various Soviet councils. As loyalty to the old way dissolves and the energy of the new rises, the dying institutions have fewer and fewer means of enacting their will and maintaining their version of law and order. This is sometimes done directly by infiltrators of the institutions themselves, those revolutionaries who ensure the existing power structures align with the new ones to ensure maximum alignment with the cause. Either way, through infiltration or dual power play, the struggle is “fought” until it’s actually fought—and by then it may be too late to fight back.

To Divide Is to Conquer

You can see from these three steps that repurposing standard military intelligence and special forces procedures—OPE—gives unhuman radicals strategic advantages. The greatest of these in our view is organization. One side is monolithically ready for the outbreak of total war and committed to absolute victory; the other may not even be in power, or is lost holding on to the old ways for dear life as what power they do have slips through their fingers.

Power chases persuasion; OPE creates it, and so the people follow. Pre-revolutionary activities work, but why exactly do they work? Why does “dividing” bring communists upward of 90 percent of the way toward “conquering”?

To understand why unhumans do what they do, understand their frame: anti-civilization. Their aim is to undo the humanity of those they deem guilty before charged. These ends justify any means. Anything and everything unhumans do is a derivative of the anti-civilization frame—from opposition to life, to opposition to liberty, to opposition to property. The hypothetical antisocialite isn’t hypothetical. Communism is a super magnet for discontents and fringes— proletariat and peasant and anyone else who is a have-not and feels they ought to be a have-more. Perhaps they aren’t losers because of their own failings. Perhaps they were oppressed. The objective of OPE then is to organize as many potential subversives (the followers) as possible under the direction of too-far-gone unhumans (their leaders). The unhumans have called the disaffected out of their situation, brought them into conscious and painful awareness of their lot in life, and then “other” those perceived to unfairly “have” at their continued expense. In short, effective OPE gives revolutionary leaders a powder keg of angry, bitter people. All they have to do is light it.

That’s what they do next.

cF0

CHAPTER 4

THREE ACTS, THREE OUTCOMES: THE PLAN FOR REVOLUTION

Men at some time are masters of their fates.

—Gaius Julius Caesar

A communist revolution in Rome?

The late Roman republic (133–31 BC) is not often if ever considered a stage for class warfare to play out. In fact, the earliest conceptualization of what we know as “communism” today dates only to the year 1840. And yet the word itself derives from the Latin communis, meaning “shared” or “common.”

One hundred years after the founding of Rome (753 BC), Greek philosopher Plato suggested in The Republic that the ruling classes of society could, to form an ideal state, “share the wealth,” as modern leftists would say.1 He argued, in a sense, that wealth meant both property and family—basically socialism and an early version of polyamory. We aren’t suggesting Plato was a proto-communist, but he was at minimum directionally aligned.

If the seeds of social change had already germinated in Roman times, why then did that period bear no red fruit? Why no proto-communist revolution? Well, they came closer than you think. If you know anything about the Roman republic, you know the land was fertile. The first society The Communist Manifesto depicts as exhibiting class warfare is, interestingly, Rome. Throughout the republican period, the plebeians aired their grievances against the patricians. In those days, there was no middle class. To be a plebeian was to be a second-class citizen in a class of two. Plebeians were manual laborers, agricultural workers, even tradesmen and craftsmen and small business owners—a far cry from our day. The patricians were the elite, in every sense of the word. They had the rights. They owned the land. They called the shots. Dang, it feels good to be a patrician.

Not cool, to the plebeians. Change was coming. For two centuries, from 495 BC through 287 BC, there spanned the Struggle of the Orders. This was a protracted period of protests, strikes, and the very early granting of something we might call civil rights, to the plebeians. But of course, equality under the law even in theory is not equality of outcome. It never is. By the first century BC, a four-hundred-plus-year-old republic was crumbling under itself, as republics are wont to do, the natural consequence of fiscal bloat, naked corruption, and overextension in foreign wars. We can’t relate.

In the late Roman Republic, wealth disparity was extreme, with a small minority of elites controlling the majority of wealth and resources. The patrician class held vast estates, dominated political offices, and enjoyed luxuries beyond the imagination of the common people. Meanwhile, the majority of Romans, including plebeians and noncitizen residents, struggled to make ends meet, facing poverty, debt, and limited opportunities for advancement. The economic gap was so pronounced that it contributed to social unrest, political corruption, and ultimately the collapse of the Republic.

As we will see, vast income inequality and disparity always, always leads to unrest, which causes a government to either be forced into reform or face a revolution. The question of whether that revolt goes communist or populist depends on a variety of factors. Chief among them, is the rise of charismatic leaders committed to their cause, as we will see. But charisma isn’t enough by itself to win a revolution.

Then and now, hard assets are the surest stores of wealth. In Rome, that wealth remained with the patrician class, landownership, specifically. This gave the unhumans of that age purchase to build their class resentment agitprop, as they always do, from Rome to France to Russia to Spain to China … to today.

To digress only slightly, in early Soviet Russia, the Bolsheviks claimed they wanted land reform for the landless working poor. Their specific targets were the kulaks (the upper middle class including small business owners). Yes, that was the plan: Take from the not-rich and give it to the poor. That didn’t work out so well.

Prior to the Spanish Civil War, redistribution of land and collectivization occurred. The socialist republic of 1930s Spain aimed to break up large estates and redistribute them to landless peasants and laborers. In regions where radicals operated freely, there were efforts to promote collective ownership and management of land through agrarian collectives or cooperatives—pooling resources and labor to cultivate land collectively. Literal communism.

And throughout early to mid twentieth century China, communists also promised land reform for their peasantry. Mao Zedong did, in fact, deliver. Remember the Chinese peasant revolts we mentioned in the last chapter? In less than a decade, thousands of landowners were executed by Mao’s mobs, although some estimates suggest the mass murder ran into the millions. The reason: disparate outcomes; 6 percent of the population owned 80 percent of the land, while 80 percent of the population owned only 20 percent of the land.2 Enter, the struggle session.

During struggle sessions, communists would drag landowners from their homes, hurl all manner of accusations at them, sentence them to death, and shoot them. In other situations, humiliation and demoralization proved more useful than execution. From Mao’s Report on an Investigation of the Peasant Movement in Hunan, published in 1927, still the early days of the long march of communism across China, comes this report of the revolutionary “association” and their campaign against the landowning “gentry.” This gets dark quick:

A big crowd is rallied to demonstrate against a local tyrant or one of the evil gentry who is an enemy of the association. The demonstrators eat at the offender’s house, slaughtering his pigs and consuming his grain as a matter of course. Quite a few such cases have occurred … After such demonstrations, the peasants usually impose fines. “Crowning” the landlords and parading them through the villages. [sic] This sort of thing is very common. A tall paper-hat is stuck on the head of one of the local tyrants or evil gentry, bearing the words “Local tyrant so-and-so” or “So-and-so of the evil gentry.” He is led by a rope and escorted with big crowds in front and behind. Sometimes brass gongs are beaten and flags waved to attract people’s attention. This form of punishment more than any other makes the local tyrants and evil gentry tremble. Anyone who has once been crowned with a tall paper-hat loses face altogether and can never again hold up his head. Hence many of the rich prefer being fined to wearing the tall hat. But wear it they must, if the peasants insist.3

Now, watch this: terror. It’s the favorite tool of the unhuman, and it works.

One ingenious township peasant association arrested an obnoxious member of the gentry and announced that he was to be crowned that very day. The man turned blue with fear. Then the association decided not to crown him that day. They argued that if he were crowned right away, he would become case-hardened and no longer afraid, and that it would be better to let him go home and crown him some other day. Not knowing when he would be crowned, the man was in daily suspense, unable to sit down or sleep at ease.4

The unhumanity of Chairman Mao will return in a later chapter. The point to be taken now is that the have-nots, be they in China or elsewhere, are a stiruppable lot. We see more parallels to this in the class conflict and corrupt leadership of late-republic Rome and eighteenth-century France. Rome could have devolved into a free-for-all like the French later would, and that seemed to be Rome’s vector right around the year 50 BC.

And so we return to the question: Why didn’t ancient Rome suffer a proto-communist revolution? Because one strong man said, Not on my watch, but he did so “in deeds not words,” in acta non verba. That man was Caesar.

No previous book or documentary we’re aware of has viewed ancient Rome through this lens of proto-communism. And the reason why, we believe, is that the fall of the Roman republic was not anything remotely like a left-wing success story. Rather, it offers a case study on how one great man of history may crush an uprising before it even occurs. And there are only three ways to do it—to defeat the unhumans. As we shall see, the case of Rome is two of them. So the end of that story will satisfy, let’s rewind to the beginning—to the start of a communist revolution, of any communist revolution, and to the three “acts” the radicals’ arc always follows. Then, we’ll come full circle—to the three ways to defeat them.

Three Acts to Power: The Arc Every Revolution Follows

Karl Marx and Frederik Engels, authors of The Communist Manifesto, published in 1848, did not decide how the unhuman revolution unfolds. They merely documented it. As we’ve pointed out and will continue to, particularly when we fly to France in the next chapter (a revolution predating Marx and Engels by seventy-five years), violent left-wing radicalism predates modernity by centuries. For as long as there has been civilization, there have been forces opposed to it: the anti-civilizationists. Societies are built on law and order and always have been, even when they were constructed with mud, straw, sticks, and sheepskin. And for that long, there have been the lawless, the bitter, the nihilistic. And they’ve done what they must, as we’ve seen, to win the people—enough people—to their side. That’s when they charge. Once that necessary OPE work is done, this is what’s next.

The Revolution, Act I: Incite

By this point in every revolutionary story, the house is divided against itself and so cannot stand. Deep battle lines have already been drawn, dividing oppressed from oppressor; have-nots from haves; my life sucks from it’s all your fault. Envy has been advertised, with that message reinforced. Many souls have fallen to the unhuman state of pure hate. And their overlords of the coming rebellion, the instigators of it all, have the allies and access they need to possess hard and soft power.

And so it begins. The Communist Manifesto, famously referring to the have-nots as the proletariat class and the haves as the bourgeoisie, reveals:

In depicting the most general phases of the development of the proletariat, we traced the more or less veiled civil war, raging within existing society, up to the point where that war breaks out into open revolution, and where the violent overthrow of the bourgeoisie lays the foundation for the sway of the proletariat.5

You caught that, didn’t you? The part about “war” and “open revolution” and “violent overthrow”? The incitement is when the cause becomes conflict. When the glass is first broken, first shots fired, first bloodshed. It is the storming of the Bastille, to preview Chapter 5. The incitement is the unleashing of the unhumans, when first it occurs. It is always ugly. And it’s almost always a surprise to those being unleashed upon.

By the way, democratic socialism with passive-aggressive action against the normal people, this is unacceptable to Marx and Engels. There is no revolution without violence. A revolution is not a dinner party, Mao said. Garden-variety socialists like the Mensheviks, they write, “wish to attain their ends by peaceful means” yet are “doomed to failure.”6

And that’s why they incite. Then they do this:

The Revolution, Act II: Seize

In the Second Act, the have-nots, after shock-and-terror violence unlike any their fellow citizens have ever borne witness to, shift from the first killing to prolonged robbing. They take your life, your liberty, and your property, in reverse order and in perverse ways (i.e., they steal your stuff, suspend your rights, then end your life).

These terrifying acts of seizure can take days such as in Russia, months as in Spain, or over decades as in China. The landlord-robbing account you read earlier occurred in the 1920s; Mao Zedong ruled the people of China into ruin until his death in 1976.

Marx and Engels include in the closing paragraphs of The Communist Manifesto this reminder regarding the second act:

In short, the Communists everywhere support every revolutionary movement against the existing social and political order of things.

In all these movements they bring to the front, as the leading question in each, the property question, no matter what its degree of development at the time.7

Translation: The most important thing is we are going to take all your stuff.

Earlier in the material, The Communist Manifesto is uncomfortably specific about the seizing that must occur for “justice” to be had. The complete and total robbing done by the unhumans includes:

“Abolition of property in land and application of all rents of land to public purposes.”

“A heavy progressive or graduated income tax.”

“Abolition of all right of inheritance.”

“Confiscation of the property of all emigrants and rebels.”

“Centralisation of credit in the hands of the State, by means of a national bank with State capital and an exclusive monopoly.”

“Centralisation of the means of communication and transport in the hands of the State.”

“Extension of factories and instruments of production owned by the State; the bringing into cultivation of waste-lands, and the improvement of the soil generally in accordance with a common plan.”8

No house, no land, no savings, no nest egg, no freedom. If the progressive platitude “You’ll own nothing, and you’ll be happy,” had a plan, this would be it. But it gets worse, if you’re a woman. Marx and Engels say elsewhere:

The Communists … desire to introduce, in substitution for a hypocritically concealed, an openly legalised community of women.9

You know what a legalized community of women is, don’t you? Following a successful communist revolution, men are to have sexual access to women at all times at no cost to themselves. That is, women become the sexual “community” property of men who have no responsibility to them or the children they father. But hey, at least the unhumans abolished so-called capitalist prostitution! (Tell us your movement is comprised of incels without telling us your movement is comprised of incels.)

As with all instigator promises to their followers, the Universal Basic Girlfriend guarantee is only fulfilled if you’re in charge. Plot twist: The incels are not.

The Revolution, Act III: Purge

The Third Act is the literal removal of those labeled oppressors and all memory of them from the commons. This great purging has also been called (and has likewise involved) ethnic cleansing, forced displacement, genocide, famine, brainwashing, and, simply, massacre. Monuments are torn down, new ones built. Old heroes are written out of history, unhumans write of themselves as “on the right side” of it. The next generation must be made to forget what was lost—if a next generation of haves is even permitted to survive the purgings of Act Three.

The Cultural Revolution of China is an obvious example of a ruthless and thorough purging. From the landlords to the loyalists of any non-communist cause, to their spouses and children and extended family and even friends, all those who were not with the oppressed were now part of the oppressors. We’ll cover the specifics of Chinese revolutionary terror in Chapter 8. For now, understand that Mao did as his inspirations Marx and Engels advised. These excerpts from The Communist Manifesto:

“The proletariat, the lowest stratum of our present society, cannot stir, cannot raise itself up, without the whole superincumbent strata of official society being sprung into the air.”10

“The immediate aim of the Communists is the same as that of all other proletarian parties: formation of the proletariat into a class, overthrow of the bourgeois supremacy, conquest of political power by the proletariat.”11

“In place of the old bourgeois society, with its classes and class antagonisms, we shall have an association, in which the free development of each is the condition for the free development of all.”12

We’ve bolded the essentials of purging. Before you reread and try to interpret what else is said in those excerpts, remember that we are not dealing with a coherent philosophy of subtle geniuses. The revolution is simply envy in action. And casting up and away, overthrowing, conquering, replacing … this is the language of cruel excision. The specifics are left up to the mob.

This the Third Act is not optional because the first two are not enough to the communist. In 1921, Vladimir Lenin famously said, “The whole question is—who will overtake whom?” Comrade Leon Trotsky shortened this to, “Who, whom?” Joseph Stalin ran with that and made it policy. So what does it mean?

“Who, whom?” means peaceful coexistence is impossible for the true believer. Either the haves will win, or the have-nots will, the unhumans believe, and victory must be total and unilateral. The only outcome is annihilation. Elimination. Purging.

Who will overtake whom?

Where from Here

Do you know how every communist revolution finally ends? The last line of the Manifesto is simply, “WORKING MEN OF ALL COUNTRIES, UNITE!”13 This is the call to action—a call to speed-run the Three Acts. Reading these and other writings of Marx, Engels, and other influential socialists of their day and after, you get the gist of their goal—to achieve something like “fairness,” that is, a “fair share” being redistributed to the aggrieved groups out of the pockets, homes, and bank accounts of those deemed guilty of all that unfairness. Then and only then will there be utopia, according to the unhumans.

That doesn’t happen. That’s never what happens following a communist revolution. What does happen (every time) is the instigators of uprising enrich themselves at the expense of all. The purpose of a system is what it does, and this is the purpose of revolution: to rob and kill the normal people. There is no great realignment of society for the benefit of the many not the few.

As Will and Ariel Durant write in The Lessons of History, “Nothing is clearer in history than the adoption by successful rebels of the methods they were accustomed to condemn in the forces they deposed.”14 And so the plebeians, peasants, and working poor are always worse off after an unhuman revolution than before. An old elite has been cast aside to make way for the new, and any and all middle classes were hollowed out, their collective wealth sent upward, not down. This is what they do.

So, to review, the revolutionary’s three-part playbook is to:

Incite violent conflict there is no coming back from.

Seize property rights, civil rights, and the right to life itself.

Purge the collectively guilty classes of people from society, living memory, and recorded history.

That’s heavy. But there is hope. These things aren’t inevitable.

Fighting Back: The Three Ways to Crush a Communist Uprising

We like civilization. We don’t like anti-civilization. If you don’t either, then you owe it to yourself to learn how to force-stop the destruction of all that you hold dear. History doesn’t repeat, but it rhymes. And three defeats of communism echo to us down through the millennia.

How to Defeat the Unhumans: Let Them Eat Themselves

Imagine a snake. It’s slithering about in search of prey. There in the grass alongside it, motion. A victim. Strike. And the snake eats itself. Its own tail, dinner.

The ancient Greeks knew that a predator suffering enough hunger could lose all instinct of self-preservation and devour its own flesh. This reality they embedded into the symbol of the Ouroboros—the snake that eats itself. You might have seen that symbol before. This explains it.

The unhumans are scavengers by their nature. Once all prey have been chewed and excised by tooth and claw or suspension of law, they have a choice: Go hungry, or keep eating.

You may have read or heard that the Soviet Union collapsed under its own weight. More so, the thing ate itself. When a society’s haves are driven out, driven underground, or driven into the ground continuously and unceasingly as a matter of policy and on a long enough time scale, there is nothing left to be had. A command-and-control economy planned centrally by potbellied bureaucrats becomes a non-economy. Eventually, it dies and decays, and so begins the protracted period of bone-picking and marrow-slurping whatever’s left. That was Russia and its former republics-turned-independents in the 1990s. Communism dies, but what lives on in its place isn’t much of a living. The way Americans of the 1950s remembered the Great Depression and all the suffering and bitter sacrifices that period entailed, Russian nationals today remember the ’90s.

Suffice it to say, the United States’ Cold War policy to “contain” communism—designed and recommended by diplomat George F. Kennan—worked. Not perfectly, as we’ll see Soviet influence the world over in upcoming chapters. But containment did make the red snake feast on itself. This outcome is acceptable if you are an observer. If you are party to the snake, not so much. (As an aside, the opposite is true in twenty-first-century geopolitics. It is Western globalists who are the expansionary, ideologically driven power, no longer the Communist Party of Russia or anywhere else.)

For the sake of all those who survive the communist revolution, we rate waiting it out as a less-than-ideal approach to defeat the unhumans. Yes, it can work. No, it’s not pretty. Fortunately, we have options—the same ones Caesar took.

How to Defeat the Unhumans: Find the Great Men of History

If the first way to fight the forces of unhumanity is nobody do anything, the second way is one person do everything. That is the gist. It is Francisco Franco during the Spanish Civil War. It is Gaius Julius Caesar in the turmoil of ancient Rome. Wrangel could have made it in Russia.

In Chapter 7, we’ll see Franco crush the communists. Then in Chapter 13, we’ll see what anyone can do to support, advise, or even become the one who says, as Franco did with steel resolve, “Wherever I am, there will be no communism.”

How to Defeat the Unhumans: Win before Fighting

It would be nice if every uprising met its more-than-a-match in a strong man (or, yes, woman, we write as we recall the warrior-prophetess Deborah from the Bible). In most of our lives, there are no Hollywood moments. No nick-of-time rescue from an impossible situation with no way out. That’s why there is despair. It’s why many have-nots dream no more dreams. That’s why there are revolutions.

In our present time, many otherwise good, smart citizens passively wait. We go to work, pay the bills, catch the game, keep up with the news, save for rainy days and maybe retirement (if not for student loans). Most of us don’t worry about anything that’s not on next week’s schedule. After all, this is the age of the superhero action flick, where someone is coming to save us. What if there’s not? What if you are destined to be a Tank Man—the otherwise unknown individual who stands before the literal armies of unhumanity, in eternal defiance? One man slowing down the tanks rolling into Tiananmen Square in 1989 didn’t collapse communism. What if there had been one million Tank Men? That’s a different story. Consider telling it.

We can do the work of saving ourselves—because we might not have another choice. This the third way may be the best way. We, the normal people, can band together, take united and decisive action, and nip revolution in the bud. We can win before fighting—as long as we understand that a fight is coming. This, too, is what Caesar did. He did not wait for the collapse. He crossed the Rubicon with his bros in tow.

Does this mean you need an army? Lock up the enemy? We are not suggesting lawless violence. We are suggesting to do what you can in the political realm —anything you can—before it’s too late. Before the terror that paralyzes.

We’ll bring you back to this moment of truth and path to victory in Chapter 13 to close out our time together, and with specifics. Leftists say that “real communism has never been tried,” but the second and third ways to defeat have been tried and work.

Caesar chose himself. He and his allies restored order and brought glory to a fragile civilization and feckless government. He is why men today think about the Roman empire every day. Those who forget the past may be condemned to repeat it, but those who understand it are guaranteed to avenge it.

There is much more to spell out here that has been untold. Knowing it, even a little of it, will empower you to beat the revolutionaries at their own game. Victory means protection of everything that has been passed to us in society. Defeat is the end of everything you hold dear. It’s humanity versus unhumanity. In the next few chapters, we’ll give you a taste of how that battle has played out over the last 250 years.

cJG

CHAPTER 5

THE FRENCH TERROR: THE UNTOLD STORY OF THE COMMUNIST FRENCH REVOLUTION

The poor have done everything; it is time for the rich to take their turn.

—Jean-Paul Marat

You may know France today as a middling European power with a generous social safety net, lots of baguettes placed outside bakeries, high fashion, and a tendency for their leaders to commit adultery. But their leaders were still sleeping around back then—and more.

France in the 1700s was the most powerful country in continental Europe. They had the highest population, the most productive agriculture, a large and strong military, and a culture the envy of their peers. Everyone who mattered in the West learned French, for it was the language of international diplomacy and science, much like English is today. The American Founding Fathers knew French; Benjamin Franklin and Thomas Jefferson spent considerable time in Paris. Peter the Great and his aristocracy brought French culture to Russia. France was the epicenter of the Western world, and it had been that way for hundreds of years. Only for all of it to blow up in a five-year period. As it turns out, the French Revolution was a proto-communist revolution.

The French Revolution saw everything of the ancien régime thrown out and reduced to ashes—religion, social codes, the nobility, all of it. Many of the ideas that took root then are ones we’re still dealing with now. Indeed, the very terms “left-wing” and “right-wing” that we all still use today came precisely from France’s National Assembly early in the Revolution, where social revolutionaries sat to the left and traditionalist monarchists sat to the right.

Before the Bastille

The French Revolution was not inspired by the American Revolution despite the two happening only a few years apart and despite the relationship between the American Founding Fathers and French elite friendly to the cause. But like the American Revolution, the actions of Great Britain set the French on the course to revolt, though not to the same degree. By the waning decades of the eighteenth century, France was losing its percentage of global empire-share to Britain. France lost the Seven Years’ War to Britain—what we call the French and Indian War in America—as well as several colonial possessions. England’s mercantile strength had risen as well, to the point they had the beginnings of their global maritime commercial empire they would become famous for in the nineteenth century. Meanwhile, France, while still strong, had begun its downward trend.

But the deepest roots of the eventual revolution were internal to France. The Enlightenment, an intellectual current that upended Europe’s traditional deference to the Church, its traditions, and the culture it gave rise to, came to fruition in the eighteenth century— and it drove many American revolutionary values. Names such as Montesquieu, Jean-Jacques Rousseau, and the like came out of this period.1 And yet despite the progress, the French government was considered a relic. In their absolute monarchy, the king held near-total power over the country and the fate of all inhabitants. Most authority was centralized around him and his court. Even so, the current French monarchy wasn’t some age-old tradition that stretched back to the Middle Ages. It was a more recent political idea, a response to the traumas of the Protestant Reformation and the subsequent warfare it brought upon the Catholic world. How do you strengthen a weakened state? You give it a strong leader. For Catholic France, this was the Bourbon monarchy.

Not two centuries into the modern king thing, the bill came due. This is literal; the French monarchy had borrowed too much money and couldn’t pay.2 (Keep in mind that there is no law of economics or physics that prevents this from happening in modern times, for those of you keeping score at home.)

Due to the economic crunch, King Louis XVI needed help and a plan from parliament, known as the Estates-General. But there was one problem: They hadn’t met in more than 150 years. The king now needed a parliament, as that body had previously held authority over taxation. This would also conveniently shift the blame for the tax hike off the king. Louis XVI hoped higher taxes would cover his empire’s mounting debts. The king’s officials spent several months visiting France’s various regions, all mostly rural, and delegates for the Estates-General were chosen. Louis XVI also asked his subjects across France to submit their grievances to the Estates-General; we forget how inconvenient basic surveys were in the age before instant social media polling and online reviews.

Well, the one-star reviews came fast and loose. If there’s one thing the French love above all else, it’s criticism. The French complained about paying too many taxes already. Too many old feudal duties remained. The Church held too much power and had been corrupted. Louis XVI realized there would be no new taxation without an official response to these grievances. So he began conceding to the demands of his people. This was the first sign of trouble. We’ll explain why.

The Estates-General was a parliament divided into three bodies: the First Estate (the clergy), the Second Estate (the nobility), and the Third Estate (the people). And because the Third Estate was so vast, they felt they were the true representatives of France, not the aristocrats or the Church. A classic have-nots versus haves situation. Because the Third Estate represented the common folk, Louis XVI decided (unwisely) to exclude them from proceedings. Nowadays, we might call this an “own goal.” The commoners of parliament didn’t respond well. Louis XVI completed the radicals’ Separation Stage for them.

And so the Third Estate, fired up now, met separately and privately at an abandoned tennis court and swore they would not disperse until they had established a constitution for France—the “Tennis Court Oath.”3 Consider the timing: The United States constitution was being written during this very period, and the everyday people of France were not about to be left out.

The ideas proposed during Third Estate constitutional debate were not entirely radical, at first, as they never are. For example, turning the nobility into an honorific title with no special authority was already on the table; Louis XVI might have even accepted that change. But dreams of egalitarianism can fast become nightmares of vengeance. The Third Estate flexed their proletariat might; they announced the formation of a National Assembly, asserting popular sovereignty over and above the divine right of kings. This was in effect the message of this upstart institution: We alone are the true voice of France. Vox Populi. The environment for uprising was well-prepared.

Supporters of the National Assembly struck first. At that time in France, there was no greater symbol of monarchist tyranny than the Bastille, an already ancient prison. Numerous would-be instigators of uprisings against the king had disappeared behind its castle walls over the previous decades, never to be seen or heard from again. Although the prison was not in nearly as much use—the Bastille held fewer than ten prisoners at that time—seizing the king’s private prison won the revolutionaries great symbolic value.4 Victory would also give the radicals gunpowder, arms, and ammunition.

On July 14, 1789, a fiery but mostly peaceful protest began (like the United States in 2020). The mob breached the gates and butchered the officer in charge. And so the French Revolution had begun. The morale of the people was never higher; the government’s ability to restore order, never weaker. Historians argue over who, exactly, was in charge of the mob as word of the Bastille’s fate spread; mob became mobs, and soon all French cities had them. We’d argue the rioters then and there (or here) needed no direction. This was an army densely ranked and filled with have-nots who, as in twentieth-century Russia, had legitimate grievances with the old feudal ways. But the prudence of America’s Founding Fathers was nowhere to be seen in mainland Europe. France fell into a state of unhumanity due to those who overran it. They had no plan or policy, but what they did have was the greatest advantage the unhuman could ever lust after: organization. A lot of people were angry, they came together, and they shared one common goal. Two, actually: Rob, and kill.

Now, what was the response to all this? Well, the great irony is that King Louis XVI was not the brutal, iron-fisted tyrant depicted in popular culture. He was a weak figurehead of his own government. He lacked the will to his own power, content to coast on legacy. His brother urged him to either flee the country along with other nobles, or crack down. Marie Antoinette as well, was not some obstinate socialite. In fact, she gave greatly to philanthropic endeavors and founded charity houses all over France. There is no evidence whatsoever that she ever said, “Let them eat cake,” but the fact you associate it with her is a perfect example of the power of communist messaging operations.5 Even two hundred years later, everyone knows this (false) quote of hers.

As the revolution continued, the most organized of the unhumans made themselves into little dictators in ground zero of the uprising: Paris. The king did nothing. It would be two years before he did something. And that was to try to escape to a loyalist region of France, in 1791. Totally and completely botched. King Louis lacked the organization of his adversaries. He and wife Marie Antoinette and their children were caught and detained by revolutionaries, then sent back to Paris and placed under strict surveillance. Just about anyone in the country who still respected the king as the authority lost it once news of his capture spread. They, too, fled if they could.

Let’s take a brief aside to consider the thoroughly European context of the French Revolution by 1791. Although the American Revolution is considered the most modern sociopolitical revolution, its leaders did not aim to overturn British society; the American Revolutionary War was a conflict over the colonies. It was elites against elites, haves versus haves. A war was simply fought over who got more. (Also coming to mind are the 1916 Easter Rising of the Irish against their British colonizers, the ensuing civil war led by Michael Collins, Irish independence in 1921, and the founding of the republic of Éire. This, too, was a revolution, but as in North America, the goal was not to invade or overrun Britain with communism but rather boot the Brits out. Not communism.)

War with Britain’s colonies across the pond was not the first revolution that eighteenth-century Europe history had witnessed. America was thus not seen as a template to necessarily follow, as some might assume. Just two years prior to the French Revolution, the Dutch had undergone their own revolution, which gave way to a new republic. In the previous century, the English had waged a bitter civil war that greatly increased the power of parliament while weakening the monarchy forever after. Intellectuals privy to the goings-on in France anticipated an unfolding of events similar perhaps to the Dutch but more likely to the English Civil War, with a monarchy restored alongside a quasi-republican government as a parliamentary companion to check and balance.

But that did not happen. Rumors spread throughout the French countryside that old King Louis had dispatched the army to attack and subdue the peasantry—and that the nobility had his back. The have-nots preempted; all over France, mobs broke into the nobles’ manors and murdered whole families where they slept. Terror.

The revolutionaries also sought to erase the past and restart the nation anew. They destroyed records related to feudal obligations, such as tax records, labor conscription, and anything else that had caused the peasantry hardship. And still, in the midst of all the murder and mayhem, first in the cities and now in the country, there was no response from the monarchy, the military, or the nobility. Modern leftists like to say, “Silence is violence.” This is true but not in the way moderns intend it. In the French Revolution and beyond, the silence of those who should be responsible for preventing and ending violence only begets more such violence. Total inaction by the French government allowed the revolutionaries total freedom of action; thus, the most radical, embittered, and cynical of the bunch set the tone. French revolutionary leaders ran a purity test of sorts on all in their midst to see how committed to the cause they were. This culminated in the extremist Jacobins putting everyone not revolutionary enough to the guillotine. It’s Bolsheviks and Mensheviks all over again for the first time. (More on the Jacobins shortly.)

Just as the revolutionary movement was hostile to the nobility, they also targeted the culturally and politically dominant Catholic Church. Monasteries were shuttered and their residents turned out based on the assumption that these servants of their faith sat on great wealth but performed no valuable work. You have more money than I do; obviously you should give it to me, thought the rebelling peasants. Entire classes were told they were justified to think this way, then and now. Oppressors and oppressed, oppressors and oppressed, all the way down until the bitter end. This is what they do.

Churches were forced to close. Priests and nuns were arrested, sometimes executed on site. Any and all surviving clergy were required to recite loyalty oaths to the new French republic declared by the radical-run National Assembly. Christianity had been officially replaced with an Enlightenment cult of science.

In 1793, amid the fervor of anti-religious sentiment and the fevered push for secularism, the world-renowned Cathedral of Notre Dame was desecrated and sacked. Its religious artifacts, statues, and stained glass windows were smashed. The cathedral was converted into the “Temple of Reason,” symbolizing the rejection of the Catholic Church’s spiritual and philosophical authority. Christian religious symbols were destroyed or defaced, and the cathedral’s interior was rearranged to accommodate new revolutionary ceremonies. This period of desecration marked a profound shift in the cultural and religious landscape of France, reflecting the revolutionary ideals of liberty, equality, and secularism.

The Jacobins, the most radical of revolutionaries, met in one of the abbeys confiscated during the initial Catholic purge. They agreed to turn every aspect of French society upside-down, seeking a “Year Zero” in which all prior historical knowledge and ideas were to be discarded and disavowed in favor of the revolutionary ideology. Out of all the factions active during the French Revolution, they pushed the hardest for rapid change, winning over many other factions to their way of thinking and doing, i.e., robbing and killing at will.

This Year Zero business is not an exaggeration—the revolutionaries devised a new calendar based on a ten-day week with three weeks to a month, ten months total, with a few extra days tacked on at the end. The new month’s names would be made up, sharing no prior links to any previous Western history.6 This was to be a new era, free of old superstitions and customs.

To force all of France to accept this extent of radical change, the Jacobins had to apply pressure—and they did, to Louis XVI.

Under Pressure, Under the Guillotine

The French Revolution was not born out of a desire to remove the monarchy by force, even if that is what indeed happened. Many Frenchmen and women would initially voice loyalty to the monarch, but they did so in an odd way. At the beginning of uprising, loyalists of all classes pilgrimaged to the royal palace of Versailles, stood vigil outside, and proclaimed their love for the king. He failed to reward these early supporters. Soon he would have none of consequence. His timidity gave the revolutionary demands legitimacy. This is why we do not speak of a “French Civil War.” The opposition did not fight back. And so the escalation continued.

Desecration didn’t end in the cathedrals with statues destroyed and windows shattered; with revolutionaries, it never does. Mobs also paid a visit to the tombs of the old French kings outside Paris, tore everything down and out, and scattered bones and corpses everywhere. Unhumans have to cut their mark into society. All symbols of the past must go, no matter how precious or revered.

How could any of this wanton destruction be classed as “reason”? It was an arrogant belief among the revolutionaries that they were the only party who had embraced the Enlightenment; anyone caught voicing anything less than revolutionary zeal was condemned as an enemy of reason. Their logic was as follows: Tradition is not reasonable. It makes no sense for the people of France to do things simply because they’ve done them that way for hundreds of years, from paying taxes to the king, to praying to the same God he did in houses of worship his family had funded generations prior. To clear out the accumulated social and intellectual mold, you kill everything. You destroy it all. You throw out the old religion, the old norms, the old calendar. The Jacobins accepted an early iteration of Blank Slate Theory, typical of communists in the twentieth century and progressives of our own day. According to this Theory, humanity is deemed perfectable; it is possible to shape a man, woman, or they/them like clay into the image of your desiring. So, too, is it believed possible to rework society as a whole from first principles untainted by the decrepit customs of the past that allowed this present crisis. If the tradition you followed brought you to this, of what use was the tradition?

Change from scratch must be total and complete. Everything has to go. In France, even mainstays like the right to an appeal were ripped out of the legal system. The revolutionaries swept aside the old organizational system of the country, replacing the medieval system of provinces with “departments,” which France has to this day.

Our point is that what was considered radical and extreme early in the revolution became mainstream and institutionalized in a few short years. The original plan of the Third Estate was to retain Louis XVI as constitutional monarch, similar to the English way, but that would not satiate the majority Jacobins. So they not only deposed him, they put him on trial as “Louis Capet,” a mere citizen of France, stripped of exalted status, together with his wife Marie Antoinette. The revolutionaries voted to lop their heads off and did so with pleasure and great fanfare. The monarchy was then declared a criminal institution. To have aided and abetted it previously was treason to the new republic, to be traitors against France itself.

With a solemn procession through Paris, the king ascended the scaffold, as thousands gathered to watch the spectacle. They cheered. With the sickening thud of the blade, the reign of the Bourbon monarchy was severed like a thread, recoiling into the annals of history.

Henry Edgeworth, King Louis’s priest, wrote that his last words were as follows:

I die innocent of all the crimes laid to my charge; I pardon those who have occasioned my death; and I pray to God that the blood you are going to shed may never be visited on France.7

Following her husband’s grisly fate, Marie Antoinette faced her own moment of terror. Clad in a simple gown, her once-breathtaking beauty diminished by her time spent locked up, she ascended the scaffold with grace, by all accounts. She refused to struggle or cry out, even as the blade fell. Her only crime was to have married a king.

Mobs broke into the prisons where various aristocrats and royal sympathizers were held. They either slaughtered them outright in their cells or dragged them out to the nearest guillotine. This new legal system reflected an outright presumption of guilt. France had fallen from ponderous debates about the fate of the king, to ten-minute show trials where the only evidence needed to condemn you to death was that someone had accused you of once saying something good about Louis XVI. Perhaps the accuser fancied your spouse and coveted your stuff. Pure coincidence, we’re sure.

The revolutionaries were at this point executing dozens of citizens per day. The first to be guillotined were the royal family, the nobles, the clergy, and all who had openly supported them. Up next were conservatives and moderates who preferred not to have blood in the streets. Death sentences went on and on until only the true believers remained. All other voices were silenced with violence or the threat of it.

But France is not an island. Word of the bloodshed spread to the neighbors. Other European powers, fearing chaos and carnage might spread to their domains, took action. Prussia, for example, declared war on the new French republic, determined to help rebuild the royal institution as a way of safeguarding their own. But they were not prepared for a gentleman’s war with forces who no longer lived like men. And so mobilization turned into retreat, giving the inhumans even more momentum now.

The Revolution Finally Eats Itself

“The Reign of Terror” is what we now call that period of barely state-sanctioned mass murder. Its ruler was the hideously ugly Maximilien Robespierre, perhaps the most repugnant unhuman to wield power in all of French history. Month after month, he personally ordered hundreds and hundreds more dead.

The Terror came to a head with the execution of sixteen Carmelite nuns of Compiègne. Accused of harboring loyalty to the monarchy and practicing counterrevolutionary activities (i.e., refusing to break their religious oaths), these devout women were hauled into Paris to face the judgment of the revolutionary tribunal. On the day they were sentenced to die by guillotine, the streets surrounding the Place de la Révolution echoed with the clamor of a restless crowd hungry for bloody spectacle.

Led forth in a solemn procession, the Carmelite sisters, imminently to become martyrs, clad in simple robes, met their fate with divine resolve. Their faces, etched with the lines of devotion and suffering, betrayed neither fear nor despair but rather showed a profound serenity born of faith. As they ascended the scaffold one by one, a hush fell over the crowd, punctuated only by the commands of the executioner. With each of the sixteen thuds of the blade, a ripple of shock reverberated through the onlookers, mingling with the haunting hymns of the condemned women, who never stopped singing until the unhumans silenced them to the last. In their final moments, the Carmelite nuns embraced martyrdom with a grace that transcended mortal anguish, their unwavering faith a beacon of hope amid the darkness of revolution.8

The nuns did not die in vain. Jacobins in attendance, realizing they were not as gleeful over the shedding of innocent bled as Robespierre was, knew their turn at the ‘tine might be next. And so Robespierre was soon thereafter framed for corruption and summarily executed himself, just ten days after the martyrdom of the Carmelites. All’s fair in hate and war.

Robespierre’s fate befell several of the most bloodthirsty revolutionaries, many of whom were likewise dragged to the guillotine, only screaming and crying famously—unlike the pious Louis, Marie, and the nuns, who led themselves calmly into death with tremendous dignity.

When studying this the bloodiest period of the French Revolution, we come away with two conclusions: These people were evil, and they got what they deserved in the end. The many Jacobins who’d called for wanton murder and butchery were themselves eventually slaughtered, treated with the same contempt and cruelty they’d shown others.

The final death count reached upward into the many tens of thousands; someone had to do something. That someone was Napoleon Bonaparte, and that something was to restore law and order.

The Legacy of the Communist French Revolutions

Despite the excesses of the Revolution, despite the needless murder and destruction, despite the unnecessary attacks on everything that came before, many revolutionary changes were popular. Perhaps we should have said “because of” rather than “despite.” After all, old feudal obligations were done with, flattening out French society so that the haves had become the have-nots (as in, have not their own lives). Positions of authority were conveniently opened up across society. Many shut out of the old regime’s institutions now had a chance to climb to the top. Regardless of all the damage the Revolution did and the bitter suffering it caused, there were changes it enacted that were long overdue. Remember, revolutionaries seek to empower those with a set of grievances, real or invented. In royal France, they were largely real.

One could defend the French Revolution on the idea that it brought about much-needed social change. However, one could also argue that Britain managed to do this without an orgy of death. And of course, the United States created lasting change without a Reign of Terror. And in both England and America, the ideals and application of the Enlightenment—such as liberty and technology, respectively—found a home in both countries without vindictive campaigns of escalating brutality. America has its constitution, which provides an orderly way to make changes to government. Britain retains its monarchy, with a reduced level of authority. So naturally, we come away from the French Revolution realizing all the bloodshed accompanying it was unnecessary. The ends failed to justify the means. Indeed, the French themselves came to this conclusion—that’s why Napoleon soared in popularity. In 1795, Napoleon put down an attempted insurrection against the republic with what was called “a whiff of grapeshot” and is credited as ending the revolution.

A few years later, in 1799, Napoleon launched a bloodless coup against the weak republic itself. He appointed himself First Consul and dissolved that disastrous revolutionary government. Within a few years, he was declared Emperor of France and crowned in the restored Cathedral of Notre Dame. He returned the Catholic Church to public life, albeit without any say in government. No more shooting priests in the head, no more beheading nuns, no more burning churches down. The Church could be a part of French life again. This one move to let the French be Catholic again moderated any remaining revolutionary fervor. Napoleon famously remarked, “I found the crown of France lying in the gutter and picked it up with my sword.” Those French who had fled under Louis XVI felt safe to return. Napoleon formulated a civil code that remains the basis for French law today. The departments stayed; no bringing back the old provinces. Feudalism stayed dead. The story of Napoleon, of course, continued beyond this, but that is a topic covered in many other books.

Years after the Napoleonic era, France once again attempted a monarchy. It did not go well. The novel and musical Les Misérables depict one of the failed French uprisings, that one in 1833. In 1848, unemployed and underemployed have-nots once again revolted—this one called the June Days Uprising—shortly after the arrival after the French-language version of the Communist Manifesto. Thousands died. Taxation was reformed. Civil rights were granted. Executive power was limited. Eventual peace didn’t last. Napoleon’s nephew declared himself emperor three years later.

Communist Manifesto coauthor Frederick Engels would lament of the 1848 French insurrection that “neither the economic progress of the country nor the intellectual development of the mass of French workers had as yet reached the stage which would have made a social reconstruction possible.”9 Real communism had not been tried. It was about to be in, you guessed it, France.

In the immediate aftermath of defeat in war with Prussia in 1871, the hardened working-class defenders of Paris seized control of the city. Radicals and revolutionaries among their ranks murdered two French generals in command and announced the Paris Commune—a new revolutionary government, with its flag blood-red—the first association of the color with communism. Karl Marx wrote The Civil War in France just as the communists were overrun and defeated by the French army and their leaders executed. This was the first real-world implementation of communism; Marx wrote of it fondly:

Working men’s Paris, with its Commune, will be forever celebrated as the glorious harbinger of a new society. Its martyrs are enshrined in the great heart of the working class. Its exterminators, history has already nailed to that eternal pillory from which all of the prayers of their priest will not avail to redeem them.10

George Kennan–style containment plus immediate bud-nipping ended this particular French revolution before it could take hold. Ideas were prohibited from infecting disgruntled soldiers and laborers elsewhere at that time in France, and the full force of law and immediate restoration of order killed the Paris commune, with no chance of survival or revival.

Elsewhere in the French revolutionary universe, that had not been the case. Amid the collapse of the French monarchy and the National Assembly’s declaration of a new republic, all was not well in the colonial world. You probably know about Haiti, how there was a slave uprising concurrent with the French Revolution, and how they ultimately won. Or is that what actually happened?

Let’s turn back a few pages into French revolutionary history and see.

Dishonorable Mention: Communist Revolution in Haiti

As an aside, we would be remiss if we did not mention a certain island nation not far at all from America’s shores that owes its identity to what happened in Paris in the 1790s. You might call Haiti an offshoot of the French Revolution shared cinematic universe. By the time of the revolution, Haiti had been one of France’s chief holdings in the New World, and as like other Caribbean nations, it had been stocked with slaves from West Africa.

In 1790, the new revolutionary French republic committed to righting all perceived historical wrongs in society. Liberation to the oppressed! Death to the oppressors! We’ll hear these refrains over and over, so get used to them. We mentioned a select few already, such as the ruling rights of aristocrats versus the everyday man and woman. Another issue the revolutionary republic decided to tackle was the representation of free blacks in the National Assembly, specifically those on the Caribbean island Hispaniola in the colony of Saint-Domingue (now known as Haiti). Half the island was ruled by France, the other half by Spain. The main advocate for his people was Vincent Ogé, a mulatto Creole and well-to-do slaveholder. His class was known as affranchis (freed people of color), and Ogé fought for wealthy affranchis citizenship in the new France—and won it. But upon his return to Saint-Domingue and announcement of the news, things did not go well for him. Colonial leadership on the island refused to acknowledge the National Assembly’s new biracial citizenship law, Ogé took up arms against the colonists, and he was captured by Spanish colonists from the other side of the island, ruled by Spain (now known as the Dominican Republic). He was summarily tortured to death before an audience of his enemies. Things really did not go well after that. This was 1791.

The spirit of revolution passed from Ogé to all affranchis. Within two months of the execution, there was open warfare between the affranchis and French authorities. Thousands of slaves soon joined them. Anarchy overtook the western (French) half of the island. Enraged mobs of sword-wielding, gun-toting blacks, both slave and free, swarmed colonial plantations and properties. One of the few eyewitness accounts from the uprising is that of an anonymous director of the Clément plantation. His barking dog awoke him suddenly on the night of August 22, 1791. This individual, a white European, ducked under his bed as gunfire blasted through windows and walls. He heard voices calling out.

“Who is it?” he asked.

“It is death!” answered the mob. “Kill, kill,” they added. They then killed everyone else in the building before looting the place. The director was eventually found but spared by a wild leader of the revolt, ex-slave Dutty Boukman, who spared the plantation director’s life because he had once treated him kindly, like an equal.

But the director’s living nightmare would continue, as he joined the rebels on a forced march to commit further atrocities. The anonymous man sensed regret and remorse set in his captors that very night. But Boukman ordered them onward with a threat: Keep going, or *I* will shoot *you.*11

Unhumans. This is what they do.

Large and warrior-like, former slave Dutty Boukman, who had been born in West Africa, kicked off that bloody slave revolt with a ceremony of animal sacrifice that blended Islamic teaching and voodoo practices. Haiti would become synonymous with voodoo occult rituals, and Boukman was a top practitioner who led the rites. To the assembled crowd he passed around a cup of animal blood which everyone drank, and he called out, “The God of the whites pushes them to crime, but he wants us to do good deeds. But the God who is so good orders us to vengeance. He will direct our hands, and give us help. Throw away the image of the God of the whites who thirsts for our tears. Listen to the liberty that speaks in all our hearts.”12

Then the blood massacres began—including the one that the anonymous eyewitness related to us. Most weren’t so fortunate. Men, women, and so many screaming children, it didn’t matter. If you were French, you were hacked to death.

The authorities would eventually catch up to the murderers marauding the plantations and put them to justice. Boukman was captured and beheaded by the French, his decapitated head placed on public display. And that plantation manager fled for France to tell his tale.13 But Boukman would become a symbol of resistance and vengeance to the Haitian slaves; he remains a galvanizing symbol of national pride in Haiti today.

This was not the end of revolution in Haiti; it was the end of the beginning. In 1792, a new French law granting citizenship to all affranchis regardless of wealth or social status failed to return any semblance of peace. Other radicals would raise up armies of the aggrieved as well, men known to history such as Toussaint Louverture and Jean-Jacques Dessalines. Both were former slaves who’d joined the rebellion following the death of Vincent Ogé. Agents of the new French republic were sent to stabilize society in 1793, with all slavery being outlawed in 1794. Louverture and Dessalines had been made the lowest of their society, the have-the-least of all the have-nots. And yet neither brought law, order, or justice. Communist leaders throughout promise their followers the world, then take it from them. This is what both men did, in succession. The French initially supported Louverture, who’d raised up a vengeance-seeking army. He declared himself “governor-general for life” amid a military dictatorship over the entire colony. Former slaves were forced back to work on former slave plantations. This is what they do.

In 1801, Napoleon Bonaparte dispatched French forces that included both white and affranchis officers and soldiers to relieve Louverture of his duties. He died imprisoned in France in 1803; Louverture met the fate he’d forced upon his own free countrymen.14

Then it got worse. Jean-Jacques Dessalines, a former lieutenant of Louverture, re-revolted against the French. Multiple factors including a yellow fever epidemic, British naval involvement, and the sale of Louisiana to the United States gave Dessalines and his forces a broken window of opportunity. Dessalines believed his comrade Louverture had not gone far enough.

Following victory over the weakened French, Dessalines re-reinstituted the plantation system, forcing fellow blacks to once again return to forced work, and in 1804 crowned himself the first emperor of a new independent nation: Haiti. Besides the brutish treatment toward his own people, his regime outlawed ownership of any and all property by white people. He then extended his racist campaign and ordered the systematic murder of all whites in Haiti. Thousands of white men, women, and children were massacred all over Haiti. This white genocide made the situation for Haitian blacks no better. His own people revolted against him, and Dessalines was killed in an attempt to suppress a rebellion against him.15

And that has been the history of Haiti ever since: robbing and killing. A country founded on unhuman revolts ends in chaos and cannibal warlords. The former French colony of Hispaniola was founded on the oppressor-oppressed story brought to life in a bloody slave revolt and gave its people the worst standard of living in the entire Western hemisphere: Haiti.16 The Spanish side maintained its integrity, repelled multiple invasions from Haiti over the next century, and was named at the time of this writing the happiest people on earth: the Dominican Republic.17 Unhumanity versus humanity. The results speak for themselves.

Note that the black Haitian dictators still reinstituted chattel slavery after France had ended it, even over their fellow blacks, once all the whites had been massacred. They brought totalitarian rule and speed-ran massacres in all territory under their control. It’s almost like that was their goal in the first place. As everyone knows, Haiti never became a utopia. And yet, it shares the same island as an actual tropical utopian paradise in the DR. The difference is everything. The difference is unhumans. Boukman’s vengeance is still being felt today in Haiti.

A communist revolution is, with irony, promised to be of the people, by the people, for the people. And it never is. The objective is never equality; it’s power. The unhumans know how to seize it. They find the have-nots with grievances, legitimate or imagined, and emotionally abuse them. They rile them up. And then, they unhuman them. The radicals turn men into beasts, people into predators, with no will but to kill. Is it death! Kill, kill. Then when it’s done, those who did not die in the uprising suffer a regret that is a living prison their conscience can never escape. All while the leader of the rebellion wins absolute power, power that in most cases exceeds that of the original accused oppressor individual or class. And they leave the place worse off than they found it. This is what they do.

We have to be very clear about this. France was already ending slavery and had begun the process of granting citizenship to the freed slaves *before* the massacres began. It’s almost as if it was never about equality at all. It never is.

Now, this abridged history of the French Terror, full revolutionary period, and its legacy probably felt unsettlingly familiar. Let’s talk about that.

The Revolution Is Now

The parallels between late eighteenth-century France and our time are almost so obvious we shan’t name them. We shall.

Let’s go back to the beginning, to the preparation of the environment for revolution. For one, the United States is dealing with our own rising foreign rival—Britain then, China now. The United States is running up a laughably unsustainable national debt. Just as France and its language were the intellectual trendsetters of Europe, America and the English language decide global culture today. Just as eighteenth-century France hadn’t convened an Estates-General in 150-plus years, the US hasn’t convened a Constitutional Convention since the original constitution was ratified, more than two centuries ago. If a Constitutional Convention were convened today, we expect a collapse into chaos like that of the Estates-General and National Assembly. A total rewrite of the American political and legal system would likely follow.

As economic and social crises come to a head in modern-day USA, radical ideologies take center stage. The political unrest of 2020 was a sort of micro-French and/or Haitian Revolution. While the mobs didn’t succeed in overturning the federal system (though it did unseat President Donald J. Trump in a highly dubious election result) or killing all white people (though many black mobs went hunting for them), we did witness an eerily similar desecration of revered symbols of the past, the storming of police stations, the tearing down of statues, and the repudiating of classic American values as backward and bigoted. “White supremacy” had to be replaced with “anti-racism,” echoing the Cult of Reason’s attacks on the Catholic Church in France.

As an aside, one of the founders of Black Lives Matter, Patriss Cullors, is herself a voodoo practitioner, just like Dutty Boukman before her. Cullors has often spoken of being an ordained practitioner of Ifá, a practice originating in West Africa. One of BLM’s most well-known tactics is the chanting of a name of a deceased member of the community in a group march, repeating the name followed by, “Say his/her name.” This is directly tied to the Ifá practice of spirit summoning and often includes the pouring of libations.18

Much as the old French and then Haitian revolutionaries operated off of fervor, force, and fear, today’s American leftists do not operate on logic but passion. The common moderate refrain that “facts don’t care about your feelings” must be reversed—feelings don’t care about your facts. Leftists operate from envy. There is no way to reason with those who manipulate the have-nots en masse to loot and to shoot. They simply hate those who are good-looking and successful. The egalitarian ideology is just window dressing.

It’s not about equality; it’s about revenge.

The French revolutionaries’ fiery hatred of their forebears sees its mirror today in modern leftist ideology. You destroy something because it feels good to destroy something beautiful and orderly. You couldn’t make it in society, so you tear that society down without regard for what replaces it. The police are racist, so just get rid of them (“Defund the police.”) Meritocracy is bad, so just get rid of it with so-called “Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion” (DEI, or sometimes DIE). Racial equality is not enough, not for America’s radical left or for the Haitian hate mobs. In the present-day US, a new intersectional hierarchy was formed, with normal people who support racial equality and color-blindness under the law getting denounced as racists— without irony and not as a joke.

In this and other ways, present-day America is a dark inversion of the first years of the French Revolution. The French Revolution promised equality under the law and meritocracy. But modern leftism specifically discards both. They want to take the lowest rung of society and put them on the top. Some have termed this “Bio-Leninism.” We do not protest the label.

In China, the CCP literally did this. During Chairman Mao’s era, the Communist Party seized factories and factories, murdered the managers, and set the untrained day laborers in charge. The result was a mass famine that killed tens of millions. More on that later.

Fortunately, America has not reached anywhere near that level of dysfunction, but China’s example offers a sobering preview of where we might be headed should the politics of resentment be left unchecked. From the looks of it, all we have are our duplicate copies of the poor man’s Louis XIV running Washington, DC, and the capital cities of most states.

We’ll let you sit with the ramifications of the French revolutionary template as we leave you with this: The Main Currents of Marxism by Polish historian Leszek Kolakowski suggests that the Marxist worldview claims to advocate for the equality of all men but is no more than bitterness that they, the Marxists, are inferior to others. The French Revolution fits this frame, as does modern American leftism: ugly, violent people dress up their bloodlust and jealousy as some heroic fight for equality, then go on to tear everything down. Whether we’re talking about the Jacobins, the Marxists, or the race mobs burning down our cities, it’s always the same ideas, and they will always have the same results.

They don’t want a workers’ paradise; they want to burn down cities.

This is what they do.

French Revolution Death Toll: 100,000

Haitian Revolution Death Toll: 345,000

cNT

CHAPTER 6

BLOOD ON THE SNOW: THE UNTOLD STORY OF THE RUSSIAN REVOLUTION

Your worst sin is that you have destroyed and betrayed yourself for nothing.

—Fyodor Dostoevsky, Crime and Punishment

Unhumans are not particularly intelligent. Great promises are made to the people they win over during the initial sweep into power. By fear, with surprise, and through force, unhumans have in various times and places subdued opposition or driven it far and away. But the regime that grants the unhumans absolute power over their adversaries does not give way to utopia, or anything like it. And so those promises go unfulfilled. Utopia looks more like anarchy. The unhumans may be brutal, but many are typically bumblers as well. They possess an instinct of cruelty but are not known for managing the consequences of it.

This was evident in the individual called Yakov Yurovsky, who commanded the red guard the night the Romanov family was murdered in cold blood. It was he who personally ordered the Tsar and his wife and children into the cellar where they and their servants were reduced to bloody corpses. Unhumans seek vengeance; they are not always particularly effective at it.

Yurovsky was from a Jewish family, and there had been violent pogroms toward Jews during late imperial Russia, so much so that Yurovsky had previously fled to Germany where Jews were safe (at the time). Upon return to Russia, he immediately joined the Bolsheviks. Where he likely had motivation, he lacked competence.

The executioner Yurovsky’s official account of the execution of the Russian imperial family and the immediate aftermath reads like a bloody slasher flick script meets dark comedy. Yurovsky managed to smuggle firearms into the building where the Tsar and his family were staying at that time, following abdication of the throne—a place called Ipatiev House hidden deep in central Russia.

On the night of July 16, 1918, Yurovsky’s thugs ordered the family awake and to get dressed; they would be going somewhere. The women put on their gowns. Suspicion unaroused, the Christian Romanovs and their servants followed their guards to the cellar. Out came the guns. Yurovsky later wrote in his official report of the matter that Tsar Nicholas’s last word was, What?

Yurovsky gave the word. When the firing squad began shooting, Tsarina Alexandra reportedly murmured a prayer, the sign of the cross. She never finished it. Errant shots hit the brick wall, ricocheted everywhere, and reportedly grazed his own men. The Romanov parents died instantly; their children, not so.

The Romanov daughters, Olga, Tatiana, Maria, and Anastasia, wore thick dresses made of diamonds and also had jewelry hidden on their persons, the thugs would later find. In the smoke, the guards heard only shrieks but could not see anything. The screaming did not cease. They realized the children had survived the initial volley of shots. So, out came the bayonets. These were no match for their ladies’ gowns, and the diamonds and jewels prevented the murderers from stabbing them to death.

Finally, once Yurovsky could make out the situation just a few feet away, direct headshots were ordered at close range, even of young Prince Alexi, the only son of Nicholas and Alexandra. The girls and their brother were summarily executed, one by one. The Russian Imperial family was no more. No direct heirs survived. The new order had begun with blood on the snow.1

The Road to St. Petersburg

The 1917 Russian Revolution was the first true communist revolution. The unhumanity of the Romanov family’s fate in 1918 was a link in the iron chain of revolutionary history, with many links spanning decades both before and after. We’ve previously covered the backdrop of pertinent imperial history up to the outbreak of uprising. To review:

Imperial Russia, like pre-revolutionary France, had been an absolute monarchy for centuries, which created an extreme divide and disparity between the haves and the have-nots.

Until 1861, Russian peasantry were slaves by another name. The abolition of serfdom didn’t improve their lot (much).

The secluded empire of Japan humiliated a laggardly industrializing Russia, in the 1905 Russo-Japanese war.

The Communist Manifesto had spread from Germany to Russia in the character of Vladimir Lenin and his comrades the Bolsheviks, the most radical of all the various malcontents in Russian sociopolitics ready for change and willing to force it.

What we didn’t mention in previous sections on Russia that are now pertinent include the assassinations of Tsar Alexander II—grandfather of Nicholas II—and Pyotr Stolypin, one of the last pre-revolutionary prime ministers of Russia, in 1881 and 1911, respectively. The assassinations were at the hands of anarcho-socialists. It was Alexander II who had ended the practice of serfdom, ironically, and Stolypin who reformed the debt slavery system so peasants could finally own their own homes and lands. Defeat by Japan upturned Russian society in what we now remember as the 1905 Revolution. Soldiers mutinied, workers striked, and peasants revolted. Like Louis XVI before him, Nicholas II attempted to avert a toppling of the old order. He agreed to a new Russian constitution and allowed for a parliament—and appointed the great land reformer Stolypin. He did not deserve his fate.

Imperial Russia at least had a future; the have-nots, after centuries of autocratic rule, finally had a ladder to climb upward toward prosperity. But World War I tore it all down. The lesson of Japan went unlearned.

The Great War exposed the true inadequacy of the Russian state, as the industrialized Germans crushed them on the battlefield. The Russians took millions of losses, adding suffering in their western border to match the previous embarrassment in their far east. This sorry state of affairs emboldened already-radical actors to rise seemingly out of nowhere and gain overnight credibility.
