To oppose the status quo was to be the voice of reason (there’s that little word again).

In early 1917, Nicholas II learned there were riots in St. Petersburg. They began on March 8, which the Bolsheviks later declared as International Women’s Day. Not just the workers in the streets, but the soldiers joining as well. It was 1905 all over again. The Tsar’s advisors (including the nefarious Rasputin) told him this time was different— the government was, in fact, to be overthrown. Nicholas II pleaded with his cousin George V of Great Britain for a way of escape from Russia to England—No, that’s your problem was the gist of his answer. Abdication was the only out. And so in the span of a week, the Russian monarchy, an institution that has lasted centuries, was no more.

A provisional government formed. Its officials promised everything early-stage revolutions promise, such as greater representation, democratic elections, and a new new constitution. And of course, the previous was to be arrested—that meant the Tsar, his entire family, and close allies.

As in France a century and a quarter prior, mere capture of the king was not enough for the most radical. In Russia, as you learned, these were the workers’ councils, known as soviets. There were soviets in factories, those made up of active-duty soldiers and veterans, soviets of rural workers and day laborers, and so on. Anywhere there were people of like mind and class to be organized, there was a soviet.

The traveling Marxist ideologue Vladimir Lenin was released from his imprisonment by the Germans while they and the Tsarist regime had been at war. He was sent to Russia to stir up trouble and knock the empire out of the war. The goal was regime change in Russia. What could go wrong? Lenin stepped off the train in St. Petersburg in 1917 and made the people a promise they literally ate up: Peace, land, bread.

Vladimir Lenin was born into a middle-class family in Simbirsk, Russia, in 1870, with his father being an educator and his mother a noblewoman. Like Yakov Yurovsky whose story began this chapter, Lenin had a bone to pick with traditionalist Russia. Several, actually. Two hundred and six to be exact. Lenin’s older brother Aleksandr, a turn-of-the-century radical, had been executed in 1887. He’d plotted and failed to assassinate Alexander III, son of the murdered Alexander II. Lenin himself was exiled to Siberia in 1897, punishment for carrying on his brother’s activist legacy.

All Lenin had to do was offer what Russians not only wanted but needed—permanent end to foreign wars, permanent land reforms superior even to Stolypin’s, and permanent food security for all. Of course, Bolshevik rule would mean total warfare against the Russian people for decades to come, full land collectivization with no private ownership, and strategic famines to punish the non-loyal with slow and painful and heartbreaking mass death. But that wasn’t part of the sales pitch. Lenin offered relief from the appalling conditions of the status quo, and the people were primed thanks to effective OPE. And so the Bolsheviks lit the match.

Violence began in the countryside and moved into the cities quickly. As in the French Revolution, communist-led mobs of Russian peasants seized land and killed landowners—and they were allowed to keep what they took.

In October 1917, the Bolsheviks stormed the Winter Palace of St. Petersburg, where the provisional government was seated. Just like in revolutionary France and Paris, anything that happened in St. Petersburg affected all of Russia. Supported by local soviets, the Bolsheviks kept control, legitimizing their claim to power. At first, they faced no serious opposition, more eloquent speeches and triumphant reinforced the perfect persuasion of Peace, Land, Bread.

One of those who might have brought the revolution to an abrupt end in Napoleon- or Caesar-like fashion was General Aleksei Brusilov, one of the most successful Russian officers during World War I, winning victory after victory over Austro-Hungarian imperial forces. He was a devout Orthodox Christian, and he was deeply loyal to his nation, but instead of taking action, he instead took the lack of actions. He seemed to reason that, as a Russian patriot, whoever the majority of the Russian people decided to lead was the legitimate authority. Brusilov thought the reds seemed too angry to rule, too radical and hoped this period would pass, with moderates emerging to bring law and order. He thought that by sitting back and doing nothing, the moderates would simply overtake the unhuman bolsheviks by magic, or something. This was the same mistake of Nicholas II and Louis XVI before him—to assume a return to sanity. It is what many of Brusilov’s contemporaries thought as well.

“Social life followed its usual course, and it seemed as if these people were merely to be the spectators and not the victims of the drama to come,” wrote Pyotr Wrangel, describing Russia’s upper-class mindset even as the Bolshevik revolution was already underway.2 The revolution comes for those who can’t believe it. It always does. In December 1917, the wealth of the aristocracy, the bourgeoisie, and foreign investors was seized. In fact, all private bank accounts were nationalized and their assets were declared the property of the people, essentially debanking the upper classes and those associated with the former Tsarist regime. It could have gone no other way.

Also amid the turmoil, there was a significant breakdown in the Russian penal system, with many prisoners being released either officially by decree or through the chaos of the revolution. The Bolsheviks issued several decrees that led to the release of political prisoners. However, the chaotic situation also meant that many common criminals were released or escaped. The immediate post-revolution period saw a rise in crime, partly attributed to these releases. Again, no other way.

Because once the Incitement of Act I occurs, there is no going back from what lies ahead in Acts II and III. This is what Brusilov and many other would-be great men of history failed to comprehend: The unhumans will not stop unless they are stopped. Brusilov wrongfully supported the Bolsheviks for a pragmatic reason and died in isolated disrepute.

But where the Russian Revolution begins to break course with the French template is organized opposition. It soon emerged. It wasn’t Brusilov, but it was bold. Enough to transition revolution into civil war.

The Russian Civil War

Spoiler Alert: The Bolsheviks won. The imprisoned Romanovs were executed. Communist rule was established throughout the former Russian empire and lasted until December 26, 1991. This fate did not come without a fight. Stood opposed to Vladimir Lenin was Pyotr Wrangel, a junior general in the so-called White Army—the assumed name of all anti-Bolshevik forces prior to and following the abdication of the Tsar.

Pyotr Wrangel, feared by the Bolsheviks as the Black Baron, was born into a noble Baltic German family in 1878. Graduating from the prestigious Nikolaev Engineering Military Academy, Wrangel displayed exceptional strategic acumen and leadership skills. Rising through the ranks of the Imperial Russian Army, he distinguished himself in various conflicts, including World War I. Following the Bolshevik Revolution in 1917, Wrangel aligned himself with the anti-Bolshevik White movement, becoming a key figure in their resistance against the Red Army, and known for his staunch opposition to Bolshevik ideology and loyalty to the Russian Empire.

The Black Baron was widely described for his distinguished appearance, often described as imposing at a height of around 6-foot-3, with a strong, chiseled jawline and piercing eyes that exuded authority. He typically dressed in the traditional uniform of a Russian military officer, adorned with medals and insignia, projecting an aura of dignity and command wherever he went. His forces respected him for demanding discipline and ushering swift justice when the need required.

Prior to Wrangel, opposition to the reds was disorganized and non-collaborative. And so Lenin pitted varying factions against each other while making agreements with different nations within the fast-dissolving empire to ensure his victory. But Wrangel wrangled together support for a traditional Russia, and they held out control over southern Russia as well as Crimea. Unfortunately, what unified the White Army became their greatest liability—their figurehead, Tsar Nicholas II, the true leader of Russia. While the Romanovs lived, the Whites planned their campaigns around attempts at rescue. First, St. Petersburg, the Bolshevik stronghold where they were held. The White Army failed, so the royal family was moved to the Ural Mountains, far away from nationalist activity. But when a dispatch of Whites finally came close, the Bolsheviks decided the Romanovs were more trouble than they were worth. Enter Yakov Yurovsky.

Dekulakization

The Bolsheviks, under the leadership of Lenin and later Joseph Stalin, implemented harsh policies toward the kulaks, who were prosperous peasants or farmers in Russia. The term “kulak” was broadly used to describe those who owned more land or livestock than the average peasant. Basically, a kulak is like what you might call a small business owner or landlord in America today. And we know how the Left treats those. They’ve always been like this. In the early years following the Revolution of 1917, Lenin’s government introduced measures such as land redistribution, which targeted the kulaks as part of their broader goal to dismantle the old feudal system and establish socialism. However, it was under Stalin’s leadership in the 1930s that the repression against the kulaks intensified.

Stalin’s policies aimed at collectivizing agriculture and eliminating private farming further marginalized the kulaks. The government portrayed them as class enemies and obstacles to the collectivization process. In response, the regime initiated a brutal campaign of dekulakization, which involved mass arrests, deportations, and executions of kulaks and their families. Millions were forcibly relocated to remote areas or sent to labor camps, where many perished due to harsh conditions. This brutal campaign not only devastated the kulaks economically but also led to widespread suffering and loss of life among rural communities, profoundly altering the agrarian landscape of the Soviet Union.

The cold-blooded murder of innocent men, women, and children is a major plot point in the story of the Russian Revolution. To target military-age males was not enough. Relative to other revolutions, the Bolsheviks were efficient about robbing and killing anyone deemed a threat to Lenin, to Joseph Stalin, and beyond. Continuous murder and mayhem were the twin defining features of the Soviet regime for its first thirty years. The main organization given the task of carrying out these brutal operations was the People’s Commissariat for Internal Affairs, known better by its Russian acronym NKVD—the predecessor to the more well-known KGB. Through this and other organs, the newly formed Union of Soviet Socialist Republics, shortened to the USSR or Soviet Union, exercised its power.

Another memorable example of Soviet ruthlessness occurred during the run-up to World War II. Nazi Germany had split Poland with the Soviet Union. The NKVD rolled in, arrested thousands of Polish army officers, and deported priests and intellectuals en masse. Many were sent to labor camps in Siberia, just as the young Lenin had. Twenty thousand of these Polish prisoners were taken to Moscow. After six months, the Soviets decided, much as they had with the Romanovs, they were too dangerous to be left alive. They shot them all. All of them. They never spoke of it again, and when they did, they blamed it on the Nazis. This was the Katyn Massacre.

The Soviet way was stupid when it was not vicious. Early in Bolshevik rule, they tried abolishing money. When that didn’t work, they implemented the New Economic Policy, which allowed for limited private enterprise. Though intended as a temporary measure, this limited form of free enterprise allowed food and goods to appear again in usable quantities. A smart, select few got rich doing this—the so-called “NEPmen.” When passed the baton from Lenin to Stalin, the great rollback began. Everything was to return to state control. These NEPmen, these successful peasants who made something of themselves, had become haves in a nation still comprised of mostly have-nots. Those who had prospered under the New Economic Policy, in particular the kulaks, were to be robbed and killed. And yet these were not even aristocrats; the old nobility were either dead or fled. These were small businessmen, hardworking Russians who’d cobbled together a new life in dark times. They lived nowhere near the nerve centers of power, tied to the central regime. Nevertheless, they were targeted for destruction. This is what they do.

Let’s pause on this for a moment—it was a crime to make something of yourself in the Soviet Union. Whatever you worked hard for had to be taken away, “collectivized.” NEPmen and kulaks had no desire to unseat the communists since they fared relatively better under them. And yet they were rounded up and shot.

Farther west, in modern-day Ukraine, “Europe’s breadbasket,” the best farmers were punished the worst. From Revelations of the Russian Archives, the story of the Holodomor:

The policy of all-out collectivization instituted by Stalin in 1929 to finance industrialization had a disastrous effect on agricultural productivity. Nevertheless, in 1932 Stalin raised Ukraine’s grain procurement quotas by forty-four percent. This meant that there would not be enough grain to feed the peasants, since Soviet law required that no grain from a collective farm could be given to the members of the farm until the government’s quota was met. Stalin’s decision and the methods used to implement it condemned millions of peasants to death by starvation. Party officials, with the aid of regular troops and secret police units, waged a merciless war of attrition against peasants who refused to give up their grain. Even indispensable seed grain was forcibly confiscated from peasant households. Any man, woman, or child caught taking even a handful of grain from a collective farm could be, and often was, executed or deported. Those who did not appear to be starving were often suspected of hoarding grain. Peasants were prevented from leaving their villages by the NKVD and a system of internal passports.

The death toll from the 1932–33 famine in Ukraine has been estimated between six million and seven million. According to a Soviet author, “Before they died, people often lost their senses and ceased to be human beings.”3

Unhumans. This is what they do.

The lasting terror of the Russian Revolution is how thoroughly it remade Russian society. If you wonder what defines the Russian spirit today, you’ll begin to realize that Russians are far more likely to take a cynical view of morality—even the religious among them are like this. The Soviets did everything possible to mess with their heads, as a people. For example, instead of just taking down the Orthodox Church like they did in the pre–World War II years, later Soviets appointed KGB assets to head the Church. Confession, which is supposed to be between the penitent and the priest, was no longer secret; instead, it’s all handed over to the KGB, straight to your file. It does not need to be explained how awful it is for an institution to appear safe and helpful, to be used against you in the most ruthless way. Communists like the system of everyone informing on everyone else. In post-revolutionary Russia, people weren’t usually shot at random; instead, they designed a vast networked informant system in which anyone denounced as a traitor to the regime would be swiftly executed—and anyone could be an informant … or one informed on. During Stalin’s reign, two million Communist Party members were killed outright, purged for one ideological offense or another because the NKVD had to hit a quota. This practice wasn’t just Stalinist policy—it happened under Lenin, too. It happened as the Revolution ran its course in the beginning. The Bolshevik movement overflowed with unhumans anxious for revenge. They even exported this ideology around the world, via the Communist International or Comintern. It would be as if we took the modern-day so-called “Antifa” radicals and put them in charge of a world power with a powerful military intelligence apparatus, and they got to do whatever they wanted.

That said, this may not be too difficult for us to imagine. In Lenin’s day and in ours, unhumans know how to frame a narrative. Lenin himself said that the modern political struggle wasn’t about theory, but about who will overtake whom, often shortened to the “Who, whom?” question we wrote about earlier. Modern leftists in the West also frame politics in these terms: “Who will overtake whom, the police or the ‘black bodies’ they oppress? Who will overtake whom, greedy capitalist landlords or the poor renters unable to afford a mortgage whom they squeeze?” Who, whom? demands that you identify your allies and enemies. The left knows who they favor and whom they don’t. If you are disfavored, any misfortune that befalls you is obviously your fault. Or it never happened, actually, that’s just conspiracy theory. But it’s good that it happened. Check your privilege. Free speech is hate speech, so you don’t get any.

Let revolutionaries rule over you, and they will decide on an arbitrary basis, who wins, who loses, who lives, who dies, who prospers, who suffers, and who goes to prison or exile. They will not be smart about it, but you can be sure they will be cruel. Ridiculous things happen when unhumans are in charge—more than you may realize.

Ouroboros as Strategy

Ouroboros refers to the ancient symbol of a snake consuming its own tail. Most see this as a warning, unhumans seem to take it as a policy.

At last, we return to Yakov Yurovsky and to the last night of the Romanovs. As we saw, he mismanaged that night about as badly as could be imagined. He also botched what came after.

In his official account, Yurovsky admitted he had not thought through the ramifications of Romanov family martyrdom and the sure effect that would have on the approaching White Army. There was evidence of murder, much of it, and it all had to be gotten rid of.

First, there was the matter of what to be done with the corpses. Stretchers were suggested. No, that would leave trails of blood outside where a truck was waiting to proceed to disposal. So someone found military-grade woolen cloth to line the stretchers and the truck bed. After the first was taken to the truck, Yurovsky caught two men looting the other bodies. They lied, of course. He threatened a second firing squad. They fessed up and turned out their pockets. Yurovsky dismissed them, ordered all bodies be left where they lay, and proceeded to pre-loot each himself. Collecting quite a haul, he carried it all personally to the commander of his guards.

A number of hours passed before all was ready for the truck’s departure. But there wasn’t a destination; nobody had thought through what to do in the way of a funeral or mass grave or cremation or any of it. The driver suggested a nearby iron and steelworks. Yurovsky had never heard of it. They went anyway.

On the way, a caravan of carriages and men on horseback appeared from the forest and stopped the truck. Apparently, they were expecting to intercept the Romanovs alive. Back-and-forth, further delay. The voyage continued until the truck veered into a tight spot between two trees and got stuck. The situation proved too tempting for the accompanying red guards, who re-looted what had been left on the family’s persons, particularly the girls. Yurovsky found the scene, ordered them out of sight, and posted guards while he wandered in the direction of the factory on foot. Perhaps a forest grave, somewhere along the way? No, all of a sudden, he happened upon a campfire of peasant workers. It was daybreak. They’d be seen. Word might spread to the Whites.

Yurovsky returned to the truck and ordered all company away, save for the most trusted few. He decided to have the bodies stripped and the bloodied garments burned. Unhuman. Diamonds, other precious stones, and pure gold previously concealed were revealed. This did not burn and so had to be disposed of. Red guards who visited the site of the bonfire the next day found additional diamonds in the embers Yurovsky had somehow missed.

Yurovsky knew the odor of burning flesh might bring the whole White Army down upon him, so he ordered all remains dumped in a nearby mine. This was in error; water barely covered the bodies, so Yurovsky debated with the guards whether to bomb the mine into rubble to seal their crimes in, or something else. He ultimately posted guards, left the site, and headed straight to the local communist authorities to report the mess. They offered no help, and Yurovsky decided to order his guards to raise the bodies from the mine for reburial elsewhere. They did; the mine recovery took all that next night.

Meanwhile, Yurovsky pursued a lead on another mine, this one more remote and presumed abandoned. The car he was in broke down en route. This pattern of bumbling continued—Yurovsky got a horse, the beast injured his foot, his men just dug a pit rather than make the second mine trip, a local spotted them, they filled in the pit, the truck got stuck again. Finally, it was decided to burn a few corpses, bury the remains, dig a mass grave for the rest, disfigure them with sulfuric acid, and rebury, with railroad ties on top so as to disguise the scene as a road. The Romanovs would not be found until 1979.4

Yurovsky lived to age sixty, but not before the Soviet secret police, the NKVD, arrested his daughter and deported her to the gulag for ten years, guilty for being the wrong kind of communist, a devotee of Lenin contemporary Leon Trotsky rather than Joseph Stalin. Later, Yurovsky’s son, a Soviet naval officer, was also arrested. It was after his daughter’s sentencing that Yurovsky was suddenly hospitalized with a peptic ulcer. Some historians believe he was poisoned—by the NKVD. He died a slow and painful death. He finally became a good communist.

“How you do anything is how you do everything,” the popular saying goes. This is the way of the communist. What grants absolute power over another does not maintain it. Unhumans are arguably the best there are at organizing; they are worst at being organized. This is the reason for the Ouroboros; they must eat their own rank and file because that is all they are able to do with competence. One moment, they shoot their victims; the next, they threaten to shoot each other. As history reveals, they often follow through. Terror needs more of itself to survive; a perpetual state of panic and revolution is required. The terror is the point. The blood is the point. The mass killing is the point. They do not believe what they say; they just want an excuse to burn cities and murder people. Scale the scene of the Romanov murders up, and that is the story of early Soviet Russia: nasty, brutal, and vicious. And occasionally stupid.

The Last Stand of the Imperial Guard

There were several points where someone besides Pyotr Wrangel—or alongside him—could have swept in, fought back, and crushed the Bolsheviks. Bolshevik means “the majority” but of course, they never held elections or anything like that. Wrangel was not able to stop them, despite his valiant last stand. Here’s why, and what we can learn.

First, the incumbent Russian powers-that-were did not perceive Marxist-Leninists as a powerful threat. Even when the reds seized cities, it was not believed they would go the distance. The Soviet Union could have been nipped in the bud; it was not, and instead, its roots grew deep and its branches broad, casting shadows over hundreds of millions the world over for decades.

A second missed opportunity was simply the West dropping the ball. Perhaps they did so on purpose. An infamous example is New York Times journalist Walter Duranty visiting and writing heaping praise upon the Soviet Union. This false reporting won him a Pulitzer. During World War II, the American press often repeated Soviet talking points, accepting without doubt the official USSR justification for invading Poland and Finland. The US news media also blasted Chiang Kai-shek as corrupt, and claimed it only made sense that the communists definitely could and arguably should win (they did).

And finally, perhaps the most-missed opportunity to crush the Russian Revolution was the one missed the most, by the most people. Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn writes in The Gulag Archipelago:

At what point, then, should one resist? When one’s belt is taken away? When one is ordered to face into a corner? When one crosses the threshold of one’s home? An arrest consists of a series of incidental irrelevancies, of a multitude of things that do not matter, and there seems no point in arguing about one of them individually … and yet all these incidental irrelevancies taken together implacably constitute the arrest.5

To take Solzhenitsyn at his words, the Russian everyman had potential to be the great man of history—the one who resisted. Not on my watch. Elsewhere, Solzhenitsyn suggests reciprocity with the reds, using their own weapon of terror against them to squash uprising:

What would things have been like if every Security operative, when he went out at night to make an arrest, had been uncertain whether he would return alive and had to say goodbye to his family? Or if, during periods of mass arrests, as for example in Leningrad, when they arrested a quarter of the entire city, people had not simply sat there in their lairs, paling with terror at every bang of the downstairs door and at every step on the staircase, but had understood they had nothing left to lose and had boldly set up in the downstairs hall an ambush of half a dozen people with axes, hammers, pokers, or whatever else was at hand? … The Organs would very quickly have suffered a shortage of officers and transport and, notwithstanding all of Stalin’s thirst, the cursed machine would have ground to a halt! If … if … We didn’t love freedom enough. And even more—we had no awareness of the real situation … We purely and simply deserved everything that happened afterward.6

The White Army under Pyotr Wrangel was willing to fight back and fight hard. Wrangel was so effective, the Soviets would have to assassinate him to prevent a counterrevolution. He was the likeliest figure to lead such an effort, had there been one. And there was about to be, too, even after the fall of St. Petersburg and the murder of the Romanovs. To shorten a long story, Wrangel had not gotten along well with peer leadership in the White Army, and he left Russia for Constantinople (now Istanbul, Turkey) in 1920. But not six weeks passed before he was called out of retirement/exile and assumed the post of commander in chief of the White Army, located in Crimea, and received executive power as well.

And yet it was too late. The coalition government Wrangel formed and led could not catch up with the Bolshevik swarm. Military engagements would halve the remaining White Army, and he would have no choice but to order an evacuation of all anti-communists from Soviet territory.

On November 14, 1920, the Russian Civil War was effectively over, as the last White Army ship sailed out of unfriendly waters. As the Bolshevik forces tightened their grip on the Crimean Peninsula in 1920, Wrangel, commanding the White Army, faced the grim reality of impending defeat. With unwavering resolve and a sense of duty to his men and the civilian population, Wrangel orchestrated a meticulous evacuation plan. As the evacuation reached its climax, Wrangel made the ultimate sacrifice, ensuring the safety of his comrades and countless refugees before boarding the final ship himself. With the echoes of conflict fading behind him, Wrangel stood alone on the deck, a symbol of resilience and determination, as the vessel sailed into the waters of the Black Sea for Constantinople. His large, solitary figure marked the end of an era in Crimea, the last stand of the Russian Empire.

Suffering not one but potentially two assassination attempts, Wrangel established the Russian All-Military Union in 1924, an organization of all fellow exiled White Army veterans and those who had served in imperial forces previously, as Wrangel himself had before the revolution. Wrangel also became the de facto leader of and voice for all Russians who had escaped. Like many, he would find work and build a new life farther West. For Wrangel, that was a career as a mining engineer in Brussels, Belgium. We implore you to read his memoir, Always with Honor, published in 1928.

About those assassination attempts … the first alleged attack was the 1920 sinking of a boat he owned but was not on at the time. The second, also alleged, came in 1928. If it was, in fact, assassination, it was successful. Wrangel passed away suddenly in Brussels. Suspicion was cast on a suspected Soviet agent, the brother of a butler who had access to the Wrangel household.

Wrangel fought the communists so well that they (very likely) took him out. Unlike other targets of Lenin and Stalin, Wrangel was, had been, and would remain the chief threat to the Soviet Union. They knew he alone could restore the Russian Empire. That is legacy.

Wrangel’s funeral procession was held in Belgrade, Yugoslavia, where he had settled in exile. It was a solemn and regal affair that drew attention from around the world. His tomb is still in Belgrade today. Wrangel was extremely respected among Russian émigrés and anti-communists for his steadfast opposition to the Bolshevik regime. As his casket made its way through the streets of Belgrade, draped in Imperial Russian and Serbian flags, thousands of mourners paid their respects, including members of the Russian diaspora, Yugoslav officials, and foreign diplomats. The procession reflected not only the passing of a military leader but also the end of an era for the Russian émigré community, as Wrangel’s death symbolized the fading hopes of many exiles for a return to their homeland under Bolshevik rule. Wrangel’s funeral was the last formal ceremony of the Russian Empire.

To the last, Wrangel never wavered in his loyalty to the Romanov family, to the Russian Empire, and his steadfast conviction that communism must be defeated.

When Wrangel was asked by a journalist why the White Army fought, he had this to say. From his memoir, Always with Honor:

There can be only one answer to this question… . We are fighting for freedom. On the other side of the northern front despotism, oppression, and slavery reign supreme. One can have the most varied opinion as to the opportuneness of such and such a regime, one may be republican, radical, socialist, or even Marxist, and still realize that the so-called Soviet Republic is nothing but the expression of an unprecedented, sinister despotism, which is sending Russia into a decline. Even the so-called governing class, the proletariat, are oppressed as much as the rest of the population. Europe no longer regards it a mystery— the veil has been torn from the face of Soviet Russia. Moscow is the heart and centre of the reaction, for there live the tyrants who treat the people like beasts. As for ourselves, anyone who treats us as reactionaries must be either blind or dishonest. We are fighting to free our people from a bondage such as they have never experienced before, even in the darkest epochs of their history. For a long time Europe did not understand this, but it seems that she is just beginning to understand what we realize so clearly—the world-wide significance of our Civil War. If our sacrifices are to prove in vain, European society, European democracy, will be forced to take up arms to defend its cultural and political possessions against an enemy who is drunk with success.7

We cannot help but wonder in grief how different the story of Pyotr Wrangel would and could have ended had he become sole commander of the White Army earlier—and had the White Army’s predecessor the imperial army of Tsarist Russia treated the threat of Bolshevism as it needed to be treated. But it was not, even when it was obviously about to be too late to do so. As Solzhenitsyn said, the uprising went unresisted, to a man—even as those men and soldiers saw what was coming. And yet they did not respond accordingly. Terror has that effect. By the time it’s felt, it’s too late.

Another opportunity for alternative history and White Army victory is the Franco strategy, which you’ll read more about in the next chapter. If you are already familiar with the Spanish Civil War, you know that the governments of Germany and Italy threw tactical support behind Francisco Franco and his Nationalist army. This made their civil war the fair fight that the Russian Revolution and Russian Civil War were not.

And so we’re left to wonder. What if US President Woodrow Wilson saw Vladimir Lenin for who he was and would become? What if the Tsar’s own cousin had recognized the true enemy in the 1910s was not the competing empire of Germany, but the dark forces of unhumanity ready and willing to murder his own flesh in cold blood? What if indeed.

We close with Solzhenitsyn:

Every man always has handy a dozen glib little reasons why he is right not to sacrifice himself.8

Beyond Pyotr Wrangel, most Russians did not. The unhumans won. They robbed and killed at abandon until there was no one left to rob and kill.

This is what they do.

Death Toll of the Russian Revolution: 20 million

cSZ

CHAPTER 7

THE LAST CRUSADE: THE UNTOLD STORY OF THE SPANISH CIVIL WAR

To promote the ideology of collectivism, false leaders are willing to stoop to the lowest and slimiest of Communist-Socialist tricks. One of these tricks … is to declare that all anti-Communists must be, per se, pro-Fascist.1

—Verne P. Kaub, Communist-Socialist Propaganda in American Schools

Internet-search “Generalisimo Franco” and the first results you’re likely to see are descriptors such as “rebel leader,” “dictator,” and of course “fascist.” Look up images of his rule in Spain, and you’ll quickly find amateur-edited photographs showing a side-by-side of Francisco Franco with Adolf Hitler and, naturally, swastikas.

It is often said that history is written by the winners, but the Spanish Civil War is a strange exception. Indeed, most accounts portray the communist-socialist republic Franco fought as “the good guys”—this despite their loss to his forces. Such an elementary (and incorrect) understanding of the initial uprising, armed resistance, eventual overthrow, and prelude to World War II was introduced to many of us by widespread required reading in school, such as For Whom the Bell Tolls by Ernest Hemingway and Homage to Catalonia by George Orwell (the same guy who wrote 1984 and Animal Farm). The “Manuel Azaña good, Francisco Franco bad” narrative is retold and reinforced in popular media, from streaming documentaries to following your own curiosity about the Spanish civil war on Wikipedia. Challenge, this narrative and you’re going to have a bad time, as our man Jack did.

This chapter is adapted from the Human Events Daily episode of the same name. Upon airing over Christmas 2023, there was great weeping and gnashing of teeth. Communists and leftists from Spain, the UK, and the United States condemned Posobiec and guest cohost Blake Neff for the Franco episode. They were decried as neofascists so loudly and widely that Franco became trending news on X (formerly Twitter). In fact, the podcast episode went so viral and so against the allowed narrative, that it was actually banned in Spain through X. Downloads were substantial, and of all the Human Events Daily 2023 episodes on communist revolutions, Franco’s was by far the most popular.

All for the crime of going against the preferred story. That is, against the story we’re told—that the republicans fought bravely for civil rights and social justice, and the nationalists staged a brutal military coup and tortured their adversaries in dark, dingy prisons for decades thereafter for the thrill of it is the story from the perspective of the losing side. This is the case not just in English-speaking media and education but also in Spain. In 2007, Spain passed the Law of Historic Memory, which mandated the removal of all remaining Franco tributes and symbols from public buildings, with some exceptions for works of religious or artistic significance. This comes after the efforts began in 1975 following Franco’s death when removal and destruction of statues, street names, and plaques commemorating those who died in the Spanish Civil War initially began. The Spanish people are forbidden from their own history.

Even when communists are defeated, their ideological descendants resurrect Act III, the purge. This is what they do. And as a result, in Spain’s case, what the Spanish Civil War is rarely portrayed with is accuracy. One of the lone voices who gets it right is Warren Carroll, author of The Last Crusade: Spain 1936. A crusade, for the new initiates, is a righteous, justified war for the sake of the cross—that is, for the honor and glory of Jesus Christ. Specifically, this classical crusade is a war to protect Christians from persecution even unto death on account of their faith. Now, everyone has heard of the holy land crusades of the Middle Ages, but few know of the crusade that happened in Spain in 1936. You already know the setup—how the environment for anti-Christian hatred was sown and persecution reaped. How Franco was called to restore law and order. How the communist-socialist Rojos had free rein to inflict terror on countryside and city alike once political power was theirs.

From Carroll’s book description:

Everyone has heard of the crusades of the Middle Ages. But few know of the crusade in our time, which living men still remember, fought for this same purpose only sixty years ago in Spain. In just six months of the year 1936, 13 bishops and nearly 7,000 priests, seminarians, monks, and nuns were martyred in Spain by enemies of Christianity. It was the greatest clerical bloodletting in so short a span of time since the persecutions on the Church by the ancient Roman emperors. Already Pope John Paul II has beatified some 200 of these martyrs. Tens of thousands of churches, chapels, and shrines in Spain were pillaged or destroyed. In response, faithful Spanish Catholics proclaimed a crusade. Against all odds the crusaders triumphed, and the Church and the Faith in Spain were saved. This is the story of that crusade, now honored in no other book in print in the English language. Most people who know of the Spanish Civil War do not understand why it was fought or how it was really won. This book will tell you. There is no story like it in the history of the twentieth century.2

So what happened?

The Comintern Comes to Spain and So Does Hell Itself

We’ve covered much history since our last mentions of Spain in Chapter 4. So here’s a quick review of the situation:

The Spanish Civil War was fought between 1936 and 1939 and ended in nationalist victory. In the run-up to the war, Spain fell into extreme social turmoil. A radical left and traditional right contended with one another in preceding years. When the Popular Front electoral victory turned the government far-leftist, anti-religious and anti-landowner sentiment became national and local policy. The Soviet Union’s Comintern, already assisting the Spanish communist movement, now hoped to export their red revolution using the coffers looted from the Russian treasury. The Spanish Republic’s far-left policies attracted not only local anarchists but also Western radicals and communist-sympathizers, who would later write of their experiences in detail.

Communist militias, aligned with leftist Republican factions, the Rojos, viewed the Catholic Church as a symbol of oppression and a pillar of the conservative establishment they sought to overthrow. As a result, they sought to eradicate religious influence from areas under their control. Nuns and priests were seen as representatives of this influence and were therefore considered enemies of the revolution. In some instances, religious figures were given the choice to renounce their faith or face execution, while others were killed outright for their perceived allegiance to the Church. The violence against nuns and priests during the Spanish Civil War remains a dark chapter in the history of the conflict, reflecting the deep ideological divisions and tensions that tore Spanish society apart during this turbulent period.

In some instances, even sexual violence—rape—of nuns occurred, with reports that it took place within the confines of churches. There is substantial documented evidence and photographs of executed nuns having their corpses put on display by the Spanish Rojos in the town square. This was indicative of the belief that the new regime had supplanted the old, and as all revolutionary regimes do, they needed to destroy the previous religion of the old. In Spain, that meant that the churches had to be desecrated, priests killed, and nuns raped.

Antony Beevor in The Battle for Spain: The Spanish Civil War 1936–1939 wrote of the unhuman brutality the Rojos inflicted upon the priests and other enemies of the socialist state: castration, disembowelment, burning alive, and burying alive after forcing the victims to dig their own graves. That said, targeting clergy and other professions including business owners was largely based on perceived class. Priests and shopkeepers perceived to be relatively poor were spared. Those deemed enemies of the poor were not. Beevor writes of rumors that an offense deemed worthy of murder by the revolutionaries was that of being seen in public wearing a hat and tie.3

Murder wasn’t the end of the communist atrocities but merely the beginning. Rojos militias and affiliated organizations expropriated land from large landowners, often without compensation, and redistributed it among peasants and agricultural workers. The revolutionary hates private property. This process involved seizing estates, collectivizing farms, and establishing agricultural cooperatives where land was collectively owned and worked by the community—communes, just as in the USSR. Additionally, Spanish Rojos sought to dismantle the power and influence of landowners by targeting them politically and socially. Landowners perceived as aligned with the conservative or nationalist factions were often subjected to persecution, including arrest, confiscation of property, and in some cases, execution.

The agrarian reforms carried out by Spanish Rojos during the Civil War claimed to address long-standing grievances related to landownership and inequality in rural areas. However, these measures were also highly controversial and contributed to the polarization of Spanish society during the conflict. They never actually led to equality, only to bloodshed. After the war, when Franco’s forces emerged victorious, many of these reforms were reversed, and land was returned to its previous owners.

One digressive yet provocate detail worth adding to the Spanish Civil War story is an unusual way the Rojos revolutionaries inflicted psychological torture on prisoners: modern art. From the Washington Post at the time:

Researchers say the Soviet-inspired cells were the size of a walkin closet, with terra-cotta bricks sticking up from the floor at sharp angles …

One wall was curved, and others were painted with circles, cubes, slanted lines, spirals, checkerboards and other shapes. By night a blinding red spotlight made it all appear to move and pulsate. Sense of time and distance crumbled.

The light of day filtered through a window dyed green—the color these tormentors thought best for gutting a man’s spirit.

“Just seeing the inside of a cell is enough to make you dizzy,” said Cesar Vidal, a historian who has written extensively on the war. “I imagine that if you were in there for days you could go mad.”

He estimated that several hundred people spent time in the cells, used to extract confessions or extort money.4

WaPo seems to be describing the modern art displays that assault citizens throughout major Western city centers and otherwise aweinspiring museums. Pinnacles of Western achievement have become open-air communist prisons, perhaps with the same effect on us all. But that’s a story for another time.

Coming back to Spain now, when the Rojos forces were not imprisoning or torturing, they were robbing and killing. They had plenty of eager recruits along the way. The breakdown of order in many areas led to the release of prisoners. Both sides of the conflict—Communists and Nationalists—released prisoners for various reasons, including the enlistment of prisoners into military units. In Republican areas, the early months of the war saw mass releases of prisoners, including criminals, as part of the revolutionary fervor, leading to periods of lawlessness and retribution against perceived enemies.

This is what they do.

All that unhuman bloodshed unleashed upon Christians and landlords among other targets could not go unpunished. Total anarchy had been unleashed upon enemies of the red state; believers in an orderly, traditional Catholic Spain felt called to quell the terror. And so a counterrevolution was born in the shape of a big-tent coup d’état— a military uprising. Even this, we admit, is a loaded term. A military coup in the American mind is usually associated with some comically bemedaled governor-general character wearing a beret before cameras and between armed guards. And yet Francisco Franco and his forces the Nationalists were more akin to 1770s George Washington and the colonial patriots. Much like the United States founding fathers, Franco and his fellows saw themselves as rebels intended to overthrow a corrupt, tyrannical government that aided and abetted murder and rape as well as other repugnant sins. We believe the Nationalist cause was righteous and neo-American. These words of Thomas Jefferson, excerpted from the Declaration of Independence, perfectly describe the 1936 counterrevolution in Spain:

Governments are instituted among men, deriving their just powers from the consent of the governed, that whenever any form of government becomes destructive of these ends, it is the right of the people to alter or to abolish it, and to institute new government.5

As in the American Revolution, the defenders of principle had a steep uphill battle. Many battles, in fact. Franco did not anticipate an extended civil war; hoping for what, perhaps could have been a repeat of the 1934 Asturias mining strike, in which Franco’s forces crushed an uprising. However, the Nationalists initially took about only one-third of Spain. From there, they received much-needed reinforcements from a Spanish unit in Africa as well as support from aligned anti-communist groups in western and northwestern Spain. Meanwhile, Rojos forces controlled major cities such as Madrid and Barcelona as well as all of Catalonia in the northeast of the country. They also benefited from the full support of the Comintern; the leftist republic received weapons, ammunition, airplanes, tanks, and other vehicles from the Soviet Union to wage civil war, exceeding far more than comparable warfighting resources that were provided to the Nationalists by Germany and Italy combined. Worldwide communist organizations also mobilized international brigades of volunteers so any would-be radical could come to Spain to rob and/or kill any and all non-communists. In fact, one of these was called the International Antifascist Brigade.

The governments of Germany and Italy supported the Spanish Nationalists against the Soviet-backed Rojos in the war. “The enemy of my enemy is my friend.” During World War II, which began just as the Spanish Civil War ended, no one adversary would be responsible for more Soviet casualties than Germany. Recall that pre-revolutionary France, a traditionalist absolute monarchy with serious grievances against it, supplied the American colonial army with essential supplies they were unable to get anywhere else. Benjamin Franklin was the man behind that persuasion of Louis XIV. We cannot fault Franco for doing exactly as Franklin and Washington had done before him to secure his country, and certainly should not judge history through the lens of the contemporary.

Despite total global communist support, the left ultimately lost Spain. Why? For that answer, we retell the same story of Bolshevik Russia: nasty and stupid. Unhumans in any time and place can organize, but they cannot stay organized. All they know to do once power is theirs is use that same power against themselves, a perpetual circular firing squad. That’s basically what happened to the Second Spanish Republic.

The Left Eats Itself (Again)

The Spanish Civil War was not just a conflict between left and right, between anarchy and order, or between anti-Christians and Catholics, though it was all that and more. It was also a war between leftists and further leftists … and the further leftists who came after these leftists were executed … and then also against the even more extreme left following those leftists … and so on and so on.

Generalissimo Franco found his greatest allies among the communists, as they tore themselves apart. They simply could not agree on what to do, to the point of literally shooting one another over disagreement. Usually, such dustups occur after the unhumans win their revolutions. The Spanish communists were trying to speed-run a Bolshevik uprising and so couldn’t wait for that apparently.

George Orwell, in his book Homage to Catalonia, discusses his experience as one of the international socialists volunteering to fight on the Republican side. He joined the Workers’ Party of Marxist Unification, known by its Spanish acronym POUM. He saw combat in that militia and suffered a bullet wound to the throat. While recuperating away from the action, he caught the news that POUM had been banned—by the socialist government! Republican forces split their attention (and ammunition) between Franco’s forces and POUM members, arresting and executing POUM members. POUM had itself been formed out of two communist factions that had a dispute with other radicals over Leon Trotsky. (Where have we read this before?) Radicals like Orwell were Marxists to be sure, but not the right kind of Marxist. So they must be robbed and killed. This is what they do.

Spanish Marxism was decentralized and anarchic compared to the disciplined unity of the post-revolutionary Soviet Union that would last until its own suicide in 1991. The socialist Spanish government, headquartered in Madrid and thoroughly smitten with Joseph Stalin their chief benefactor, purged all non-Stalinists there and elsewhere they controlled. The term “friendly fire” does not even begin to describe the internal conflict; there were vicious street battles in which more than 1,000 communists died, falling to each other’s fire. By contrast, Franco unified all conservatives, Catholics, and anti-communists with a simple message that, translated for modern audiences was this:

Make Spain Great Again!

And so they did.

Francisco Franco, a Great Man of History

On July 18, 1936, Francisco Franco gave a radio address from the Canary Islands. He knew the time and the season; a leading conservative politician had just been murdered by leftists. Of robbing and killing there would be no end—unless it was ended. By force and firepower unlike anything the communists imagined would or could oppose them.

Franco’s own words, translated into English, are a bit more elegant than our abbreviated version above. From Franco to Spain, to any and all who were turned in, on the eve of civil war:

Spaniards!

To all of you who feel holy love for Spain, to all of you who in the ranks of the army and the navy have sworn to serve the fatherland, to those of you who swore to defend it from its enemies with your lives, the nation calls you to defend it. The situation in Spain has been growing worse every day: anarchy reigns in most of the countryside and the towns; authorities named by the government preside over revolts, when they do not directly promote them. Pistols and machine guns are used to settle differences between groups of citizens, who murder each other treacherously and treasonously while the public powers do nothing to impose peace and justice.

Revolutionary strikes of all kinds paralyze the nation, ruining and destroying its sources of wealth and creating a situation of hunger that will throw working men into a state of desperation.

Artistic monuments and treasures are the object of the most frenzied attacks by revolutionary hordes obeying the commands they receive from foreign directors, who count on the complicity or negligence of governors and officials.

The most serious crimes are committed in the countryside while the forces of public order remain in their barracks, restrained by blind obedience to governors who intend to dishonor them. The army, the navy, and other military forces are the target of the lowest and most slanderous attacks by the very ones who should safeguard their prestige.6

Ironically, for being remembered in the West as a fascist dictator, the eventual victorious general Franco—the self-proclaimed caudillo, or leader, of postwar new Spain—didn’t actually do a lot of fascism or dictating.

Following the surrender of all communist Rojos forces in Madrid on March 28, 1939, Franco became, in our view, a Washingtonian figure but without term limits. His top priority upon assumption of power was to restore something like a traditional Catholic monarchy, which had been foundational to Spanish civilization for centuries. A G. K. Chesterton quip offers a helpful frame for viewing Franco’s motivation before, during, and after the Spanish Civil War:

Do not remove a fence until you know why it was put up in the first place.

This was the counterrevolution in Spain: Keep up the fence. A conservative, Catholic, royal fence. There was no need for radicalism. Franco did not execute thousands of his own followers as Stalin or Mao did, nor did he turn Spanish society upside-down in the name of progress. There was no terror-famine in Spain, no Great Leap Forward, or Cultural Revolution. He was, simply, the caudillo. The boss.

As old allies in Germany and Italy fought and fell, Franco refused to join. The Spanish Civil War had been in effect a proxy war between the European Axis powers and the Soviet Union. It also served as a convenient testing ground for World War II tactics such as dive-bombing and close air support. One right-wing faction of Nationalists called the Falange favored the fascism of Hitler’s Germany and Mussolini’s Italy, but they were marginalized. World War II was all risk, no reward from the Spanish perspective. The fence stays up.

Over time, things would change, with Spain transitioning over the twentieth century into a left-wing constitutional limited monarchy, with the old royal lineage of kings returning to power. But this change moved at a safe pace for the people. Spain was not irrevocably broken under Franco or during the peaceful transition of power back to the old royal family that had ruled Spain prior to the conflicts of the 1930s. There was an initial and even lasting grief, of course, as is the legacy of every civil war. Tens of thousands died. A people and a nation were scarred. And yet Spain remained. There was no bemoaning of the loss of heritage because that loss was not allowed to happen.

What Spain saw instead under Franco was an effort to restore many aspects of Spanish culture, such as the Church—just as Napoleon had done in post-revolutionary France. Now, Franco was not a religious official but was a regular church attendee. He was and remained a Nationalist; he valued what his nation valued, and that was among others, the Church’s meaningful role in Catholic Spanish society, identifying the religion with the nation.

With the obvious good that the Nationalists fought for, how could so many otherwise good people fight for the other side? Ernest Hemingway and George Orwell are in the canon of Western literature. And yet they joined the revolutionary brigades to fight alongside those banning religious schooling, burning Bibles, torturing clergy to death, and murdering homeowners and landowners at random. What’s going on there?

Documentary filmmaker Mike Cernovich has famously said that all media is narrative. This is prescient for the current age of fake news, alternative facts, and community notes. And it is also perennial. Leftists of old and of the present have been first and foremost propagandized; after all, the Messaging Stage is essential to their prepping of a given society for upheaval and ruin.

Just as events in Spain were heating up, leftists in the United Kingdom, the United States, and elsewhere radicals had quietly infiltrated were marketing the cause. Leading Russian American anarchist agitator Emma Goldman even traveled to Spain in 1936 to work in the propaganda effort. The global left spun a sob story about democracy. It worked. And it was partially true, too. Although the socialist-communist Second Republic was a failing democracy—right-wing parties won the November 1933 general election but were denied power—the government was, technically, a democracy. The Spanish right were instigators of the actual war, beginning it all with a military coup. Of course, the nationalists would point out that the Rojos were persecuting and torturing their own citizens. Franco had created what public relations professionals might call an “optics problem”—how bad a thing looks versus how bad it actually is. Unfortunately, from the perspective of the Nationalists, their efforts gave their enemies ammunition in the media—pro-democracy Westerners were told that a fellow democracy was under attack, and they’re being helped by fascists. One can’t imagine better recruiting for the communist side.

Those who actually joined the international brigades to risk life and limb were more than dreamers. Or less than, if you know what we mean. Volunteering to loot and shoot total strangers in a faraway land appeals to certain lawless, radical, and disgruntled contingents. The pseudo-ideology of Marxism persuades those with hate in their hearts. These malcontents are easily won over by the promise of hurting those they hate. The Republican cause provided a romantic narrative to conceal the truth, casting the Rojos communist-socialists as progressives building a new, vibrant, and diverse society, the spreaders of modernity, held back by fascist killers who wanted to drag the good working people of Spain back to the Dark Ages by hook or by crook.

Orwell, of course, came away disillusioned with the Rojos. Prior to service in the brigade, he had been pro-socialist. But after witnessing the violent purges of the Rojos side for himself, he was put off by all left-wingers from then onward. Orwell noted the open lies told by the Popular Front government, detailing great battles that never happened while ignoring those battles that didn’t go so well for their forces. Brave revolutionary soldiers were denounced as cowards and traitors (the wrong kinds of Marxist) while socialist activists who had never fought were hailed as heroes. Newspapers in London and around the world repeated the Republicans’ lies. The screaming, deceit, and hatred came from those who did no fighting. All in all, Orwell was none too pleased with how much he was lied to in that war. Both 1984 and Animal Farm were written after his stint in the civil war, the totalitarian regimes of both novels based no doubt on experiencing communism in Spain. But in our view, his Homage to Catalonia is a more sobering read than 1984 because he described real times and places with an honesty his former soldiers-in-arms did not show.

One of the only Franco books available from the non-communist perspective is Mine Were of Trouble by Peter Kemp, a British volunteer for Franco. In it, Kemp recounts his meetings with Francisco Franco; Kemp first met Franco in early 1938 after joining the Spanish Legion. During this meeting, Franco expressed his gratitude to Kemp for fighting on the Nationalist side and shared his views on the dangers posed by the Soviet Union. Franco also mentioned that he believed Britain would have to take the lead in the fight against communism in the years to come. This was a fight Franco knew firsthand but would leave to others once Spain’s domestic enemies were defeated.7

We’d like to leave this chapter of history with a story from that defeat, one you probably don’t know.

Fortress of Freedom

The Alcázar of Toledo is a historic fortress-palace located in the ancient city of Toledo, Spain. Situated atop a rocky hill overlooking the Tagus River, the Alcázar commands a strategic position that has been occupied since Roman times. The current structure dates primarily from the medieval period, with Moorish influences evident in its architecture. Over the centuries, the Alcázar has served various purposes, including as a royal residence, a military fortress, and a military academy. The Alcázar became a national symbol of Spain.

From July to September of 1936 during the military rising’s early stages, Nationalist militias had held the Alcázar, but by the end, they were barely holding on, and in dire need of relief. Commander of the besieging Rojos forces demanded surrender, and they had more than words. The son (Luis Moscardó) of the man leading the Alcázar’s defenders (Colonel José Moscardó) had been captured. If only the Nationalists would surrender, Luis’ life would be spared. The colonel asked to speak with his son. It was allowed. This is their conversation:

Luis: Dad!

Col. Moscardó: What’s going on with you, my son?

Luis: Nothing, at all … they say they will shoot me if the Alcázar does not surrender. But don’t worry about me.

Col. Moscardó: If it is true, commend your soul to God, shout, ‘Long live Spain,’ and you will be a hero who died for her. Goodbye my son, a big kiss, with much love!

Luis: Goodbye Dad, a big kiss, with much love!

Col. Moscardó: You can all spare yourself the waiting for end of the deadline and start shooting, my son. The Alcázar will never surrender!8

Luis was shot by firing squad one month later. His death was not in vain. Franco himself made sure of it.

Upon arriving on the scene at the Alcázar, Franco wasted no time in assessing the situation and coordinating with his officers to devise a strategy for the assault. With characteristic decisiveness, he deployed his troops with precision, aiming to swiftly overwhelm the Rojos attackers and secure the Alcázar’s liberation.

Cheers rose up from the defenders of the Alcázar as Franco’s relief army arrived. The Soviet-backed Rojos attackers outside screamed curses and insults at seeing the traditional colors of Spain flown by Franco’s forces. Franco’s presence on the front lines served as a rallying point for his troops, inspiring them to press forward with unwavering resolve. Leading by example, he fearlessly led his forces into the heart of the battle, navigating the labyrinthine corridors and courtyards of the ancient fortress with determination. Despite the fierce resistance encountered from the Republican defenders, Franco remained steadfast in his commitment to reclaiming the Alcázar for the Nationalist cause. His resolve and leadership proved decisive, culminating in the successful liberation of the fortress and a significant victory for the Nationalist forces. The battle was won and so would be the last crusade.

And that is precisely what this war was. Christian versus anti-Christian. Civilization versus anti-civilization. Human versus unhuman.

In Mine Were of Trouble, Peter Kemp describes an incident in the midst of the Spanish Civil War, where he was injured by a mortar explosion while serving in the Nationalist forces. Despite his severe injuries, including a shattered jaw, multiple lacerations, and a head wound, Kemp refused to give in to despair. He was determined to survive and return to his home. Kemp’s resilience and determination are evident as he walks to the headquarters himself for medical treatment, despite his injuries. The soldiers for Franco were humans, not like the writings of the leftists and the revisionists would have. They prevented communism from taking Spain.

The Spanish Civil War made willing martyrs of many. And that is one of many differences between the unhumans and the rest of us, particularly those of faith. Good, believing people are willing to die for a cause greater than ourselves. But unhumans are willing to kill innocents—and each other—for no cause at all.

This is what they do. And all it takes to stop them is one man who says no, one man with some help. A man who says:

Wherever I am, there will be no communism.

And he means it.

As an addendum, how hard is Spain’s current government trying to prevent anyone from knowing the truth about Franco?

When the socialists took control of the government in the mid-2000s, they banned any symbols of the Franco government from public display. In 2019, they exhumed Franco’s body from its public tomb in the Valley of the Fallen and reinterred him in a municipal graveyard in Madrid.

This is what they do.

Death Toll of the Spanish Civil War: 500,000

cVX

CHAPTER 8

THE UNHUMAN REPUBLIC OF CHINA: THE UNTOLD STORY OF MAO’S COMMUNIST REVOLUTIONS

You must all be aware that modern war is not a mere matter of military operations. It involves the whole strength and all the resources of the nation. Not only soldiers, but also all citizens without exception, take part.

—Chiang Kai-shek

When you read about modern China today, you’ll find the nation is now the world’s manufacturing hub and boasts an advanced command economy. Its skylines reach beyond the horizon and at night gleam the colors of cyberpunk utopia. The world buys everything from here. And yet China is in an intense geopolitical rivalry with the United States and the collective West, engaging in full-spectrum unconventional warfare against America, her citizens, and her allies— a gray-zone war that is at the time of this writing not yet open and “hot” enough to merit an overwhelming military response. This couples with the irregular revolutionary war the West is currently engaged in internally against the forces of Cultural Marxist-Leninists.

But China, known as the “Central Kingdom” in Mandarin, before modernization and globalization was a different place. This was the land of foot-binding, in which for a thousand years, young girls had their feet broken and misshapenly healed to keep them small in the name of culture and tradition. This is the place where for almost three hundred years, Han adult males were required to shave their heads except for one long braid in the back—or be put to death by the Qing Dynasty, led by the Manchu.

A dynastic succession of emperors ruled the period of imperial China—the Han, the Tang, the Ming, the Song, and finally the Qing. Similar to the European divine right of kings, the Chinese ascribed to the “mandate of heaven,” the belief that only one ruler at a time could be the legitimate authority over all China, and that ruler possessed total power over the land and the people—so long as they ruled justly. As we’ve seen previously, the last imperial dynasty—the Qing—lost that mandate. The British defeated the Qing in the Opium Wars, which were fueled by the British-backed illegal drug trade in China. This is how Britain won Hong Kong Island and built the city there. Shanghai was opened up to the British, then the French. The Boxer Rebellion was an anti-foreigner rebellion that was covertly funded by the Qing Dynasty, but that, too, was defeated by a coalition of European nations together with Japan and even the United States. Beijing, then called Peking, was occupied by this military coalition.

With the mandate of heaven surrendered, the Chinese people rebelled. There is a theory in China of dynastic cycles, and it was seen as coming to pass. And so a 3,500-year-old system of government came to an end. The old imperial system was dead; the new Republic of China was born.

The Mandate of Communism

In Chapter 3, we briefly met Sun Yat-sen, nationalist leader of the new republic set up to govern China after the fall of the Qing Dynasty. Sun is regarded today by all sides as the founder of modern China. Where there is a power vacuum, multiple factions are sucked in to fill it. This was the case for China in the 1910s and early ’20s. Friends of the newly formed Soviet Union were the least of Sun’s worries in the first months of republican order. A warlord named Yuan Shikai challenged Sun’s rule. The republic was too weak to govern all China, and supporting Yuan were a host of other local warlords claiming possession of various territories. Yuan took over the fledgling republic and even briefly declared himself a new emperor. From the north, the Soviets observed a golden opportunity to solve this regional faction problem. That’s how the 1921 Shanghai meeting came to be, which saw the founding of the Chinese Community Party with thirteen members in attendance.1 The low building is still there in Shanghai today, and Jack visited on multiple occasions in his years living in the city (ironically, it is now across the street from a nightclub district). Several members of that initial meeting were Soviet intelligence officers, so from the very start, the CCP was a Soviet-funded, Soviet-financed operation. They found easy allies in China’s working class; no persuasion effort was required.

Consider one of the founding CCP members, Mao Zedong, son of a wealthy peasant. He resented the long days of toiling in the fields. His father had eventually become a grain merchant and saved up a little money, more than most in his class, but Mao didn’t want to follow in his footsteps; instead, he wanted complete and permanent change. Revolution. Mao learned something growing up in remote and rural Hunan Province: the power of thugs, banditry, and martial force. And these brutal tactics, such as burning villages, torturing innocents, and the like, tactics which cause us to recoil, excited Mao. He derived pleasure from suffering, as all unhumans do. And so Mao’s brand of Leninism, later known as Maoism naturally, was more savage than any incarnation of communism seen prior or since. Mao saw purification of his people through pain as the only acceptable route. We see a basis for this in the Chinese Buddhist tradition of pain as essential to the journey of life. To live is to suffer, both physically and psychologically. Thus, the greater the pain, the greater the glory. Together with Confucianism, the Chinese philosophy that elevates hierarchy and the well-being of the family and strength of the community over the rights of any one individual, the Chinese people’s ideological heritage allowed communism to thrive quickly and deeply in a way it did not anywhere else.2

The CCP grew in power and in number in the rural, western reaches of China and would soon come into conflict with the Republic of China, centered along the eastern coast. Mao and his followers— known as the Red Army—deployed tactics on anyone in their way, those used against the landlords you read about in Chapter 3 but also anyone who would not pledge enthusiastic loyalty to Mao. And of course, all torture and execution was conducted in public, in plain view of the peasantry, so they could understand who was in charge. With funding from the Soviet Union, Mao carried out a ten-year reign of terror throughout western China, running parallel to the civil war against the Republic. It was in 1927 that the communists suffered a massacre of their own in Shanghai. Growing vexed by their rise, the Nationalists, headed by Chiang Kai-Shek, hired a local Triad gang—China’s most famous organized crime syndicate—to find and eliminate every communist they could. They did.

World War China

Chiang Kai-shek’s rise to prominence and leadership of the Nationalists began in the early 1920s amid the chaos of China’s Warlord Era. Initially trained in military schools in China and later in Japan, Chiang became a protege of Sun Yat-sen. Following Sun’s death in 1925, Chiang assumed leadership of the Nationalist Party (the KMT) and embarked on the Northern Expedition, a military campaign aimed at reunifying China and ending the warlord era. Chiang’s strategic brilliance and political maneuvering allowed him to consolidate power, eventually leading to the establishment of a Nationalist government in Nanjing in 1927. The only threat to Chiang’s new republic was Mao Zedong and his Soviet-backed Red Army.

After the Shanghai Massacre, the Communists and Nationalists broke into an open civil war, which continued until another player entered the scene: the Empire of Japan. Japan invaded China in 1931, at first taking only Manchuria, which the Japanese then turned into a puppet state called Manchukuo. They even located the last emperor of the old Qing dynasty (remember Pu Yi?) and put him in charge. At that point Japan controlled both Korea and Taiwan as well. The importance of this invasion cannot be overstated because now the Nationalists were fighting a two-front war against the communists to their west and the Japanese to their north. But the Nationalist forces, led by Chiang Kai-shek, pushed the communists out of eastern China and the cities they held, and encircled them.

Depleted and with no vehicles and barely any horses, Mao decided on a grand retreat—called the Long March. Late in 1934, he led the Red Army all the way out to rural Yan’an Province, as far as he could go. He lost 90 percent of his army to desertion, disease, injury, or infection; things looked bleak. But then something fell right into Mao’s hands—a stronger push by Japan into China. This was a massive problem for the Nationalists because the Japanese army pursued their forces into major power centers Beijing and Shanghai. The Japanese rained down utter destruction on the Chinese city dwellers; the Rape of Nanjing in 1937 is legendary for its ruthlessness.

Enraged at the Nationalists, Mao concluded that the urban proletariat was not ready to lead a revolution in China. Instead, he shifted his focus to rural areas, where he believed the peasantry held revolutionary potential. This marked the beginning of Mao’s infamous strategy of rural-based guerrilla warfare and his emphasis on mobilizing the rural population to overthrow the existing social and political order. Mao became the father of modern guerrilla warfare.

At that time, some of the regional warlords were still around. One warlord was a man named Zhang Xueliang, who managed to kidnap Chiang Kai-shek and told him that in order to fight back against Japan, he would have to form an alliance with Mao. What Chiang didn’t know was that Zhang had already cut a deal with Mao. Mao knew what had happened back in Shanghai when that Triad gang took out all his comrades there. So Mao made his own deal with the Triads and with every remaining warlord, among them Zhang. No matter who won the conflict with Japan, Mao would be among the victors. The First United Front was formed to fight the Japanese, first covered back in Communist OPE Stage 3, Infiltration. At that point, the actual revolution had not fully unfurled; Mao’s CCP had not yet attempted to overthrow the Republic, merely skirmishing with their forces. But that would all go on hold in order to take on imperial Japan together.

Together. That’s a pernicious little word. Keep in mind the vastness of China. Mao’s forces were in the west and the Nationalists to the east, much nearer the fronts of fighting that Japan had opened up. So while the communists did experience some limited engagements with the Japanese, Mao let the Nationalists do most of the fighting—or rather, most of the dying. Subdue the enemy without fighting. Meanwhile, throughout this time, Mao consolidated support among the peasantry and rebuilt his army. Had Japan not invaded when they did, the CCP may have ended with the Long March. Mao couldn’t have planned it better himself.

Because of Japanese invasion, not only were the people of China subjected to the horrors of Nanjing and the infernal human experimentation by Unit 731 (among other atrocities), the war ultimately created the conditions for the CCP to revive itself and take over. The Soviets invaded Manchuria and resoundingly defeated the Japanese. This set the stage for massive Soviet support of the CCP to the north of Beijing, in a key industrial region. Then after Japan fell to the United States and Allies in 1945, Mao made his comeback.

Note this: Immediately after World War II ended, the United States made it clear it was in their interest that the Nationalists could coexist peacefully with the CCP. But at that point, communists had infiltrated the US State Department and much of the US government. (Senator Joseph McCarthy was right.) These spies and their friendlies wanted the communists to win; they needed China to fall. They saw what Mao was doing, they knew what Mao was doing, and they believed he could go the distance. And so in 1946, the Nationalist-Communist unity period ended and the civil war restarted. The CCP with the Soviets now ensconced in Manchuria was poised for total victory. The United States did not provide Chiang Kai-shek with the material needs he had expected them to in order to win the war, despite having substantial assets in nearby Japan.

Generalissimo Chiang Kai-shek’s last significant military engagement was the Battle of Huai-Hai. The battle occurred between November 6, 1948, and January 10, 1949, in the Huai River region of eastern China, south of Beijing. Chiang Kai-shek’s Nationalist forces faced off against the Communist army, now named the People’s Liberation Army (PLA) commanded by Mao Zedong. Despite fierce resistance, the Nationalist forces were decisively defeated by the PLA, marking a turning point in the Chinese Civil War. The loss at the Battle of Huai-Hai effectively sealed the fate of Chiang Kai-shek’s Nationalist government.

The Communists unseated the Nationalist government in 1949. Chiang Kai-shek and his Nationalist forces fled the mainland, reestablishing the Republic of China on the island now known as Taiwan. On the way out, the Nationalists swept through the national treasury and various museums in Beijing, rescuing as many artifacts as possible so the communists wouldn’t destroy them. Chiang knew that not only do unhumans arrest, torture, and execute anyone in their way, they also view history itself as their enemy.

Generalissimo Chiang Kai-shek’s flight to Taiwan echoes Pyotr Wrangel’s final stand in Crimea about thirty years earlier. Unlike Wrangel, Chiang was able to leverage foreign military support to establish the Republican government in exile in Taiwan. To this day, the Taiwan issue in China is a remnant of the unfinished Chinese Civil War, as the CCP officially views Taiwan as a renegade province, and Taiwan, the exiled Nationalists, officially view themselves as the rightful government of all China.
