The American Response That Wasn’t

There was a failed mission to keep the communists from winning in China after the defeat of Japan. That mission had been headed by the capable General George Marshall. But why did this mission fail? According to Joseph McCarthy, it was because of a conspiracy so immense as to dwarf any previous venture in the history of man. He argued that General Albert Wedemeyer had prepared a plan that would’ve kept China a valued ally—but had been sabotaged. When Marshall was sent to China with secret State Department orders, the communists at the time were bottled up in two areas and fighting a losing battle. But because of those orders, the situation was radically changed in favor of the Communist forces. What orders? As we now know, General Marshall embargoed all arms and ammunition to our allies in China. He forced the opening of Nationalist-held arsenals in Manchuria, allowing the communists to get their hands on lots of captured Japanese weaponry. Oh. Those orders.

Were the communists allowed to win? Was Mao Zedong allowed to take China? Was this part of a deal that many have alleged took place between the highest levels of the US government, begun during the President Franklin D. Roosevelt administration then later was carried out by President Harry Truman’s State Department? Yes.

We’ll ask you this: Was there anything in it for the United States, for the Soviet Union to be handed a powerful ally next door?

No, there was not. Not then, not now.

But what if there was a group that wanted to create a one-world government? And what if there was a group of people who knew in order to do that, you would have to cut a deal with the other superpower in the world at the time. And who was that? The Soviet Union. Now, let’s say you wanted to found said globalist government. You might call it something like … the United Nations. But how would you get the Soviets to agree to take part in this program when they wouldn’t have to, or even have obvious interest in doing so? What could be the dowry for such a dark marriage?

Oh.

China. Let hundreds of millions of people fall to communism, and you have the Soviets’ acquiescence. A little far-fetched? Well, it’s not conspiracy theory when the conspiracy is already confirmed to have occurred. Declassified communications discussing these plans all the way back to the Franklin Roosevelt administration reveal some US government officials wanted communism to succeed not just in China but across Europe as well (we will explore these in the context of the Venona Intercepts further in Chapter 9). As Senator Robert Taft of Ohio said, “The greatest Kremlin asset in our history has been the pro-communist group in the State Department who surrendered to every demand of Russia at Yalta and Potsdam, and promoted at every opportunity the communist cause in China until today communism threatens to take over all of Asia.”3 The top-secret Yalta agreement specifically, mutually signed by President Franklin Roosevelt, British Prime Minister Winston Churchill, and Soviet Premier Joseph Stalin, agreed to hand over all of Manchuria to the Soviet Union as a reward for entering the war against Japan.4 The Soviets would invade Manchuria on the same day as the US atomic-bombed Nagasaki: August 9, 1945. Russia still controls Outer Manchuria to this day. Pro-communist State Department indeed. There’s a reason the US helped to industrialize the Soviet Union for years prior to World War II, even prior to American involvement in World War II. (Yes, that also happened. Not conspiracy theory. You can easily look this up. One notable example is the 1929 agreement between the Soviet Union and the American engineering company Albert Kahn Associates, which helped industrialize Soviet factories. Then, of course, there was the Lend-Lease Act, which gave billions of dollars in aid to the Soviets beginning in 1941.) Scholars who have explored Soviet-American relations in the context of China include diplomatic historians like John Lewis Gaddis and Melvyn Leffler as well as political scientists such as the great John Mearsheimer.

So, after the Japanese were forced to surrender in China and leave, the Nationalists were left to wither on the vine by the United States. Meanwhile, the communists fought nearer and nearer to the east, into former Nationalist strongholds, with millions taken out in the fighting. Over 2.5 million people died from starvation amid the civil war. As Mao himself said, “a revolution is not a dinner party.” He didn’t care how many people died. He gave orders to torture people, execute them, lay siege to cities where hundreds of thousands of people starved. And that was only a paltry sum compared to the millions who would die under his postwar rule. Because Mao wanted, more than anything else, absolute power within all China. It didn’t matter how many of his own people had to die so that he could get it.

All the while, Mao’s CCP continued to receive support from the Soviet Union from their power base in Manchuria. The Nationalists got support from nobody. And so, in October 1949, Beijing fell to the Red Army. After all the dust settled, Mao Zedong took to the top of the Gate of Tiananmen and proclaimed to the world the establishment of the People’s Republic of China, with the Chinese Communist Party as its official head and he as its brain. The CCP rules China to this very day.

As Mao stated, “The Chinese people have finally stood up.”

The mandate of heaven belonged to Mao. Not because he had earned it through just rule but because none were left to oppose his claim to absolute authority. And so none did. With one word, he could order executions, have girls presented to him (some as young as thirteen), and issue any decree without reason.

And his first policy would address agriculture—the world of his childhood.

Chaos under Heaven

What Mao had done to rural landlords during the 1920s peasant revolts, he would do again in the 1950s, nationwide. This was the Land Reform Campaign, this time not as insurrection but as government mandate. He led the CCP into every single peasant village and found anyone who owned land. If you owned land, it was taken from you. Revenge was now norm and policy. Landowners were shunned, publicly mocked, and in many cases executed. Millions of people died as this was carried out. Millions. In peacetime. Land reform for a population of more than 300 million people is a vicious war, more arduous and complex than crossing the Yangtze River because Mao’s red troops were 260 million peasant soldiers. There were those killed, then those who died as a result of the ensuing famines. This was class warfare in a battle to the death, and in this case, it was murder-suicide. And along the way, they did debanking, too; all banks were nationalized, and those deemed class enemies had their wealth—savings, assets, land— confiscated. This is what they do.

As for the killing to add to the robbing, it’s difficult to calculate with accuracy how many landowners were murdered by the Chinese Communist Party in the decade following World War II, and it wasn’t just landlords who were targeted by the death mobs. Mao soon realized that business owners, managers, and academics all caused problems for him; thus, he went after all of them.

But for the scholars, Mao had a new idea. He devised something called the Hundred Flowers Campaign in 1956, allowing academics and writers to speak out and tell the truth about what had been happening since the People’s Republic was founded. He said, “Let a hundred flowers bloom,” In effect, Just let us know how it’s going, if you have any criticisms; nothing will happen to you.

Something happened. The Hundred Flowers Campaign was an entrapment operation. Hundreds of thousands of intellectuals and professors were targeted in a new campaign called an “anti-right-wing movement,” as any critique of CCP policy was considered right-wing extremism. According to Chairman Mao, right-wing extremism was the enemy of the Revolution, and anyone who opposed him was a right-wing extremist. In fact, it was a year before the intellectuals gained the courage to respond to Mao’s call. Anyone earnest (foolish) enough to tell the truth was denounced, fired, or forced into labor camps. Many were specifically sent away to reeducation as well, where they would be driven to suicide.

Before retaliation against them, educators had complained bitterly about Chairman Mao’s obvious attempt to crudely parody the Soviet Union. Under Mao, Marxism-Leninism was held up as orthodox doctrine to be accepted without question. Wider social criticism focused on the authoritarian rule and various abuses of privilege of the new political elites. Yes, the revolution overthrew the old elites and replaced them with friends of Mao. This is what they do.

In the Hundred Flowers Campaign, every person who came out was not only persecuted but sent to the countryside. They were told that if they didn’t understand the importance of the peasantry, they must be made peasants. From the late 1950s into the 1960s, intellectuals were enslaved on farms all over China. And this was before the infamous Cultural Revolution. We’ll get to that in a bit. But millions must die first.

Like all communist organizations, the Chinese Communist Party was officially atheist and banned any practice of religion. Thus, Christianity in China was persecuted as well. Churches were closed and missionaries forced out. There were not the mass murders of priests, nuns, and other clergy and faithful as had occurred in communist-socialist Spain, but the CCP forced all Christian leaders to swear allegiance to the CCP. In a revolution, the unhumans always come for the people of God, because for vengeance-as-policy to thrive, there can be no higher power or greater will to serve than that of the Party. They smash religion, remove God from the public square, and anoint themselves the new supreme lawgivers. The revolution never ends.

Amid the persecution of academics and believers, Mao grew impatient, so he took a leap. One could say, a great leap.

The Great Leap Forward

As the ten-year mark of the People’s Republic was approaching, Mao felt frustrated that communist utopia had not yet been achieved. Why wasn’t the communism working! Millions had been killed, millions more deported, and still millions of others removed from their work, homes, and families and enslaved beneath the peasantry class. These peasants had then been placed in charge of all farms and factories, and yet nothing seemed to be working. Mao decided that industrial and agricultural growth would be made to move faster, better, and cheaper. And so he thought up the Great Leap Forward.

The Great Leap Forward had two objectives: first, industrialize the society and economy to catch up with the West, and second, transform China into a collectivized society where full socialism would be achieved. The latter was attempted through collective communes, like the 1871 Paris Commune, but for the whole of China. The massive farms would no longer be owned by any one individual, but by the state. You wouldn’t live in your own house anymore; instead, you’d live in a dormitory. You wouldn’t prepare your own food; instead, you’d be served in a cafeteria. You would have collective meals, collective work, collective education, and collective life. You would live at the beck and call of the state—if you were told to take a great leap forward off a bridge, you did so.

As for the first objective of the Great Leap Forward—catch up with America, Western Europe, and the Anglosphere—Mao came to believe that China would need to deviate from the path of the Soviet Union. The CCP must organize all peasant labor and eliminate all inefficiencies. Thus, what remained of private property was confiscated. Land, buildings, tools, livestock, even private homes—all of it was seized by the government.

The historian Philip Short wrote that officially, everyone was supposed to have at least six hours of sleep a day, but some work brigades boasted of working up to four or five days without stopping. And if there’s one thing you can trust from communists, it’s their labor data!

Mao was also obsessed with steel—which in the 1950s was being used to build a historically prosperous postwar West—and he wanted to find ways to make more. China had to make more steel, and had to make it faster. So, Mao then cooked up the idea of the “backyard furnace.” Spare iron would be put into these spontaneous makeshift furnaces to help make steel. Anyone in China who had iron was ordered to go into their backyards and smelt this iron right there. All farm equipment in China was melted, including household implements, cooking utensils, ladles, pots, woks, pans—everything was smelted down.

With all the farming and cooking equipment destroyed, you can guess what happened to the harvest. It was not good. This one policy resulted in a famine that killed tens of millions. Estimates of the death toll range for 47 to 60 million dead; regardless of the estimate, still a horrifying time. (The book Hungry Ghosts goes into detail on the struggles of this period.)

Food shortages got so bad that peasants resorted to eating sawdust, leather, and manure. In Sichuan Province, there were reports of peasants cooking the soil itself—actual dirt. Of course, dogs, cats, mice, insects—all were fair game as malnutrition spread throughout China. It got so bad that some resorted to cannibalism as their family and neighbors died all around them. Parents killed their own children as an act of mercy. There are reports that after they killed them, they ate their own children. This was life in Mao’s China.

Chairman Mao, in his quest for a communist utopia where everything was equal under heaven, in his quest to right the wrongs of a capitalist right-wing and imperialist society, forced human beings to kill their kids and eat their friends. This was the most horrific humanitarian crisis to ever occur in peacetime in human history. And it was done by design. Peak unhumanity. And yet, as with the Buddhist and Confucian philosophies that seemingly underpinned Mao’s strength-through-suffering orientation, there was metaphysical precedent for the starving of China. Ancient Taoist texts tell of the xiān, the immortals who have ascended from manhood to godhood. Some lived quiet lives of enlightened hermits, others wielded great supernatural powers, depending on which story you read. There’s one we find to be particularly … enlightening. From the third-century BC book of Zhuangzi, there is a parable telling of a conversation between the characters “Cloud Chief” and “Big Concealment.”5 Notice the instruction given to achieve a state of enlightenment:

Cloud Chief said, “Heavenly Master, it has been hard indeed for me to meet with you—I beg one word of instruction!”

“Well, then—mind-nourishment!” said Big Concealment. “You have only to rest in inaction and things will transform themselves. Smash your form and body, spit out hearing and eyesight, forget you are a thing among other things, and you may join in great unity with the deep and boundless. Undo the mind, slough off spirit, be blank and soulless, and the ten thousand things one by one will return to the root—return to the root and not know why. Dark and undifferentiated chaos—to the end of life none will depart from it. But if you try to know it, you have already departed from it. Do not ask what its name is, do not try to observe its form. Things will live naturally end of themselves.”6

We do not have reason to believe Mao Zedong knew this parable and had it in mind while planning the Great Leap Forward. And yet the “inaction” of China’s agriculture industry, following the great smelting … the “smashing” and “spitting out” of all CCP enemies … the great “forgetting” that would occur in the Cultural Revolution (more on this shortly) … and the “undoing,” “sloughing off,” and “becoming blank and soulless.” Are we stretching an old parable about the difficulty of winning esoteric wisdom? Or are we acknowledging the ancient spiritual heritage that Mao uniquely brought to the physical plane? Was Maoism the union of Buddhism, Confucianism, and Taoism, given a vehicle in the form of sociopolitical revolution, and brought to life in the real world? These might be questions for another book.

Regardless, as you might imagine, all was not well in Mao’s house amid the destruction his plans had wrought. Senior leaders of the Chinese Communist Party, such as Deng Xiaoping and Liu Shaoqi, realized a new plan might be in order. They asked Mao to consider economic reforms to improve the situation and mitigate the mass starvation. Initially, Chairman Mao agreed. But when rumors of a change in policy spread, there was joy. The people were won over. Mao didn’t like that. Not. One. Bit. No one could usurp his connection with the people. And yet he had a bigger problem—for the people who had lived through the Great Leap Forward, the people who had endured the years of famine, they all knew who was to blame. Chairman Mao came up with a solution for his problem, one that would go down in infamy. He decided that Deng Xiaoping, Liu Shaoqi, Lin Biao, and any other Party members who opposed him, had to be purged.

The Cultural Revolution

Mao understood his own party leaders were coming for him after the disaster of the Great Leap Forward. So, he did the only thing he knew how to do well, he launched another revolution within his own revolution. In 1966, Chairman Mao mobilized the youth of China to launch a second communist revolution, this one against his own party. He held massive speeches everywhere, summoning China’s young people to attend and hear him live. These young cadres, called the Red Guards, were formed everywhere in the country, from the remote deserts in the west to the great cities in the south and east.

Mao only asked for two things: unquestioned loyalty to himself and unquestioned loyalty to the revolution. With these marching orders, the Red Guards got to work. The Red Guards would march up and down the streets, going onto buses and holding up Mao’s Little Red Book. The Red Guards would ask you to make sure you always had your copy of the Little Red Book on you. To make sure you’re reading it, they’d ask you to quote it. Fail to do so, and you’d be pulled off the bus for your struggle session. If you were accused of anti-revolutionary thought at school or at work, you were persecuted. One such persecution is depicted at the beginning of the Netflix series The Three-Body Problem, adapted from the Chinese novel of the same name. A professor is humiliated before a crowd of young Red Guards, all waving the Little Red Book, as his wife betrays him, and his daughter watches as he is beaten to death to conclude his struggle session. The professor’s crime was to teach non-atheistic science in the classroom.7 This is what they do.

During these struggle sessions, victims would have placards put around their necks and be forced to self-incriminate in public spectacle, surrounded by hundreds. They would be spat upon, they would have things thrown at them, and in some cases, they would be killed outright. Teachers and other people targeted by these Red Guards would throw themselves out of buildings and kill themselves rather than face a struggle session—or cannibalism.

Yes, the Red Guards took the leftist slogan “eat the rich” serious. As many as 421 people were killed and eaten by Chinese communists even though there was no famine this time around. Victims included professors, landlords, secret Chiang supporters, and as per usual, the wrong kind of communists. Murder and cannibalism sometimes took place with great fanfare and celebration at country fairs, with tens of thousands of people joining in the consumption of human flesh.8 This is what they do.

The Red Guards soon became too powerful for anyone to control, and the cult of Mao was so powerful that everyone had to go along simply to get along. Churches at this point were completely destroyed. Anything foreign was attacked. You had to prove your worth—your revolutionary fervor for the Great Helmsman.

Old emperors were dug up, their bodies desecrated. Graveyards were smashed. Statues were destroyed. All enemies of Chairman Mao were targeted for destruction. Even Lin Biao, a loyal officer of Mao’s Red Army during the civil war, now Minister of Defense, was a target. It turned out that Lin Biao may have been having secret discussions with the Nationalists in Taiwan to try to do something about Mao.

Mao found out what was going on but not before Lin Biao escaped with his entire family by aircraft. Taking off from Hubei Province, they were supposed to land in the Soviet Union, but the Soviets didn’t want issues with Mao. Thus, they tried to make it to Taiwan, but the plane ran out of fuel and crashed in Mongolia. Some say it was shot down. Everyone on board died.

Mao lost his mind and summarily accused anyone and everyone of secretly plotting against him. Much of this paranoia led to the famed Sino-Soviet split in the communist world, which the Americans exploited during the Cold War. It was in these final years that President Richard Nixon and his Secretary of State Henry Kissinger paid China a visit, the first Americans to step foot in the country since World War II. This was 1972.

In 1976, Mao Zedong finally died. His body was embalmed and set on display in Tiananmen Square where it resides to this day. Though the Cultural Revolution died with Mao, the People’s Republic of China did not. Soon after, Deng Xiaoping would take center stage and bring much-needed economic reform to China, sowing the seeds for its later economic dynamism, particularly when globalist investors, entrepreneurs, and corporations would use labor arbitrage to cut the cost of goods sold, destroying the American Dream for millions as a result. Deng later ordered the Tiananmen Square Massacre in 1989. We could write an entirely separate book on this one consequence of China’s communist revolution—that it made a nation’s workforce available for hire at pennies on the US dollar. We would argue that the peasantry of China, urban or rural, needed the United States presence not in 1972 or during the rapid industrialization of the ’90s and 2000s, during which “Made in China” replaced “Made in USA” for almost all consumer products. The good people of China called for aid in the 1940s. Some military assistance was prepared to be sure. Not enough to turn the red tide. This was known at the time, and it was allowed to happen.

For communists, the ends justify the means. Millions must be shot for there to be one shot at utopia. This is what they do. And the West knew it.

In 1770, British statesman Edmund Burke wrote, foreshadowing Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn:

When bad men combine, the good must associate; else they will fall, one by one, an unpitied sacrifice in a contemptible struggle.9

We’d argue that the sacrifices made in the struggle against the forces of unhumanity are never contemptible. What we do hold in contempt are the otherwise good men who allow evil to triumph simply by doing nothing. That’s the story of China’s communist revolutions.

Perhaps the next time there is a Generalissimo Chiang Kai-shek, we will rally around him, give him everything he needs, and crush the revolution before it can even occur. Perhaps.

Or the unhumans will seize everything, purge the past, and eat the rich—literally.

This is what they do.

Death Toll of the Chinese Communist Revolution: 50 million

cYF

CHAPTER 9

NEW AMERICAN FOUNDING: THE UNTOLD STORY OF CIVIL RIGHTS AND CULTURAL MARXISM

Think of the 1960s. Right now.

You’re probably having some rapid-fire thoughts. The Beatles. Andy Griffith. Hippies. Rock concerts. Vietnam. The Cold War. Assassinations. The moon landing.

Stark social changes might come to mind, too, ones that made and unmade the American social fabric. Like the pill. Or no more Christian prayer in the public school. White flight from cities to suburbs. The Civil Rights Movement. Of course, we don’t see that last one like the others. Civil Rights meant freedoms were expanded and uptight social codes were weakened or abandoned. Perennial wrongs were finally righted. Much changed in the ’60s, much for the better.

Now talk to the left. Activist, radical, feminist, reefer enthusiast. Take your pick. From that side, you’ll hear a very different take on the 1960s. They’ll speak of the period the way a small-town Bible belt meemaw thinks of 1776. To the left, the Civil Rights era was the new American founding—a successful revolution that superseded the first one that got so much wrong. The legacy of the 1960s was thus the necessary overthrow of an oppressive old order.

And yet something conservatives fail to understand, like many things, is that this left-wing history is actually more accurate than that of the right—the conservative interpretation of the ’60s sociopolitical transformation being some vague sense of freedom and youth protest and that march to Selma. Or was it “on” Selma? “From” Selma? Most on the right don’t know, don’t care. Not relevant.

And yet it is. The narrative of the ’60s known by roughly half of American citizens is shamefully incomplete. We understand how that ignorance came to be, so we don’t fault them—or you—if you don’t know the true story of that period. Most of us learned about the 1960s in social studies class, when we were assigned books to read and essays to write about the plight and progress of, among other populations, African Americans. The entire history of 1865 to the 1960s was sloppily abridged for us in school, then crammed into a few weeks. There was slavery, racism, Jim Crow laws, lynchings, more racism, the burning of Black Wall Street, then Rosa Parks and Martin Luther King Jr. sitting down and standing up (respectively) for African American civil rights. There were peaceful protests, virtuous speeches, and dirty white policemen. Dr. King changed millions of minds, we learned, from one line of one speech, then some evil racist shot him dead. And so he became a martyr, all America was inspired by his example, and we swept away all discrimination to usher in a modern democratic society. Right?

This is all so simple children can understand it—and that’s the point (note also the similarity to the Gospel of Jesus Christ). The Civil Rights era was the first moral fable embedded into actual American history education. This version of the past also shines a floodlight of shame on everything else we learned about American history prior, stretching all the way back to the beginning. We were told about the War of Independence and the fight against British tyranny. But we were also told that the Founding Fathers doing that fighting were racists and slaveowners—and by the way the British actually outlawed slavery and ended segregation decades before the US did, so maybe King George III was the good guy, actually.

Education has consequences. Fast-forward the children who learn this, and what do we see during their adulthood? Icons of early America are torn down across the country, hidden away, or melted into ore. Kids who took the simpleton civil rights story seriously came to see American history as oppressor versus oppressed in all things. They grew into radicals. And so disfiguring and destroying historic statues is one of numerous obvious outcomes.

Since the “pre-’60s America bad, post-’60s America good” narrative is embedded into the Western psyche early on, everything else we learn about American history is built onto this structure, both in school and in adulthood. The Civil Rights Movement is taken to be the most straightforwardly ethical occurrence in American history— thus, anything attached to it is inherently good or easily justified. This interpretation oversimplifies a complex event that was also in many ways destructive, especially to those we are to believe it helped most. We cannot undo that legacy of harm or put an end to the perma-revolution the 1960s brought us if we falsely believe it was all a sacred moral crusade.

It was not.

This is the untold story of the new American founding—it was, in fact, the Civil Rights Revolution.

The Real Civil Rights Legacy

Martin Luther King Jr. is the central figure in the popular retelling of the ’60s. He is praised and worshipped as a peaceful activist, and he did indeed promote peaceful activism similar to Gandhi, whom MLK referred to as his “guiding light” (Dr. King visited India in 1959.)1 And yet what non-leftists forget (or were never told) is that MLK’s nonviolent resistance was not the sum total of the Civil Rights Movement. In fact, in the mid to late ’60s, civil rights activism was often accompanied by those it claimed to help, with that activism many times devolving into violence (by black activists), resegregation (also by black activists), and new racism (again by black activists).

Massive fiery riots blew up major cities across America; for example, following the 1967 armed uprising in Newark, New Jersey. Tens of thousands of whites fled, never to return, as they were marked as targets by radicals based solely on their skin color. They were white. Had this happened in some faraway country, we’d call it ethnic cleansing. In Newark and in other communities affected by this era’s riots, the urban middle class was hollowed out entirely, both black and white, leaving only an underclass of all races surrounding an upper class who could afford maximum-security dwellings amid and above the squalor left by a violent revolution.

Drive through any US city today to see exactly what we mean; read Left behind in Rosedale by Scott Cummings to learn how it got that way. Crime rose, government programs came in, and peaceful residents who had the means fled.

This happened in thousands of cities and towns all over America. Millions fled their homes and communities. Most of those cities and towns never recovered. All those interconnected urban villages were forcibly smashed. Families that had lived together for one hundred years were split apart, most never to meet again.

What the left calls “White Flight” was really state-backed ethnic cleansing.

And it was all done by design.

We can’t help but see a parallel with the kulaks, middle-class Russians completely cleaned out by Bolshevik policy and practice. Deathly poor peasantry replaced them, as Soviet leadership dined on the finest meats and wine. In America, the black family structure was left for destruction, as the infamous Moynihan Report would reveal.

The Moynihan Report, officially titled “The Negro Family: The Case for National Action,” was a controversial government report authored by Daniel Patrick Moynihan, an American sociologist and later a United States Senator. Moynihan, who was an assistant secretary in the US Department of Labor at the time, wrote the report in 1965, under President Lyndon Johnson.

After acknowledging that the struggle for civil rights going back to the founding of the United States finally enabled black Americans to “reach the highest peaks of achievement” as individuals, the Moynihan Report offers this lament:

The most difficult fact for white Americans to understand is that in these terms the circumstances of the Negro American community in recent years has probably been getting worse, not better.

Indices of dollars of income, standards of living, and years of education deceive. The gap between the Negro and most other groups in American society is widening.

The fundamental problem, in which this is most clearly the case, is that of family structure. The evidence—not final, but powerfully persuasive—is that the Negro family in the urban ghettos is crumbling. A middle-class group has managed to save itself, but for vast numbers of the unskilled, poorly educated city working class the fabric of conventional social relationships has all but disintegrated. There are indications that the situation may have been arrested in the past few years, but the general post-war trend is unmistakable. So long as this situation persists, the cycle of poverty and disadvantage will continue to repeat itself.2

Persist and worsen it did, as if by design. Remember, The Communist Manifesto demands the elimination of marriage as an institution with a “community of women” shared by uncommitted men as its replacement.3 And that’s exactly what happened among the working poor, white, yes, as well as other races, but particularly and specifically black families. This was (and is) Cultural Marxism. It was not an accident, it was an agenda.

But we digress. The point we make on urban destruction is that it was a widespread and violent phenomenon in the 1960s. Adding to Newark, there were also the 1964 Harlem riots, the 1965 Watts riot in Los Angeles, the 1965 Detroit riot, and the riots in Washington, DC, following the assassination of Martin Luther King Jr. in 1968. There were also bomb threats and actual bombings—terrorism—all for the cause. The 1969 Days of Rage in Chicago, organized by actual Marxist militants calling themselves the Weather Underground, come to mind, though the Weather Underground would continue its activity into the 1970s. State and local officials demanded overwhelming police presence to crush the uprisings and arrest the criminal elements among all anti-war and pro-black protesters. This was to everyone’s benefit, black and white, veteran and civilian. Previous chapters showed us what happens when open insurrection is allowed to exist and persist; openly violent Marxist factions were deemed domestic terrorists and watched closely by the intelligence community for many years to come.

Revolutionaries seek opportunity not only on the street with anarchy and bloodshed but also through sneakier tactics such as lawfare. A popular yet false belief many have about the 1960s Civil Rights period is that the iconic “I Have a Dream” speech encapsulates what all activists desired to achieve. This ties in with the mythology surrounding MLK. A myth to be sure but an understandable one. Already it is a myth forgotten by the far left. Did you know that MLK’s most famous quote—“I have a dream that in the future my children will be judged on the content of their character and not the color of their skin”—is not included in his memorial in Washington, DC? There’s been a bait-and-switch. The Civil Rights Movement asked for equality and got the laws to make it happen. But race hustlers moved in to inflame black rage and keep them down to keep the money coming. Thus, already-achieved equality under the law must be kept at a perceived generational distance away so there is always something to agitate for, something to get funding for. We’ll explain.

As we do so, consider the little word that keeps coming up in this book: “equality.” In every revolution, equality is spoken of in word but is never with deed. The oppressed class, organized and motivated, do not seek to be peers of the oppressor class. They want to take their stuff; they want to reverse their previous roles by any means available to them. The 1964 Civil Rights Act was one such way. President Lyndon Johnson signed into law the illegality of discrimination based on race, but under pressure from left-wing activist groups, that law has been reinterpreted ever since to allow for weaponized discrimination against European, Jewish, and Asian Americans, reframing this direct discrimination as “affirmative action” to “offset” past injustice. Out of this reframe was born the legal doctrine of “disparate impact,” which states that if a policy at any company, organization, or institution has an unequal outcome based on race, sex, or national origin versus what would be predicted by overall population percentages, we must assume the policy is discriminatory and must be abolished—or else, lawsuits, bad press, boycotts, and protests. Lawfare. The permanent enshrinement of racial awareness in federal law.

Of course, it does not take a genius to understand that all of life has “disparate impact.” Human beings are different, both within groups and between them. And yet now we are stuck with legal precedent that says you can’t allow reality. It is unfair and must be altered. You may not discriminate—unless it is demanded of you. Only the activists who’ve infiltrated the bureaucracy may decide which is which, or rather “who” is “whom.”

Lawfare and the threat of it—a hand-me-down of the Civil Rights Movement—has resulted in mandatory doublethink in modern life. We’re told our civilization has made great advances far and away from past racism, which we do not deny, and yet we have reinstitutionalized racism and upheld its legality, as long as it is done against individuals deemed members of historically oppressive groups. Even that is only theory. In practice, overachieving racial minorities in the US are held to a higher standard in Ivy League admissions, higher than both the majority-white student population and others. Discrimination against the most successful is policy; it’s constitutional. Holding no-whites-allowed rallies and meetings on campuses and in government offices across America, also constitutional. Allowing anti-Semitic propaganda and open violence in the name of resistance to alleged oppression on the other side of the world, constitutional. This is the final form of the left-wing Civil Rights Revolution—everything is inverted into the opposite of what it was purported to be. What was meant to move us to treat one another as humans has mutated into something unhuman. And as a result, the new constitution that is the Civil Rights Act has undone pertinent sections of the first. We do not expect this situation to be corrected for a reason we’ll explain shortly (a reason that may already be obvious to some of you reading this).

The Declaration of Independence says that “all men are created equal.” We may be identical in humanity, similar in capability, and in equal protection under the law. But obviously this does not mean that all men are created equal, shall remain equal, and must all die equal. Again, little kids get this. Consider how we treat athletics. Perhaps you already have, anticipating our easy dunk on the radicals (pun intended). Sports is one of the last areas of American society that remains something like a meritocracy, where only the ability to perform matters. Outcomes are disparate, as are incomes, because both are based on ability. This reality blocks left-wing pseudotheories about disparate outcomes due to privilege or oppression. It’s unfair, and so is life. Radicals cannot have that. Expect to see sports come under attack in the very near future. Protests, lawfare, even violence.

We’ve now gotten one thing straight: The ’60s didn’t happen without legitimate grievance driving great and necessary change. People wanted to abolish Jim Crow—an understandable desire, considering how much pain and suffering legalized racial discrimination and “separate but equal” segregation caused. But as the decade wore on, many young radicals got angry that sweeping away Jim Crow laws in 1964 did not immediately eradicate all causes (or consequences) of their grievances. And where youths were gathered, they made their voices heard. That was the college campus.

What Happens on Campus Goes Everywhere

In 1970 at Dartmouth, a group of Black Panther radicals took over a building called Cutter Hall, then renamed it the El-Hajj Al-Malik El-Shabazz Center, after the Islamic name of black nationalist Malcom X. Cutter Hall remained occupied for months on end, as the administration would not kick them out. The protesting students demanded a Black Studies Department, which had not existed previously (neither did any form of what we now know as “grievance studies”). The administration’s inaction either way spurred on occupation of more campus buildings, along with huge swaths of public space, all claimed by the Black Panthers. Some critics at the time predicted that surrendering to these demands at Dartmouth—and to activists at other universities making similar threats to staff—would result in the nationwide establishment of fake academic departments producing fake scholarship. Radicals and the generations after them would take all these classes because they would be the only ones they could pass. And so would begin the permanent ghettoization of the university system. But in the age of affirmative action, such talk was dismissed as racist, naturally. No such thing would or could happen. These disadvantaged students needed a leg up. Help them fit in better. It’s only fair.

Fast-forward to today. Every college has two, three, or even ten of these fake departments running grievance studies courses that actively disprepare students for productive careers and meaningful lives in the modern world. Young people admitted to these schools via affirmative action admissions policies do not fare well in the usual academic classes. Instead, they excel (if you could call it that) in grievance studies, graduate, and become full-time activists, operating in state and federal governments, in media and entertainment, in the NGOs that suck up billions in funding and philanthropy per year doing God knows what, and—of course—back in the education system. Play your cards right, and you can become the next president of Harvard University.

Boom and Doom

Of course, people of color were not the only ones behind the activism of the 1960s. There was another driving force alongside them, one with more economic and social heft: the white American. A racial revolution was joined by a social revolution, led by the baby boomers. For decades, the baby boomers were the largest, most significant demographic in America, so they were the group treated most favorably politically. When the boomers were in their twenties, everything in American life was oriented toward boosting young people so they could succeed. This explains the dramatic expansion of college availability. The ’60s was the first period in American history where anyone who wanted to go to college could. When boomers tell you they paid for their tuition with a part-time summer job, they’re telling the truth. College was cheap in those days.

The advent of oral contraceptive technology in 1960 made the concurrent sexual revolution possible, separating sexual activity from procreation. The era of “free love” cut the boomers off from the moral codes of their forebears. This fueled the burgeoning second-wave feminist movement, which declared that women have rights not just in terms of employment but sexual behavior as well; there were to be no more double standards, and the consequence of free sex (unwanted children) could and should be avoided. These were radical breaks with what came before. For context, first-wave feminism refers to the early-twentieth century granting of voting, property, and other rights to women—equality under the law. The second wave in the ’60s said, in effect, No, women and men are more than equal; they are the same. Or they should be. If they’re not, they must be made to be. The feminization of men and masculinization of women we observe in popular culture today is an experiment begun in the ’60s as part of this equity initiative.

How did we get so much radicalism so soon? Consider the conjunction of influences in 1960s America in the same geographic locations. The campus explains it all:

The sizes and number of universities expanded to fit a growing population of young people. These universities needed to be staffed, so faculty were hired at an unprecedented rate as well. Tuition would necessarily increase in the subsequent decades to fund new and additional hires.

The Vietnam War raged at the time, and college attendance was the most accessible path for disgruntled young people to dodge the draft and avoid the risk of death in a faraway jungle. (Even though enrolled college students were not subject to the Vietnam-era draft, they protested anyway.)

Left-leaning individuals were already inclined to enter academia, and the hiring environment in those days was hospitable to PhDs who sought tenure. Furthermore, universities in that time tended to let go of professors who were too old, so many old positions were opened up. This allowed radicals to swarm the academy at all levels, leading to an academic culture that was and is left-wing through and through. In many university departments today, more than 90 percent of all faculty are not just liberal but identify with far-left views. The roots of today’s campus radicalism lie in the ’60s because that era’s radicals were in the right place at the right time.

The baby boomers came of age amid social chaos not seen by any prior generation of Americans. With the country’s moral superstructures torn down, young people could do whatever they wanted, however they wanted, with the justification they were overthrowing an evil system. That said, structural racism was a real grievance, and that period marked the greatest deconstruction of it in American history. So naturally, public figures of the baby boomer generation take credit for the Civil Rights Movement. But civil rights activism was not new.

Civil Rights before the Civil Rights Movement

Civil rights activism had been a mainstay of American history for decades prior to the 1960s, even centuries. The abolitionist movement was a powerful social force in the run-up to the American Civil War. Conscientious and compassionate figures in the 1920s called for lynching bans. The famous Brown v. Board of Education Supreme Court case that desegregated public schools was decided in 1954— after about half the country had already abolished school segregation. Jackie Robinson drove the integration of Major League Baseball, but the state of California, with its many anti-segregation laws, forced the NFL to desegregate, too, because ownership wanted to put a team in California. By the end of the 1950s, segregation had been eliminated, outside of hardline places like the Deep South, where resentment over what they call the War between the States was still palpable. However, it is still common to portray pre-’60s America as some racist hellhole that the radicals had to rescue us from. This is slander.

We do agree: Equal treatment under the law is good. Actual color blindness under the law is a goal that should be championed. This is obvious to us now, but also to good, smart people of our distant past. As a civilization, we were already well underway in the accomplishment of that vision by the 1960s, a vision that is a thoroughly Christian one. It comes as no shock that antislavery activists were Christian, as were the countless leaders and supporters of desegregation and other efforts to deconstruct actual systems of oppression. Saint Paul the apostle in Galatians 3:28–29 wrote the religious (and timeless) justification for Jefferson’s words on the equality of all men under the law:

There is neither Jew nor Greek, there is neither slave nor free, there is neither male nor female; for you are all one in Christ Jesus.4

But as progress continued into the 1960s, we began to see radicals turn reactionary—and stir up violent and ongoing antiprogress through Cultural Marxism. And so just as society had begun to see the dawning of a day when we treat one another as human beings of equal worth in God’s sight, our sunrise was eclipsed unexpectedly. The US Supreme Court removed God from the public school system. The universal Western mythos of Jesus Christ began to be forgotten. Unity of all in a national melting pot was replaced with hyperconsciousness of race and difference, slowly then all at once. Academia, through their grievance studies, then the federal bureaucracy, hiring graduates indoctrinated into those studies, began to “communize” the races of America. That is, it became policy to treat people not as individuals with inherent dignity, inalienable rights, and altogether unique life experiences, but to treat individuals as representative of their groups and either guilty of oppression or a victim of it, as decided by the entire collective history of the group. Sounds a lot like haves versus have-nots, doesn’t it? It’s Cultural Marxism is what it is. And like Marxist ideology proper, there isn’t much logic to it. For example, in the wake of Civil Rights, the US Census Bureau created absurd identities like “Hispanic,” as if an African-descended Dominican and an Argentine with German ancestry have some ethnic or racial similarity. Since “Hispanic” is considered non-white, we have an absurd scenario where blond-haired, blue-eyed Puerto Ricans and Mexicans are considered the same ethnicity as non-blonde-haired, non-blue-eyed Puerto Ricans simply because they share the same nationality. You don’t speak English. You probably can’t figure out how to legally immigrate. You probably don’t have any skills or prospects. You must be oppressed! By white people! In the leftist worldview, racism and bigotry are laundered into acceptable discourse with all individual differences, abilities, and achievements dismissed and replaced with cartoonish misinterpretations of group differences, disparities between them, and with have and have-not labels assigned by fiat. And keep in mind that the only commonality between all peoples in our Hispanic example is that they speak Spanish as a mother tongue.

The absurdities of the Census Bureau and the leftist assignment of oppressor and oppressed categories are classic communist OPE. But the reason for them in this story is a uniquely American one: Follow the money. It pays to be oppressed in the age of civil rights. If you can present yourself as aggrieved, you may agitate for special treatment under civil rights law. For example, people from the Indian subcontinent, as well as nearby groups like the Pakistanis and Bangladeshis, used to be classed in the US as “Caucasian.” But activists, following the money, pushed for Indians, Pakistanis, Bangladeshis, and more to be considered Asian to be able to apply for special grants available to disadvantaged minorities. The law is the law, so they’re still eligible for free money, despite those groups boasting the highest income of any in the US.

Among black Americans, we’re noticing African immigrants leapfrog the descendants of slaves academically and economically—the very people those affirmative action laws were meant to help. African immigrants and their children use pathways meant to settle historical injustices done to the descendants of slaves—and sometimes, the two groups clash on campus. Make it make sense; we sure can’t.

We haven’t been ready for these conversations because we haven’t been ready to challenge the legacy of the 1960s.

It’s time.

They Fought “The Man,” Then Became Him

The 2020 George Floyd protests gave modern Americans a brief taste of the turmoil that was typical of the 1960s, but the influence of that decade wasn’t limited to large demonstrations. Soft cultural revolutions of this sort have happened in all the decades since, but we haven’t heard those opposed to general “wokeness” or Critical Race Theory specifically talk about it. Instead, those who do address the numerous unwanted aftereffects of the 1960s point out specific policies to be undone or issues to be addressed. And yet in doing so, they still operate within the moral frame set by the social revolutions of the ’60s.

As for leftists themselves, they act like they’re perpetually stuck in the ’60s, claiming they oppose “the man” as always they have. In truth, the far left has been in charge of entire cities and states for the entire duration of a human lifetime and have made either no progress or taken everyone backward. They might propose one reform or another to fight alleged systemic racism in the public school system, but the left has been running the public schools in Los Angeles, New York, and Boston for decades. No conservative or moderate has done anything of any importance in any major American city for decades, education or otherwise, and yet they still take all the blame. This shows how pervasive left-wing control of the social narrative is—and may continue to be for a long time. At the end of it all, they demand more laws, more discrimination against whites/Jews/Asians, and of course … more money. A lot more money. The majority-black schools in major cities run by leftists that receive the most money perform the poorest. They must need more money. And fewer white people.

Consider The Bonfire of the Vanities by Thomas Wolfe, a novel addressing social issues in 1980s New York. Spoiler alert, if you haven’t read it: An investment banker accidentally hits an honor student in the Bronx, sending the student into a coma. The accident sets off a Trayvon Martin–like controversy, blowing up the banker’s life with misery and scandal. Political opportunists descend on him, including an Al Sharpton–like figure before Al Sharpton became famous. The novel was published in 1987 yet reads like it was written today.

This is cyclical. We’ll see a surge of left-wing activism as if no one has tried this equality thing before, they’ll win concessions from the government, they’ll get paid, everything gets worse, they come back and blame “the man” for stalling their progress, and the cycle starts anew. There is no self-reflection, only a desire to blame someone else—and get paid. Always get paid.

Vacuuming money from the system has been the semi-secret of activism since the 1960s. You might remember from history class mention made of the “Cloward-Piven Strategy.” The activists and academics (we repeat ourselves) Richard Cloward and Frances Fox Piven devised this scheme in 1966. Their stated plan was to “end poverty” through what’s now called Universal Basic Income. “The right to income must be guaranteed, or the oppression of the welfare poor will not be eliminated,” they wrote.5 The lofty explicit was not met, of course, but equality was achieved—now millions of blacks, whites, and many other races are equally poor and stuck subsisting from the welfare state generation after generation for more than fifty years now. We’re not fans of Wikipedia, but their contributors write this helpful summary of Cloward-Piven’s impact, which was enacted by their progressive allies in the Democratic party:

In papers published in 1971 and 1977, Cloward and Piven argued that mass unrest in the United States, especially between 1964 and 1969, did lead to a massive expansion of welfare rolls, though not to the guaranteed-income program that they had hoped for.6

At least bureaucrats were able to take from the haves and redistribute to the have-nots. Federal welfare recipients more than doubled from 4.7 million in 1966 to 9.7 million in 1970.7 Scholar John McWhorter has written extensively on the financial and familial destruction the 1960s welfare state brought to the community it purported to help the most—black America. From NPR, of all places:

In the late 1960s and early 1970s, [McWhorter] says, welfare became “a program that had no time limit,” a situation that “brought out the worst in human—not black—but human nature.”

“Welfare has ruined communities of other color too,” McWhorter points out.8

Cultural Marxists. This is what they do.

So why hasn’t the madness been stopped? We’re going on more than half a century now. Well, we suggest the reason why is what happens if you try—you get canceled. Speak or write openly and honestly about the excesses of the Civil Rights period and the extremes they left us with, and your livelihood is imperiled. Here comes that lawfare. That is at worst. At best, you’ll lose out on social and professional opportunities. Consider why so few American kulaks counter-protested the billions of dollars of unnecessary destruction done in the name of George Floyd in 2020, or the number of lives lost during those riots. If you disagreed with the robbing and killing (there these two are again), you were accused of putting property over people. To disagree with violent uprising, to the revolutionary, is to issue tacit approval of whatever they are protesting. Further, to acknowledge that the legacy of the Civil Rights Act has been the usurpation of the constitution ratified in 1788 will result in what? What will you be accused of? Oh, so you oppose civil rights? OK, klansman. It’s all so tiresome. We got here because the 1960s went there.

Make no mistake: The ’60s were a revolution in every sense of the word. Millions fled cities and towns. What occurred may not have been as destructive as the French or Russian Revolutions, but this social revolution in the ’60s was moved by the same spirit: Whatever came before must be destroyed. The old ways must go. There must be equality not in opportunity but in outcome. If there is not, it will be made so by force.

Realize that the only counterforce to radicalism in the 1960s that turned our revolution into a protracted culture war is our economic productivity. We are so rich we can run social engineering experiments. And afford them we did, because we solved the ’60s by spending our way out of them. We could, then and now, give activists everything they want because we can afford to. But when the money runs out, watch out. The cold war of Cultural Marxism will turn hot quick.

Who could have seen this all coming? One man did.

McCarthy Was Right

Senator Joseph McCarthy witnessed the diffusion of communism theory and practice into Western institutions, from entertainment to government, and for that he was cast as an anti-Semitic villain, as so many of the communists and communist sympathizers he outed (and ousted) were unrelatedly Jewish or secretly homosexual, with the latter being obvious blackmail targets at that time in US history. However, McCarthy also had Jewish investigators on his committee, and these accusations were unfounded. Thus, McCarthy’s efforts to protect us all were rebranded as a conspiratorial “Red Scare.” They were not. Nor were they partisan, as one of McCarthy’s own counsels was a young lawyer, Robert F. Kennedy, Sr. from a powerful Democrat family, his brother John having been just elected to the Senate himself. The Kennedys were staunch anti-communists and considered fellow Irish American Joseph McCarthy a family friend. McCarthy even reportedly was romantically involved with two of the Kennedy sisters.

Recall that communist history is written by the losers; McCarthy is reviled because his methods were effective. McCarthy terrifies the cultural Marxists, even to this very day. In February 1950, a full decade before the events of this chapter, Senator McCarthy announced he had compiled a secret list of “members of the Communist Party and members of a spy ring” in the State Department.9 It didn’t end with the government. McCarthy’s public and private hunt for Soviet sympathizers and far-left radicals led him to the U.S. Army, education, organized labor, and the entertainment industry. Mass media was already known for harboring aspiring revolutionaries, as the House Un-American Activities Committee (HUAC) had already shown beginning in 1947.

Believe it or not, even the great Walt Disney himself testified to HUAC during the so-called Red Scare. Yes, Walt Disney was an avowed anti-communist who warned communists were infiltrating Hollywood:

Investigator: “What is your personal opinion of the Communist Party, Mr Disney, as to whether or not it is a political party?”

Disney: “Well, I don’t believe it is a political party. I believe it is an un-American thing. The thing that I resent the most is that they are able to get into these unions, take them over, and represent to the world that a group of people that are in my plant, that I know are good, 100 per cent Americans, are trapped by this group, and they are represented to the world as supporting all of those ideologies, and it is not so, and I feel that they really ought to be smoked out and shown up for what they are, so that all of the good, free causes in this country, all the liberalisms that really are American, can go out without the taint of communism. That is my sincere feeling on it.”

Investigator: “Do you feel that there is a threat of Communism in the motion picture industry?”

Disney: “Yes there is, and there are many reasons why they would like to take it over or get in and control it or disrupt it, but I don’t think they have gotten very far, and I think the industry is made up of good Americans, just like in my plant, good, solid Americans. My boys have been fighting it longer than I have. They are trying to get out from under it and they will in time if we can just show them up.”10

It wasn’t until the 1990s that Soviet records known as the Venona Intercepts would corroborate the good senator’s claims. Of course, you probably never even heard of the Venona Intercepts:

The materials that first made their way to the surface in the early 1990s—records from Moscow’s Russian Center for the Preservation and Study of Documents of Recent History— provided proof past peradventure that the Communist Party of the United States was subsidized by the Soviet government and used as a base for extensive espionage …

In the 1940s, the NSA had a top-secret program called Venona which intercepted (and much later decoded) messages between Moscow and its American agents. The recent publication of a batch of Venona transcripts gives evidence that the Roosevelt and Truman administrations were rife with communist spies and political operatives who reported, directly or indirectly, to the Soviet government, much as their anti-communist opponents charged. The Age of McCarthyism, it turns out, was not the simple witch hunt of the innocent by the malevolent as two generations of high school and college students have been taught.

The sum and substance of this growing body of material is that: Ethel and Julius Rosenberg, executed in June 1953 for atomic espionage, were guilty; Alger Hiss, a darling of the establishment was guilty; and that dozens of lesser known persons such as Victor Perlo, Judith Coplon and Harry Gold, whose innocence of the accusations made against them had been a tenet of leftist faith for decades, were traitors or, at the least, the ideological vassals of a foreign power.

Even moderate politicians who insisted upon the fact— and argued that these people might have influenced U.S. foreign policy—were scorned. Sen. Robert Taft of Ohio said, “The greatest Kremlin asset in our history has been the pro-communist group in the State Department who surrendered to every demand of Russia at Yalta and Potsdam, and promoted at every opportunity the communist cause in China until today communism threatens to take over all of Asia.”11

In fact, Robert Oppenheimer, the director of the Manhattan Project, considered the father of the atom bomb, was himself named in the Venona Intercepts. According to historian John Earl Haynes, who specialized in twentieth-century history at the Library of Congress with particular focus on communist infiltration, Oppenheimer was named in the intercepts and even had a series of code names from the KGB: Chester, Chemist, and Yew.12

Total and complete vindication. Everything makes sense now, doesn’t it? Including why “Joseph McCarthy is the most unjustly demonized individual in American history.”13

By 1954, his methods were deemed not nice, and he was censured—officially reprimanded—by the US Senate, bringing his work to an end. McCarthy was right; his fellow senators just didn’t like his tone. The infamous rebuke of McCarthy’s work came from a lawyer hired to defend the U.S. Army from allegations of subversive activities: “Have you no sense of decency?”14 As an immediate consequence of this question in 1950’s America, “McCarthy’s immense national popularity evaporated” and he was “ostracized by his party, and ignored by the press[.] McCarthy died three years later, 48 years old and a broken man.”15 He may not have been decent, but he was right. McCarthy understood that Western civilization was “a final, allout battle between communistic atheism and Christianity.”16 Winner takes all. Our only regret is he, like Pyrot Wrangel and Chiang Kaishek, was isolated from the support he needed, when he needed it most. Senator McCarthy’s chief counsel throughout the hearings, a man named Roy Cohn, would later go to spend over a decade working with a young New York real estate developer named Donald Trump.

In the following years, allegiance to communism and aspirations of Soviet influence in America morphed from a socioeconomic to a cultural Marxism, given the nation’s unprecedented postwar civilizational prosperity. Meanwhile, the infiltration into US institutions, especially Hollywood, as Walt Disney himself warned the nation, would continue into the 1960s—and beyond—until total ideological and legal capture was complete. McCarthy warned that this was happening and caught it in real time.

This is what they do.

Toll of 1960s Cultural Marxism: 1,000+ deaths, 4.5 million ethnically cleansed

c11J

CHAPTER 10

RED-HOT COLD WAR: THE UNTOLD STORY OF SOVIET-SPONSORED REVOLUTIONARY WARS

By this point, you get it. You know how the communist revolution plays out across time, place, and race. The details differ; the acts the story follows do not. Neither do the roles. It’s always some troupe of communists who want to ruin everything for everyone else, and a coalition of nationalists who … don’t want that. Once you understand the frame, you know what clash will play out.

And so if you were given a pop quiz on the various communist conflicts and America-Soviet proxy wars of the Cold War era, you’d probably get a passing grade, even if you didn’t know the exact dates, belligerents involved, or how precisely it all ended. What happened in France happened in Russia. What happened in Russia happened throughout the developing world before, during, and after World War II. But especially after World War II. Here’s why.

Unhumans: The First Globalists

The Soviet Union you know by now went about remaking the world in their image, much the way the so-called global American empire (the “GAE”) does today through cultural export, currency control, and of course, military might. The Soviet Union attempted the same, funding their multinational effort by looting the Russian treasury.

You probably know a few of these Soviet-backed takeovers well. Perhaps you even have close relatives who fought in them, survived them, or escaped them. Both of us certainly do. But what you may not know was how these various uprisings started, why they succeeded (or failed), and what they can teach us about the future of communism— a future that is already upon us, as we’ll reveal in Chapter 12.

The Kennan Containment Theory, proposed by American diplomat George F. Kennan, advocated for a strategic approach to countering the spread of Soviet influence and communism during the Cold War, which began after World War II ended in 1945. The theory focused on containing the Soviet Union within its existing borders through diplomatic, economic, and military measures, while supporting allies to create a united front against Soviet expansionism. Kennan emphasized patience and persistence, believing that the flawed Soviet system would eventually collapse under its own internal weaknesses. This theory guided US foreign policy throughout much of the Cold War, shaping initiatives such as the Marshall Plan and the Truman Doctrine.

The Soviet Union started a decades-long proxy war with the West following World War II, after having been a quasi-ally against Nazi Germany and briefly an enemy of Japan once that empire’s defeat became inevitable. There was of course the Greek Civil War, where Britain- and later US-backed forces fought communist rebels from 1944 to 1949. Greece survived, a free nation of free people, in what we consider a wrap-up conflict in the context of World War II, as British forces were already on the ground due to the southern European campaign against Nazi-allied Italy. But the Soviet Union didn’t provide much help to the communist rebels, at least not the help they needed, and so their defeat was all but inevitable given the superior firepower the West helped provide.1

Then of course there was the Korean War of 1950 through 1953, which saw Soviet- and later Chinese-backed northern communists battle southern nationalists. The war famously never resolved, and the border of isolated North Korea and industrialized South Korea remains at precisely where the fighting stopped. Chairman Mao’s son died in the fighting after China intervened. While this was a hot war, it did not follow the communist revolution model. Around 36,000 US military service members died in the Korean War. We honor their service in the fight against communism.

Let us give a brief word about Vietnam. The Vietnam War was a war about many issues, as all wars are. Certainly, anti-communism and containment were part of it. But that was not the only driving force. The war ended with 58,220 Americans dead and communist control of Vietnam. The Vietnam War began with a relatively bloodless revolution against the power vacuum of 1945. France couldn’t hold onto its colony because France had been decimated in World War II. While it is true that the Soviets of course supported the communists in Vietnam, and it became a major flashpoint of the Cold War, it did not follow the pure communist revolution model in that there was no real government to overthrow in the first place. What we now know about the Gulf of Tonkin incident, a false flag, throws much of the Vietnam War into new light. For the soldiers, we honor their service in the fight against communism.

The story of communist globalism unfolded elsewhere, particularly in Latin American, where there were many communist revolutions and attempts—humans versus unhumans. These have much to teach us that, yes, will be familiar, but will also feel prescient given the time and season in which you’re reading this book. Our fast-paced tour of Cold War communism begins now.

The Cuban Revolution (1953–1959)

Cuba under the dictatorship of Fulgencio Batista was a rich target for a Soviet-backed communist takeover thanks to its elite classes of business owners, government officials, military, police, and mobsters who operated as an aristocracy. Income disparity, yet again, as we always see. The government was seen by many of the people as corrupt, and although there was an upper middle class in Cuba, there was also widespread poverty. None of this made the current system look good, so the people of Cuba were primed to be conned with communist promises of better education, redistribution of land (of course), and the nationalization of business.

The uprising had a face and a name: Fidel Castro, a direct agent of the Soviet Union. He was imprisoned for his first attack on Batista’s regime on July 26, 1953. Upon release, he fled to Mexico and trained a guerrilla force. One of the fighters he trained was Che Guevara, the heartthrob of left-wing teen girls to this day. Castro called his tiny army the “26th of July Movement,” crammed eighty followers onto a yacht, and landed back in Cuba on December 2, 1956.

The war was renewed, and Castro won. When Ed Sullivan interviewed Castro shortly after the latter’s victory in January 1959, Sullivan compared him to George Washington. As if. Later that same day, January 11, 1959, Castro told interviewers on Face the Nation, questioning him about human rights, “I am not a communist at all. I will never be against any right.”2

But on December 2, 1961, in a speech honoring the fifth anniversary of his landing the Granma, Castro came clean.

I am a Marxist-Leninist, and I will be a Marxist-Leninist until the last days of my life. If we had paused to tell the people that we were Marxist-Leninists while we were on Pico Turquino and not yet strong, it is possible that we would never have been able to descend to the plains.3

You might be wondering how exactly Castro pulled off a Marxist takeover. Well, you’ve probably gathered it wasn’t too difficult. It did not take much for Castro to incite the Cuban people to revolution because Batista’s regime was seen as corrupt and cruel by a vast swath of the populace. This is how communists can win before the fight starts—by exacerbating the issues of disparity and inequality, real or feigned. Castro, like others, simply sold himself as a liberator to a naive populace who had no idea how much worse their lives would be under (new) communist oppressors. Poor Cubans went from bad to worse. Cubans with any money either fled, or were liquidated.

The KGB, the Soviet Union’s intelligence agency, played a role in supporting Fidel Castro’s revolutionary movement in Cuba by providing training, intelligence, and covert assistance to his forces. The KGB offered strategic guidance to Castro’s movement, helping them organize and execute guerrilla warfare tactics against the regime of Batista. Although the extent of the KGB’s involvement during Castro’s initial rise to power in 1959 remains somewhat ambiguous, the agency’s support enhanced Castro’s operational capabilities and bolstered his efforts to overthrow Batista. After Castro came to power, the KGB continued to provide intelligence and security assistance to the new Cuban government, strengthening ties between Cuba and the Soviet Union.

Che Guevara played key roles in both the seizing and purging phases of Castro’s takeover of Cuba. When it came to the seizing stage of Cuba’s revolutionary war, Che was the head of the National Institute of Agrarian Reform (INRA), in charge of implementing land reform under the Agrarian Reform Law of May 1959. The law limited landownership, took away the larger estates and granted ownership to the laborers who had worked the lands, nationalized (stole) foreign-owned land, and abolished the payment of rent for land. Che also supervised the nationalization of US-owned businesses in Cuba, including oil refineries, factories, and casinos.

Despite having no training in economics or management, also in 1959, Che was appointed President of the National Bank of Cuba with the mission of nationalizing the banking sector to give Castro’s government control of Cuba’s financial system. The Soviet Union provided extensive economic support, including loans and grants in an attempt to stabilize Cuba’s economy. But the revolutionary government issued Law No. 890 in 1960, which nationalized all commercial and industrial establishments, effectively debanking the upper classes and foreign corporations. Here we go again.

But wait—there’s more! In February 1961, Guevara became the head of the Ministry of Industry responsible for transforming Cuba’s nationalized economy to eliminate financial incentives. The Communist policies Guevara implemented led to a rapid drop in productivity, increased absenteeism, the near collapse of sugar production, the failure of industrialization, and the introduction of rationing. This occurred in what had been one of Latin America’s four most economically successful countries.4 Failure to understand the role of incentives to create a robust economy is a recurring theme in communist regimes; this is why unhumans end up eating themselves, figuratively and sometimes literally.

Guevara also led the purging phase of Cuba’s revolution. As Commander at La Cabaña Fortress prison, he oversaw the executions of between five hundred and two thousand people in 1959 alone, including former government officials deemed complicit in Batista’s abuses of power, members of Batista’s army and police, as well as those deemed traitors, informants, or war criminals.

Others purged included Castro’s rivals in the Cuban Communist Party (of course), members of opposition parties, journalists, writers, human rights activists, gays, and clergy and lay members of religious groups, especially the Catholic Church. Guevara worked alongside Soviet-sent Angel Ciutah, a veteran of the losing side of the Spanish Civil War, in forming a new intelligence agency to do what communist intelligence agencies do.5

Soviet expansionist policy in Cuba came to a head in October 1962 with the infamous Cuban Missile Crisis—the thirteen-day confrontation between the United States and the Soviet Union over Soviet ballistic missiles deployed in Cuba, a convenient ninety miles from the US coast. Both powers reached the brink of nuclear war, but remember Atomic Diplomacy? Neither President John F. Kennedy nor Soviet Premier Nikita Khrushchev wanted to be the side that shot first for fear of the other’s retaliation. And so the conflict that almost brought World War III ended in a quasi-peace deal: The Soviets would get their missiles out of Cuba, and the US would not invade Cuba (again; see the failed US-backed Bay of Pigs Invasion to overthrow Castro). The US would also remove its own missiles, at that time stationed in Turkey.

The next headline-worthy moment in Cuban communism came in October 1965 from Che Guevara. He wrote a farewell letter to Castro, resigning his posts as minister and commander, renouncing his Cuban citizenship, and declaring his intention to leave Cuba to spread communism elsewhere. He was thankfully executed by the Bolivian army in October 1967, finally becoming a good communist.

Castro continued as Cuba’s dictator until turning over this role to his younger brother, Raul, in 2008. The latter died at the age of ninety in 2016. Cuba has never recovered from communism; society has suffered a tortuously slow death similar to the Soviet Union, their former backer. Times were tough under crony capitalism for the working class of Cuba. But under communism, it’s been absolute misery for all classes—except for party leaders who seized everything and shared it among friends. Today, Cubans own nothing and aren’t happy. Meanwhile, until the fall of the Soviet Union, Cuba was used as a distribution channel for communism throughout Latin America, as we will soon see.

Marxist Chile (1970–1973)

Never trust a communist to tell you the truth.

In Chile, the election of Salvador Allende in 1970 with help from the KGB brought into power a Marxist determined to seize totalitarian control.6, 7 Allende had close ties to Fidel Castro and the USSR, visiting Cuba and Russia and hosting a monthlong tour of Chile by Castro. Way back in 1933, Allende had cofounded Chile’s Socialist Party, which would eventually embrace violent revolution as a means of taking power, in 1967. Allende prepared to seize control of Chile by building a militia of 30,000 men, larger than the Chilean Army, and amassing caches of weapons all over the country supplied by the USSR. To take power, Allende’s supporters planned to plunge Chile into a civil war that would cost 500,000 to 1,000,000 lives, the higher estimate being 10 percent of the country’s population.

Then came the 1970 election. Allende won no authority from the people, as he only received 36.6 percent of the vote in a three-way election. But he lied to Chile’s congress, claiming he fully supported the constitution, so they would be persuaded to break the tie and choose him as president.8 It worked.

In power, Allende did as he pleased—the usual robbing and killing these revolutionaries always do. Allende ignored Chile’s Congress and court decisions, while his policies led to runaway inflation, shortages, strikes, protests, violence, and lawlessness. Allende stole businesses, both foreign and domestic, and land, calling his thefts “nationalization” and “land reform.”
