All this chaos provoked Chile’s House of Deputies, the lower house of its bicameral legislature, to prepare a resolution comparable to articles of impeachment for Allende that asked the military to remove Allende from office.

Responding to the pressure from the people and Chile’s Congress, Chile’s military did their duty and launched a rising on September 11, 1973. Allende refused an offer of safe passage and killed himself instead during the attack. A military council chaired by General Augusto Pinochet took control of Chile to restore order (which they did) and prosperity (which they did, the latter by hiring economists trained at the University of Chicago in the free market economic principles of Friedrich Hayek and Milton Friedman). Chile put in place a new constitution in 1980 that laid out a path for future elections and a peaceful transfer of power, which occurred after Patricio Aylwin defeated Pinochet in the presidential election in 1989 and Aylwin took office in 1990.

Pinochet offered reciprocal punishment to the communist revolutionaries, demoralizing their cause and diminishing their ranks. All allies of anti-civilization were ruthlessly excised from Chilean society. The story of tossing communists out of helicopters hails from Pinochet’s elimination of communism during the mid to late 1970s.

Wherever Pinochet was, there was no communism.

And the globalist intelligentsia didn’t like that. Not one little bit. In a matter of days after the successful military rising, human rights organizations that had taken no interest in the atrocities of Allende pounced on human rights violations committed in the process of purging Allende’s red minions from positions of power or influence, from which they had intended to slaughter Chile’s unsuspecting citizens. Since then, in an effort to discredit the free-market policies that made Chile thrive, leftists have been ceaseless in their efforts to hide both Allende’s crimes and Pinochet’s successes. (Also consider that Allende himself made Pinochet commander of the army the day after the Chamber of Deputies passed their articles of impeachment and had asked the military to take Allende out; Pinochet does not seem to have planned the military rising, as has been alleged and implied.)

Leftists also used lawfare to harass and discredit Pinochet after he stepped down from power to terrorize future leaders in South America and abroad from putting up effective resistance to communism. Amnesty International connived to create a new legal principle of “universal jurisdiction,” which was used by a leftist Spanish court to have then-Senator Pinochet arrested on October 16, 1998, while in London for back surgery.9

In a speech to the British House of Lords on July 6, 1999, former Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher summed up the dangers of the left’s vengeance campaign against Pinochet for defeating communism:

My final point concerns the implications of the Pinochet case for the conduct of international relations, which are essentially based on trust between nation states. This trust has now been shattered by the prospect of the courts in one country [Spain] seeking the extradition of former heads of government from a second country [the UK] for offences allegedly committed in a third country [Chile].

Senator Pinochet is, of course, being victimised because the organised international Left are bent on revenge. But on his fate depends much else besides. Henceforth, all former heads of government are potentially at risk; those still in government will be inhibited from taking the right action in a crisis, because they may later appear before a foreign court to answer for it and in a final ironic twist, those who do wield absolute power in their countries are highly unlikely now to relinquish it for fear of ending their days in a Spanish prison. This is a Pandora’s box which has been opened—and unless Senator Pinochet returns safely to Chile, there will be no hope of closing it.10

Pinochet was held from October 1998 to March 2000 under house arrest in London, when the British Home Secretary, Jack Straw, declared him unfit to stand trial for human rights violations on health grounds and allowed him to return to Chile, where he (and his family) were again pursued by lawfare until the end of his life. He was never convicted.

Pinochet died on December 10, 2006.

From this episode in the global war on global communism, we receive this succinct takeaway:

The mandate that led to Allende’s downfall came from the people of Chile. He caused suffering to gain absolute power, as communists always do. He had the men and weapons ready to defeat Chile’s military and kill upward of a million of his countrymen to gain absolute power, but Chile’s Congress, and Pinochet, with Chile’s military leaders and police, struck first.

Communists hate it when you do that, so when you do and they lose, they write the history from their perspective; as in the case of Franco in Spain, the loser’s version is the one remembered.

Pinochet did not allow communism to take over his country.

Cambodia’s Killing Fields (1975–1979)

Pol Pot is universally reviled and fairly so. His reputation precedes him, and he is one of the only communist dictators to have a Hollywood film made about him, The Killing Fields (1984). The most commonly known anecdote about his red reign of terror over southeast Asian country Cambodia is the sick fact that he ordered all Cambodians who wore glasses to be executed, as eyeglasses indicated superior intelligence and thus firmer resistance to the cause.11

Who the heck believes something like that? And what else does that person believe? Well, for perhaps the first time, we’d like you to learn the truth about Pol Pot in his own words, from a 1977 meeting of the Communist Party of Kampuchea (now Cambodia):

At the time that we were working out the Party’s line [i.e., mission, at the First Congress of the Party held in Phnom Penh on September 28, 29 and 30, 1960], Kampuchean society was divided into five distinct classes: the working class, the peasant class, the petty bourgeoisie, the bourgeoisie and the feudal class …

Which class most exploited the others? Which class was the most exploited and the most numerous?

An examination of the Kampuchean society of that time revealed that the peasants were 85 percent of the country’s entire population. Therefore, the peasants were the overwhelming majority of the population. They were exploited by all classes. They were exploited by the capitalists and the landlords. However, it was from the landlords that the peasants suffered the worst, most varied and most direct exploitation… .

The ruling classes were the feudalists, the landlords and the capitalists. How could they satisfy the demands of the peasants? They couldn’t. Their lies and their deceit could only help them for a time. When, after several actions, the peasants still had not recovered their lands, their discontent was transformed into anger, then class hatred, hatred arising from the class contradictions …

Thus, we were able to agitate among and mobilize 85% of the peasants throughout the country. It was a prodigious force, and that is why we dared to struggle. Our Party believed in the people. Why? Because the Party had clearly seen the class contradictions and seen that the people were struggling through their own movement to resolve them. Our role was only to guide them to unite their strength, so that they would not go into combat without concentrating their forces.

This was the analysis formulated by the First Congress. It opened the way to a correct orientation for our Party. If we could not have grasped it, we would have taken a wrong path.12

Despite Pol Pot’s rant against the landlords, nearly all peasants owned land—just not enough to cover the high taxes imposed by France when Cambodia belonged to the colony of French Indochina from 1867 to 1953. High taxes did what high taxes always do—bring social mobility to a screeching halt. Peasants became slaves to their moneylenders, as in pre-revolutionary Russia.13 As 85 percent of Cambodia’s population, peasants were the powder keg at the bottom of Cambodian society, and Pol Pot lit the match.

Prince Norodom Sihanouk, Cambodia’s leader under various titles from 1941 to 1970 and again from 1993 to 2004, educated his people—but not in practical skills that generate prosperity, thus creating a frustrated intelligentsia ripe for communist doctrine as a means for status. Sihanouk built roads, railroads, and a seaport but let Cambodia lag behind its neighbors in industrialization; he honored agriculture over industry.14 His attempt to ease the peasants’ tax debt plight with a credit system failed due to corrupt bureaucrats.15 He overlooked the one weird trick of lowering taxes, which would have let them keep enough of their own money both to get out of tax slavery and afford the tools and fertilizer to improve their crop yields. When the US showered foreign aid money on Cambodia in the 1960s during the Vietnam War, Sihanouk failed to put a stop to his military officers stealing the money and living large on their ill-gotten gains— corruption that tore the fabric of Cambodian society.16

Prince Sihanouk was ousted on March 18, 1970, in a vote taken by the National Assembly when he was out of the country. The National Assembly appointed General Lon Nol as their new leader. Supported by the US, Nol ruled as a dictator with a military and bureaucracy even more corrupt than they were under Prince Sihanouk.

Seizing their opportunity, the communist Khmer Rouge escalated their already antigovernment insurgency and plunged the country into civil war. Communist China directly supported the Khmer Rouge by providing military training, financial aid, and arms. This support was crucial for their ascendancy to power and the eventual maintenance of their regime, especially given the escalation of proxy war, from March 1969 to August 1973, as the US bombed Cambodia to demolish camps and supply lines of the Soviet-backed North Vietnamese, the Viet Cong insurgent fighters, and the Khmer Rouge government. This put millions of acres of cropland out of production, resulting in food shortages and refugee status for tens of thousands.17

Long story short, Prince Sihanouk was against the Khmer Rouge when he was in power but for them when he wanted to get back into power, even becoming their nominal head of state from 1975 to 1976. The result was that the people saw the Khmer Rouge as a nationalist force freeing them from tyranny. Instead, they were about to find out how bad tyranny could get.

Who better than Pol Pot, the leader of the Khmer Rouge, to explain how to incite revolution? Like the previous quote, the following is from a five-hour speech Pol Pot gave in the capital Phnom Penh on September 29, 1977, to reveal the existence of the Communist Party of Kampuchea, which had operated in secret since its founding by Pol Pot and twenty-one others way back in 1960. From that five-hour speech, remember the enemy he is referring to here are his own countrymen:

Open forms of struggle are not enough. They can only operate on the surface and do not penetrate deeply among the people. Let us take the case of the press. We could have published 100 dailies, but we would have only been able to write within the framework of laws of the state of the feudalist and capitalist classes. Thus, the revolutionary content could not have reached the people. The class content, the content of the struggle to overthrow the exploiting classes, could not have reached the people at the grassroots. It was only when we went to work among the people secretly that the revolutionary content penetrated to the people at the grassroots. We need correct lines of action and correct forms of struggle to be successful in our work, to mobilize our forces.

The Party also attached importance to a good division of labor among the cadres [full-time Party employees]. To some cadres, it assigned open work, to others, work as a deputy in the Assembly, or as a member of the government under the old regime, or as a functionary in the administration, as well as open work in various mass organizations and in the press. These are different forms which can be used to incite the masses. In this way, we divided work among ourselves.

However, there were some sections of clandestine work which were organized to carry out agitation secretly among the people. In Phnom Penh, there were sections which carried out open work, and sections which carried out secret work. It was the same in the countryside, there were some sections for open work and others for secret work. Secret work was the fundamental thing; it allowed us to defend the revolutionary forces and also allowed us to arouse the people. If all of us, or too great a number of us, had been working openly, the enemy would have been able to destroy many or all of us… .

We were able to carry on our struggle both in the countryside and in the cities, both secretly and openly, in the Assembly, in the government, in associations, mass organizations, the press, associations for the expansion of education, even in the cremation societies, associations for defense of the pagodas and associations for defense of Buddhism. We could wage the struggle there, mobilize the many to the greatest possible extent, using different forms and slogans …

We combined large, medium and small-scale attacks, made them follow one another ceaselessly, attacked day and night, in dry season and in rainy season, relentlessly, so as not to leave the enemy time to catch their breath or reorganize, consolidate and develop their forces. We divided the enemy into smaller units, attacking the flanks, the rear, the weak points, and avoiding a frontal attack at all times.

In this way, we could attack the enemy at any time, whether our forces were small, medium-sized or large. At the same time, we could preserve our forces and wipe out the enemy to the greatest extent possible.18

The Khmer Rouge captured Phnom Penh on April 17, 1975. First things first, they demanded everyone give up their guns. Next, they abolished money, the entire banking system, all private property, schools, and finally civil rights.

Then they went around the city telling everyone to evacuate immediately and leave all their things because they would be allowed to return in three days after the city had been swept for enemies. This was a lie. They were emptying all of Cambodia’s cities. The people had just become property of the state and were being sent to work on collective farms—or to be executed—so the Khmer Rouge could transform Cambodia into an ethnically pure communist utopia. Year Zero was announced, and all the old ways were purged—no pre–Khmer Rouge history, culture, traditions, or laws mattered. Nor did the people who believed in or followed them.

Yale genocide expert Ben Kiernan calculates that when the Khmer Rouge were in power from 1975 to 1979, they killed up to one-quarter of Cambodia’s population, as many as 1.8 million lives. Like the National Socialists of Germany, the Khmer Rouge worked to destroy Cambodia’s ethnic and religious minorities:

Of the 400,000 Vietnamese in Cambodia, Kiernan says 200,000 were expelled by the Lon Nol regime in 1970, Pol Pot drove out an additional 100,000 in 1975, and the rest were murdered.

In the mostly Muslim Cham community, there were 90,000 Cham deaths, 36 percent of their population.

Of the 425,000 Chinese in Cambodia in 1975, only 200,000 were left by 1979.

A 1975 Khmer Rouge report gloated that the Buddhist monasteries had been destroyed. Fewer than 2,000 of Cambodia’s 70,000 Buddhist monks remained in 1979.19

What made the Cambodians so vulnerable to genocide? In his memoir Survival in the Killing Fields, Dr. Haing Ngor speculates that it was karma. No, not literally karma but near-universal belief in karma. Perhaps the people of Cambodia submitted willingly to the Khmer Rouge because they believed they deserved what they brought. Perhaps this was how Pol Pot won—the operational preparation of the environment occurred first in the battlefield of the Cambodian psyche.20

So then what happened? Well, Cambodia transitioned from a Vietnamese-backed government in 1979 to a government supervised by the UN between 1991 and 1993, then finally to a constitutional monarchy in 1993. This made Norodam Sihanouk and the people of Cambodia the big winners because Sihanouk became king from 1993 until 2004, when he abdicated and was succeeded by his son, Norodam Sihamoni, who is still on the throne as of this writing.

Although the Khmer Rouge were driven from power in Cambodia by an invasion of the Vietnamese (yes, the Khmer Rouge were even too extreme for the communists), they retreated to remote bases near Thailand and conducted guerrilla warfare into the 1990s. After the death of Pol Pot in 1998, the surviving leaders either defected or were sent to prison, officially ending the Khmer Rouge.21 The last trial was held in 2018, finding the defendants guilty of genocide.

The Cambodian people, despite the horror they endured and which many did not survive, won in the end. Human rights were restored. The Cambodians ultimately won because Pol Pot went too far; the Vietnamese realized they didn’t need a genocidal bully next door, so they took him out. The old leadership was rescued and then made the most of it by transitioning to a constitutional monarchy with a constitution and the checks and balances of an executive branch, legislature, and judiciary. The people have a say in tax policy, have access to education, and have the possibility of social mobility.

We recommend rereading the Pol Pot speech excerpt quoted here. It is a rare and ghastly window into the unhuman soul. Deeds backed up those words. Communists then and there and now and here work to bring down the society they target, first by stealth in what seem to the unsuspecting to be normal encounters, then by violence, next by military action, and finally by genocide. When you see the pattern, you can stop it.

Do you see it?

A Long Red Night in Nicaragua (1978–1990)

Nicaragua’s President Anastasio Somoza Debayle was a son of a b—, but he was our son of a b—. He graduated from West Point, loved America, and hated communists.22 Somoza succeeded his father and brother, who had been the military leaders of the country since the 1930s. As Soviet-backed Sandinista rebels began to foment revolution in his country, he deployed the National Guard to conduct raids and sweeps in areas suspected of harboring leftist guerrilla groups. These military operations were aimed at rooting out Sandinista members and sympathizers. Suspected communists, leftists, and subversives were subject to arrest and detention. Somoza also employed intelligence gathering to monitor and track the activities of communist and leftist groups. This information was used to preemptively target and disrupt potential threats.

Clashes between revolutionaries and the Somoza government came to a head following the 1972 earthquake in Managua. The city was devastated and so was the economy; tens of thousands died, many thousands more were made homeless, and 80 percent of all commercial buildings in the capital city were no more.23 Somoza allegedly bungled the emergency response, as presidents are known to do, and he lost the support of many working-class Nicaraguans.24 His already strained relations with the West also worsened due also in part to Soviet-style disinformation that was happily spread by American journalists.

American academic and former Sandinista supporter Dr. Robert S. Leiken came clean in 1984 that he had been wrong to support the communists. After paying the country a visit himself, he realized he had been a victim of communist propaganda, saying, “American journalists have been taken in, co-opted in effect, by a larger group, the so-called Marxist internationalists … who express solidarity with the Sandinistas.” Dr. Leiken called out “censorship by omission,” an example of which was a joyous crowd of pro-Sandinista Nicaraguans … who had been told their ration cards would be seized if they did not attend the rally. Western journalists left out the latter bit. Fake news is not new. “If the media does not report inconvenient facts, how then is our Congress, whose perceptions are largely shaped by the media, supposed to make informed decisions?”25

Not just Congress, Dr. Leiken, but also the commander in chief. Somoza’s opposition to communist expansion in Latin America should have mattered to the US president at the time. It did not. Jimmy Carter, likely a believer of the disinformation coming out of Nicaragua, vowed in his inaugural speech in 1977:

Our commitment to human rights must be absolute … there can be no nobler nor more ambitious task for America to undertake on this day of a new beginning than to help shape a just and peaceful world that is truly humane.26

This book is about shaping a just and peaceful world that is “truly humane” by defeating the communist revolution and its nonbinary pink-haired cousin socialism, too. That takes care of the human rights question. And the noble and ambitious task we propose for America to undertake is to do just that.

We are wary of human rights organizations because it seems to us that the effect of their activism is to demonize self-defense against totalitarianism in all its forms, secular and religious, as they did with Franco, Pinochet, and President Somoza.

But Carter committed to human rights for the whole world as his priority—even though he was not president of the world, he was president of the United States of America and that was not the job we elected him to do.

So when human rights organizations complained about Somoza, Carter was moved to get involved. In the name of peace and human rights, he scared off Nicaragua’s allies, starved its treasury of the dollars it needed for defense by blocking its exports of coffee and beef, and when its last chance for self-defense against the KGB-backed Sandinista rebels was an Israeli ship filled with weapons and ammunition, he pressured Israel and the ship turned around and sailed home.

Somoza fled the country and stepped down on July 17, 1979, in large part because his National Guard ran out of bullets. This despite the fact his government was waging war against communist guerrillas intent on overthrowing the Somoza family dynasty that had ruled the country since 1936. President Somoza had previously identified a communist campaign to incite revolution as coming from the USSR through Cuba by the usual avenues of newspapers, university professors and students, and schoolchildren, plus a surprising new one: Jesuit priests, who propagated the communist message among the next generation of upper-class families.27

In a futile effort to show President Carter how the Communists were brainwashing young people, President Somoza wrote:

To illustrate my point, I used the Isle of Youth as an example. This small island lies about 250 kilometers south of Havana and it has been converted into a virtual Communist educational fortress. At that time I estimated that some ten thousand African students were on the Isle of Youth receiving indoctrination. That estimate has now been proven incorrect. There are over fifteen thousand African students on that island. They come from the Congo, Ethiopia, Mozambique, Namibia, Sao Tome, Angola, and the western Sahara. Published reports now positively show that the only students on this island who are not of African origin are from Nicaragua … I felt sure the White House and the State Department had the same information. They must have known, as I did, that thousands more students were on the mainland receiving the same indoctrination program. I used all of my intelligence and verbal skills to convince the United States that what was happening in Cuba was no short-term ideological briefing. I desperately wanted those representatives of Mr. Carter to know that this was for the long pull and that Cuba was playing “for keeps.”28

The Sandinistas had won. And so they did what all communists do: take everything. To give his readers an idea of the rights the Sandinistas seized from Nicaragua’s citizens when they came into power on July 19, 1979, President Somoza quotes the Sandinista Defense Committee’s Directive establishing the block captain system in the capital city of Managua published in La Prensa on September 29, 1979:

The counter-revolutionaries are becoming a threat to our revolution.

They are organizing and are harassing our militia, our army, and our military personnel. That is precisely why we at the CDS [initials in Spanish for the Sandinista Defense Committee] must assume the control and the vigilance of the counter-revolutionaries. As well as informing the security agencies. The CDS must be the eyes and the ears of the revolution. They must at this time be its principal defenders.

This control must be carried out as follows:

It is necessary to have a map of the suburb where you live.

A census must be taken to determine who lives in your block. Each block must make a list of its neighbors.

If a counter-revolutionary individual lives in that block, we will do the following: 

A. Locate his house

B. Make a file

C. Have constant vigilance of the identified element to know all his movements.

All the members of the CDS must carry out this vigilance and for that purpose it is important that they establish posts in each block. All night there must be watches by turns, each car that goes by, take down the make, color and license number. Every time the dog barks, see who goes by and where he is going. Keep watch to know which neighbor arrives late, if he carried packages or is with friends. Watch those houses where cars arrive late at night and take down all details. When you see someone who doesn’t belong in that section, watch him and follow him so that we know what he is doing. We must not let even one movement go by, since it could be the counter-revolutionaries.

Regarding the authorization of traveling, migration papers, there must be verification that the person who must approve departures is the person in charge of the Sandinista Block Committee.

All persons must carry a letter from his old Sandinista Block Committee to the new CDS of where he will be living.

Be alert to the following: 

A. If you have a Somocista neighbor: accuse him.

B. If you see a strange movement in your block: watch it!

C. Carry out your night watches to watch for counter-revolution!

SANDINISTA DEFENSE COMMITTEE (CDS) MANAGUA, D.N. September 29, 1979.29

As for all the things the Sandinistas seized and people they purged in 1979 when they came into power, President Somoza has the receipts. His memoir documents that the Sandinistas confiscated property of the Somoza family and all their associates and supporters and the property of all known sympathizers. More than 8,000 members of the National Guard were imprisoned together with 50,000 citizens suspected of Somoza support. Millions of dollars fled the country with more than 150,000 refugees who did not return.30 Furthermore, the Sandinistas harassed Christians of all denominations, seized church property, and demanded clergy preach only preapproved sermons.31

Along with the usual robbing came the killing. Immediately upon seizing power, they murdered (sometimes through torture) more than 5,000 enemies of the revolution.32 One of them, Somoza himself. A commando team from the Argentinian Revolutionary Workers’ Party assassinated President Somoza on September 17, 1980, with automatic weapons and rocket-propelled grenades as he rode in his armored Mercedes near his home in exile in Asunción, Paraguay.

In the following decade prior to their electoral defeat by Violeta Barrios de Chamorro in 1990, the Sandinistas did what Marxists do. They. Stole. Everything. It turns out that unhumans are for private property after all, as long as it is theirs.

Their thefts became known as the “Sandinista Piñata,” after the party game where children use a stick to hit a papier-mâché figure stuffed with candy and prizes until it spills its contents, and the children scramble to collect the most loot.33

The Sandinistas stole mansions, farms, businesses, factories, cars, fine art, cultural artifacts, historic relics, computers, video cameras, plus “motorcycles, farm tractors, military trucks … radio transmitters, shops and factories, hotels and restaurants, travel agencies and import-export offices, amusement parks and huge ranches holding the country’s most productive farmland.”34 Then they burned the records of their thefts and threatened crippling strikes if they weren’t allowed to keep their ill-gotten gains.

The most likely big winners are at the top of the heap of the Sandinista thieves, the brothers Humberto and Daniel Ortega. General Humberto Ortega was the defense minister in Daniel’s first term as president from January 10, 1985, until new president Violeta de Chamorro took office on April 25, 1990, and where he continued in command of the Nicaraguan army. Ibarz quotes an estimate of his fortune as $100 million.

After losing three consecutive elections for the presidency of Nicaragua in 1990, 1996, and 2001, Daniel Ortega was reelected in November 2006 and began his second term in 2007. As of the writing of this book, he is seventy-eight years old and serving his fifth term.

The communist Sandinistas were only able to oust President Somoza in July 1979 thanks to the support of US President Jimmy Carter after leftist human rights activists triggered his boundless moral vanity.

Violeta Barrios de Chamorro was able to win the presidency of Nicaragua in 1990 thanks to the support of US President Ronald Reagan for the Contras, who fought a guerrilla war against the Sandinistas. Chamorro stabilized Nicaragua’s economy through capitalism.

Somoza could have prevented all of this, but the US pulled their support for him, he was assassinated, and the communists took over the country—all because Jimmy Carter was a principled world leader.

How’d those principles work out?

The Afghan-Soviet War (1979–1989)

Let’s talk about 9/11—but only briefly. Specifically, who’s to blame. It might not surprise you that less than half (46 percent) of people surveyed believe Al-Qaeda under Osama bin Laden is to be held responsible. One-quarter of respondents blame the US government.35

September 11 is outside the scope of this book, but what’s well within scope is why the doubt? Could it be because the Central Intelligence Agency is credibly alleged to have supported mujahideen guerrillas and helped to train radical Muslims to fight in the proxy war in Afghanistan against the Soviet Union?36, 37 Yes, of course it could, and it probably is.

Like the US military under President Joe Biden, retreating in rushed disgrace from Afghanistan in January 2021, Soviet forces were also pushed out of Afghan territory decades prior. Why were they there in the first place? We’ll let you guess.

Yeah, you got it right: a communist revolution. It all started back in 1973 with a coup that kicked out the Afghan king and ended the monarchy. Mohammed Daoud Khan, a man of ambition and vision, toppled his cousin, King Zahir Shah, while he was abroad. Overnight, Afghanistan transitioned from a monarchy to a quasi-democratic republic with Daoud at the helm. His initial moves were bold and modern—pushing for women’s rights and societal reforms such as, surprise, surprise! land redistribution. But these seemingly anti-Islamic ambitions edged the nation closer to Soviet influence.38

Radical shifts to modernize Afghanistan rubbed traditionalists the wrong way, and by 1978, simmering tensions exploded into the Saur Revolution. A Marxist-Leninist faction, the People’s Democratic Party of Afghanistan (PDPA), ousted (and assassinated) Daoud in yet another coup. And so the Democratic Republic of Afghanistan was born, with the Soviet Union as acting midwife and the KGB attending.

To maintain that early grip on power, the Afghan communists imprisoned, tortured, and executed all who opposed. These brutal yet unsurprising tactics sparked a wildfire of resistance across the mountains and valleys of Afghanistan. Enter the mujahideen, a motley crew of fighters bound by their shared disdain for the PDPA’s atheistic and foreign-backed regime. While some were native Afghans and other Arab volunteers, these simple men united under a common banner of defending their faith and homeland against what they saw as an existential threat.

As the internal strife escalated, the Soviet Union, watching its new ally falter to next-generation warfare as the US recently had in Vietnam, decided to step in. December 1979 marked the beginning of a new chapter, as Soviet tanks rolled across the borders into Afghanistan. This wasn’t mere intervention; it was a full-scale invasion aimed at propping up the faltering communist regime.39

In the US, charismatic congressman Charlie Wilson championed the Afghan freedom fighters, ensuring that the mujahideen received not just weapons but also a place in the global spotlight as “the good guys.”40 And so the CIA launched Operation Cyclone, one of its longest and most secretive operations, funneling billions into supporting the mujahideen. The battleground of Afghanistan became a proxy stage for a larger duel between superpowers, with the US, Pakistan, Saudi Arabia, and others pouring extensive resources into the conflict.41

The war dragged on for nearly a decade. Even with 115,000 troops, the Soviets just couldn’t seem to squash the insurgency. It was a hydra; sever one head and still another appears. Eventually, with his vast empire facing imminent collapse, Soviet leader Mikhail Gorbachev pulled the plug. In 1988, the Geneva Accords were signed, and by February 1989, the last Soviet troops rolled up and out, leaving the Afghan communists to fend for themselves against the not-going-anywhere mujahideen. Fast-forward to 1992, and the communist republic fell just as the Soviet Union had. Afghanistan spiraled into a messy civil war, and by the mid 1990s, the Taliban had risen to power. That period of strife also paved the way for Al-Qaeda.

More than one million Afghan civilians lost their lives in the communist versus anti-communist war, with tens of thousands of actual Afghan and Soviet combatants killed. All for the dream of a Marxist utopia.

This is what they do.

Death Toll of Cold War Communist Revolutions: 4 million

c16F

CHAPTER 11

BLACK AND RED: THE UNTOLD STORY OF COMMUNISM IN AFRICA

The story of communist revolution in Sub-Saharan Africa has been recast as the noble struggle of liberation. There is among other liberal heroes the former political prisoner Nelson Mandela, healer and reuniter of his people following systematic suppression by white European settlers for more than 500 years. Much like the election of President Barack Obama in 2008, the presidency of Nelson Mandela, beginning in 1994, has been viewed as desperately needed reconciliation and an example for all other nations with similar complicated histories. If you saw Morgan Freeman and Matt Damon in Invictus (2009), you probably came away with the surface-level understanding of the post-apartheid era in South Africa having begun with national and multiracial pride through sport—in that film’s case, the 1995 Rugby World Cup. The legacy of colonizer Cecil Rhodes and his contemporaries across Europe, having plundered southern Africa and punished her people for generations, was at an end. Now there could be peace thanks to the first black president. A few years earlier, in 1980, the eponymous former Rhodesia had become Zimbabwe, declaring independence from European power and influence, leading the way for South Africa.

That’s the story we’re told by the media, in school, and on the internet just about everywhere you look. But it’s incomplete. Certain troubling inconvenient facts are left out, as they usually are when what really happened doesn’t fit the Cultural Marxist oppressor-versus-oppressed narrative. And that’s the case for the untold (true) story of communism in Africa. Add in the usually overlooked uncomfortable details, and the heroic drama of black liberation quickly mutates into a dark comedy of errors.

Rhodesia House Blues

This is the tale of a forgotten conflict of the Cold War.

A name you probably know well is Robert Mugabe, the Marxist-Leninist leader of the Patriotic Front, ruling party of Zimbabwe (formerly Rhodesia) since Great Britain negotiated the nation’s independence in 1980. Mugabe is one of the few facts that popular culture gets right about this place and period. Most clickbait-tier education about the world’s worst dictators include usual suspects Hitler, Stalin, and Mao (and recently President Trump due to the mental illness Trump Derangement Syndrome, or TDS). But those lists also often include Mugabe. Here’s how he came to power—and how he brought his nation and people down into ruin.

In 1965, Rhodesia was a colony that declared itself independent of the British Empire. In the 1960s and ’70s, black nationalist groups like the Zimbabwe African National Union (ZANU), led by Mugabe himself, and the Zimbabwe African People’s Union (ZAPU), led by Joshua Nkomo, waged a protracted guerrilla war against the white minority government of Rhodesia’s Ian Smith.1

Mugabe had been jailed from 1964–1974 for his paramilitary activities. After release, he fled to Mozambique where he led ZANU’s guerrilla forces in a war of liberation against white rule. The conflict escalated through the 1970s, with ZANU receiving backing from China while ZAPU was supported by the Soviet Union.2

According to United States Army Lt-Col Dave Grossman, in the 1970s, Rhodesia’s military was considered among the best trained globally, facing off against a poorly trained but well-equipped insurgent group with KGB sponsorship. Throughout the guerrilla conflict, Rhodesian security forces maintained an approximately eight-to-one kill ratio in their favor.

The Rhodesian Light Infantry, known for its high level of training, reported kill ratios from thirty-five-to-one up to fifty-to-one. Despite lacking close air and artillery support and not possessing a major advantage over their Soviet-backed adversaries, the Rhodesian forces’ exceptional training provided them with unmatched tactical superiority.

Under mounting military pressure and facing international sanctions due to Rhodesia’s legacy of white colonialism, the Smith regime was forced to the negotiating table with Great Britain and the majority-black opposition leaders. A ceasefire, new constitution, and multiracial elections brought ZANU an easy parliamentary majority and propelled Mugabe to prime minister of new Zimbabwe.3

Proclaiming a policy of reconciliation as South Africa would a few years later, Mugabe initially tried to allay the fears of the white population and international observers. However, tensions soon emerged between ZANU and ZAPU over who would rule. From 1982 through 1987, Mugabe sent the North Korean-trained Fifth Brigade to crush resistance in ZAPU strongholds in Matabeleland and the Midlands, killing thousands of Ndebele civilians in what became known as the Gukurahundi massacres. Revolutionaries always eat their own.4 Having neutralized his main rival Nkomo and ZAPU, Mugabe further consolidated power, becoming president and declaring ZANU the only legal political party. He then put equity into action regarding the white African population.5

Now, equity differs from equality in the insidious way you learned in Chapter 9. Remember the disparate impact doctrine of Cultural Marxism? Mugabe and ZAPU made it national policy. Consider land reform. Equality under the law would have allowed both white and black farmers legal standing to purchase and manage land for agricultural and other purposes. Equality is what unhumans say they want; whenever they win the chance to create it, they destroy all hope of equality. Instead, they drive for equity.

Disparate ownership of farmland in Zimbabwe was deemed unacceptable. Only 3 percent of the population-at-large had been white; yet whites owned 51 percent of farmland.6 Thus, in the Cultural Marxist frame of Mugabe, the disparate impact needed to be correct—blacks should have owned 97 percent of the farmland, not 51 percent.

And so Mugabe’s regime followed the same subversive plan of his predecessors Mao and Stalin—he ordered prosperous and profitable farms seized from capable whites and redistributed to black families, many of whom had neither the knowledge, nor the skill, nor the experience running a modern industrial farm. And yet this was only “fair,” as seen through the Cultural Marxist lens.

You may have seen occasional memes online mocking the Zimbabwean currency, notable for its $100 trillion notes, highest of any nationally issued currency in the world. A loaf of bread cost 300 billion Zimbabwean dollars. There’s hyperinflation and then there’s whatever the hell that was. In any case, the economic annihilation of Zimbabwe and the collapse of its agricultural productivity were direct results of anti-white land reform. And as is usually the case, when the unhumans come, they leave their victims without property, liberty, and life. White farmers whose families had worked the same land for generations were forced off their own property without any national or international legal recourse, yes, and of course, some less-than-enthusiastic farmers were murdered. By the early 2000s, most of the remaining white population had been ethnically cleansed, either slaughtered outright in semilegal white genocide, or driven off their land and expelled to South Africa. Cultural Marxism has consequences.7

Mugabe was finally ousted in a 2017 coup after four decades in power, but the revolution he’d forced his people into went nowhere. Modern-day Zimbabwe still fails the human rights check even and particularly among the black population. After all dissenting political parties were outlawed, a new “Patriotic Bill” eliminated free speech and freedom of association, instituting punishment for unapproved speech, particularly (well-earned) criticism of the government. Enemies of the state are arrested, tortured, and held in prison for years without bail or trial. The overwhelming majority of these victims are black. There are also plans for more tolls, levies, and taxes so all Zimbabweans are burdened, not just the most vocal among them.8 (Do we have to say it? We do.)

This is what they do.

“Kill the Boer”: New Apartheid in South Africa

The apartheid policy of South Africa mirrored that of Jim Crow in southern US states in multiple ways, racial segregation being the most obvious. The history of communist movements throughout the world has in times and places been shown to have real moral rot it attempted to extricate. In white South African society, blacks were second-class citizens; inequality under the law was the law. Rhodesia-turned-Zimbabwe told a similar story. And again, in South Africa as there, the goal of equality was not assigned a practical system to achieve it. Instead, the Cultural Marxists pushed right past it and plunged the country into a downward equity spiral. And that’s not even taking to account the fact that avowed communist Nelson Mandela, darling of the Western progressive elite, had been justly imprisoned for actual crimes, which as President Ronald Reagan put it in 1986, included “calculated terror … the mining of roads, the bombings of public places, designed to bring about further repression.”9 Yet another character sanitized for the affluent, like Che Guevara and others before him. (Look up “South Africa necklacing,” if you don’t believe us.)

The struggle against white South African rule stretched back many decades; the seeds of sudden uprising were often sown long prior, even before the eventual belligerents in revolutionary war were born. The African National Congress (ANC), founded in 1912, initially pursued a strategy of nonviolent resistance against segregation. However, the hardening of apartheid policies in the 1950s and the Sharpeville Massacre of 1960, where police killed sixty-nine mostly peaceful protesters, convinced many that more militant action was needed.10 In 1961, ANC leaders such as Nelson Mandela and Walter Sisulu formed Umkhonto we Sizwe (MK), the armed wing of the liberation movement. MK was given military and financial support by the Soviet Union, including training for ANC fighters by the KGB. MK launched a sabotage campaign President Reagan would later scorn. But Mandela was captured in 1962 and sentenced to life in prison for treason.11 Other top ANC leaders like Oliver Tambo and Joe Slovo went into exile and directed the struggle from abroad.

The next major watershed came after the Soweto Uprising of 1976, when police killed hundreds of students protesting the teaching of Afrikaans, a white African language, in schools. The unnecessary brutality of the government response fueled massive resistance and a new generation of activists like Steve Biko, who helped found the militant Black Consciousness Movement.12 The state cracked down harder, banning opposition groups and detained thousands. Biko was arrested and beaten to death in police custody in 1977, becoming a martyr of the anti-apartheid cause. Radicals then and now begin their work by rallying people with actual, genuine grievances; black South Africans had plenty, thus, violent resistance felt justified.

Through the 1980s, unrest spread as the United Democratic Front and other grassroots organizations made much of the country ungovernable. MK and the Azanian People’s Liberation Army (APLA), the armed wing of the Pan-Africanist Congress, intensified their guerrilla campaigns and bombings of military and government targets.13

Facing a spiraling crisis, escalating international pressure, and the end of the Cold War, the apartheid regime was finally forced to the negotiating table. In 1990, white South African President F. W. de Klerk unbanned opposition groups and released Mandela and other political prisoners. After difficult talks and continued violence by diehard elements on both sides, a settlement was reached for a transition to majority rule. The ANC won an overwhelming victory in the 1994 elections, making Mandela the country’s first black president.14

Everything thereafter was kumbaya, right? Wrong! Radical black South Africans, now with the political power they’d bitterly sought for decades, replaced the old racial caste system with a New Apartheid rather than merit-based laws. You can guess by now what that means: land reform! In post-apartheid South Africa, farms were seized from white owners by coercion and by force. The idea was to address historical inequalities and redistribute land to black South Africans affected by apartheid. And so the have-nots robbed the haves. They killed, too. To this day, horrific crimes against humanity stain South Africa’s name at home and abroad, with numerous reports of black gangs kidnapping and torturing white farmers to death, squatting on their land, and stripping the property bare to resell the parts for scrap. This is late-stage Cultural Marxism. It will probably get worse before it gets better. We’ve both done business with white South Africans; they’ve casually mentioned the goings-on of daily life: self-segregating for their own personal safety, owning (and occasionally having to use) firearms, hiring 24/7 private security, and staying as close to their private gated communities and near-maximum-security compounds. It’s just not safe to be white, in certain areas. The unhuman press denigrates South Africans for taking such measures, with one particular outlet lashing white South Africans as “oppressors” who once felt “so safe,” celebrating the fact that many no longer do (or are).15 Trying to protect yourself from roving terror mobs intent on robbing and killing you is racist, to the unhuman. Unfortunately, revolutionaries in the United States are intent on re-creating that New Apartheid domestically. This is only “fair.”

How is South Africa doing after they wrote racial quotas into their new constitution? The electrical grid is now failing across the country as of the time of this writing.

Let’s close this brief yet sobering chapter with a preview of our own possible future in the West (for among other reasons because by now you’ve gotten the full picture—unhumans pull the same tricks whenever and wherever they go; you don’t need encyclopedic detail). Far-left anti-white activist Julius Malema is known for preaching (and singing) hate toward white South Africans. A clip of Malema leading a packed stadium of thousands of black South Africans singing, “Kill the Boer,” referring to white South African descendants of seventeenth century Dutch settlers, went viral during our initial research for this book. Malema has been described by former president Jacob Zuma among other prominent South Africans as a future national leader.16 Moderates are less bullish on his odds, as his antics are terrible for a country with already worst-in-class public relations.17 One of Malema’s ambitions is, of course, more land reform—even more mandatory relinquishment of white-owned land across South Africa to the black population. He is popular.18

Most telling with regard to his future is how his open call for violence toward whites has been well-received (and acted upon) by young blacks under his influence—and how Western press interprets those remarks. Whereas a maligned figure like former President Donald J. Trump has metaphorical language like a “bloodbath” occurring in the American auto industry twisted into a literal promise of violence, Malema’s actual call to literally kill was interpreted in the brightest of possible lights.19 No, Malema was not calling for actual killing when he said, “kill,” wrote the New York Times, and those who took him at his word are “far-right.”20 The absurdity only makes sense when we realize the oppressor-versus-oppressed frame has been applied.

For black revolutionaries to call for the killing of whites is mere misbehavior, tradition, they don’t know any better. But for you to not want white people murdered is extremism.

This is what they do.

Death Toll of Communism in Africa: 50,000+ deaths, 1.5 million ethnically cleansed

c17N

CHAPTER 12

INSURRECTION AND THE IRREGULAR REVOLUTION

A revolution is not a dinner party, or writing an essay, or painting a picture, or doing embroidery … revolution is an insurrection, an act of violence by which one class overthrows another.

—Mao Zedong

Unhumans is far from a complete history of the communist revolution in all times and places. We could have covered the 1919 communist revolt in Berlin, Germany; the Soviet-supported Mongolian Revolution of 1921; and the extended Laotian Civil War, stretching from 1959 to 1975, that replaced the monarchy with communism, to name but three. There are so many stories we could tell. Alas, this is a book, not an encyclopedia; an overview, not a reference work. This is for the best, in our view. Because knowing what the unhumans do with the weapon of terror and the absolute power it brings them is not enough. And so additional coverage of communist history and depth of detail into it does not give us what we need next: what to do about it. Specifically, the knowledge, the resources, and the will to crush the revolution, wherever and whenever it occurs, even before it can occur. The past offers us myriad lessons. What Chapter 3 offered as preview—how to stop the left when they go too far—we now give you a fuller review. Before we get into that, we must address the lingering question some of you still have:

SO WHAT?

Yes. So what that the Roman plebeians got along poorly under the patricians. So what that French peasants occupied an old castle. That two Germans named Marx and Engels wrote a little book most modern people have never read. Or that Russia, China, and Spain shed their own blood in bitter internal conflicts. That’s just civil war. History offers plenty of examples of those. So what? Do we really see any situations in modern times playing out (or starting to) just like the Bastille, Ipatiev House, or the Great Leap Forward?

Well, there does seem to be a lot of that Operational Preparation of the Environment for Revolution going on, with regard to separating, messaging, and infiltrating. Chapter 3 offered several bloody examples. And as for direct parallels between past and present, a recent example in twenty-first-century America is that online activists have seized administrative and moderation control over most major US social media platforms. The right to free speech has been suspended by nonelected actors, while actual representatives of the citizenry do nothing about it but appear on friendly TV networks pointing out leftist hypocrisy, encourage us to “imagine if the situations were reversed,” and write sternly worded letters that nobody reads.

Meanwhile, the secret e-police operate the way Friends of the Soviet Union and other Soviet propagandists did to interact with individuals, groom the vulnerable ones, cancel the uppity ones, then form the most radical into mobs, as David Kowalski describes in Stalin and the Spanish Civil War. Near-identical social media manipulation by radicals is well-documented in among other places the Twitter Files, as released by Elon Musk, and yet has gone not at all well-retaliated against.1

OK, that parallel is a gimme. More are as easy to find. Already we’ve documented more throughout this book. But is this an exercise in logical fallacy? Are we confirming a conclusion decided in advance, then failing to introduce any evidence to the contrary? Is this all— gasp—a CONSPIRACY THEORY?!

These objections are fair. If you were to tell a neighbor or friend that any of this secret communist stuff means we’re about to have a Red Dawn scenario, they’ll ask if you polished your tinfoil hat today. We can’t really be serious about a coming American Bastille, Neo-Bolshevism, or One Giant Leap Forward for All Mankind pushed by the WEF … can we? After all, most Americans and other Westerners have it OK. We’re nowhere close to the techno-paradise of flying cars and freedom cities promised by 1950s futurism. But ask any one person on the streets, at work, or in church how they’re doing and they’ll tell you “fine.” Most people can afford a place to live, at least one car that works most of the time, and near-food products for three square meals a day.

And yet something is off. In 1999, 71 percent of Americans felt “satisfaction” with the way things were going overall. Twenty-five years later, that’s fallen to 19 percent.2 Life-course trajectories are lower now, too. Hopes, more pragmatic; expectations, sobering. We’ve realized that the great American dream was a promise with an expiration date. The internet and all its conveniences could not keep globalization from smashing the American producer economy into a crater of consumption.

Something is deeply wrong with the way things are going and you know it. You may not be able to explain it with studies, surveys, or statistics, but you feel it. You’ve felt this way for a while. Like there’s some outside force or group or … something … that’s sent us all off course from the libertarian utopia we should’ve achieved by now. It doesn’t seem like one -ism or -ation is entirely to blame, like globalism or immigration, capitalism or inflation.

The past makes sense of the present. Evidence of the unhuman activity is everywhere we look. But can we really pin all those on communists? Nobody pays attention to CPUSA. And there hasn’t been a Carmelite nun–style massacre. Or mass arrest and torture of landlords. But they’re arresting landlords in New York City, now. And yet … the history of the revolution … the present day … it feels directionally accurate, doesn’t it? We don’t want to hindcast or weave a convenient web of unrelated facts into a narrative that can’t hold the weight of two or three fact checks. Still, you can’t help but notice. Are others noticing, too? Is it just you? This demoralization, the demonetization, the demoting and demonizing. It’s real. All of it. Yet none of the authorities in charge of responding to real threats, foreign and domestic, seem to be doing anything. Not even talking about it, really. All rhetoric and funding is focused on endless overseas wars and whatever controversy the latest conservative public figure caused with a quote taken out of context. Meanwhile, things are getting worse, and it’s as if we’re not allowed to talk about why.

Yes. You’re on to something. What feels like is there, is there. And “it” is good at organizing and also at staying undetected. Moving below the radar. Operating in secret.

The terrible truth is that there is a distinct revolutionary movement we are witnessing in the modern-day West, one directly and indirectly responsible for all that demoralizing and demeaning. And yet it’s different from previous uprisings in visibility, publicity, and intensity. It is a revolution nonetheless and follows the same arc as every unhuman story does. It is:

The Irregular Communist Revolution

If you don’t understand gray zone warfare, you don’t know what’s going on. From the Atlantic Council:

The gray zone describes a set of activities that occur between peace (or cooperation) and war (or armed conflict). A multitude of activities fall into this murky in-between—from nefarious economic activities, influence operations, and cyberattacks to mercenary operations, assassinations, and disinformation campaigns. Generally, gray-zone activities are considered gradualist campaigns by state and non-state actors that combine non-military and quasi-military tools and fall below the threshold of armed conflict. They aim to thwart, destabilize, weaken, or attack an adversary, and they are often tailored toward the vulnerabilities of the target state.3

Put another way, a gray zone war is fought in ways that fail to provoke an organized, tit-for-tat response from the target. When you’re a gray zone warrior, they let you do it. You can do anything. On the international stage, the twenty-first-century Chinese Communist Party has achieved gray zone mastery, waging an irregular yet full-spectrum war against the United States, Europe, and Western allies throughout southeast Asia, from manufacturing fentanyl to kill unsuspecting American teenagers, to sending Chinese fishing boats into international waters with naval escorts to ward off other countries.

History does not repeat, but it sure as hell rhymes. What unhumans do anywhere, they do everywhere. And so the best frame for understanding the nearly universal malaise and low-intensity misery most Americans feel is this one:

We are suffering through an Irregular Communist Revolution.

Specifically, the Irregular Communist Revolution in the late-state republic West is a Cultural Marxist revolution—the targets are social and cultural, not economic. Just as military war has changed, so has revolutionary warfare. In gray zone conflicts, there is no distinction between civilian and combatant.4 Established international human rights separating the two are thus undone.5 Unhuman. This is what they do.

In the United States and throughout the West, the Cultural Marxist mental model of oppressor versus oppressed has separated the sociopolitical haves from the have-nots. This effort was begun in the 1960s in academia and has proven successful in all mainstream institutions. The dominant decision-making process of every major organization, public and private, now demands elevation of the alleged oppressed with subjection of the accused oppressor. This is what it means to be “woke,” by the way. Woke is shorthand for this lens through which you view: that all interactions, from everyday relationships to passing encounters to any conflicts along the way, are all intersections between someone of an oppressed class and someone of an oppressor class. Like classical Marxism, which cast any proletariat individual as a representative of all proletariats and any bourgeois as a representative of all bourgeois, woke unhumans the soul, turning people into labels with inherent value or disvalue. But it does so in a cultural rather than socioeconomic context—Cultural Marxism. By the way, “oppressed” and “oppressor” are misnomers, as actual evidence of oppression is not a prerequisite to wearing the label with pride. If someone of your race, gender, orientation, nationality, or other group experienced prejudice at some point in the past, then the “benefits” of that oppression are bequeathed to anyone taking up that label with pride (or comedic irony, hence the occasional obviously white person who identifies as black). Claims of that identity are enough as are assertions of oppression. The reason why is obvious: unhumans organize. They need as many people with them, not against them, as possible. The bar is low.

Operational preparation of all American environments via the separation, message, and infiltration stages are complete. And so we are all subjected to the communist revolution, fought and won in the gray zone, without the authorities recognizing this threat for what it is going on sixty-plus years now. The inciting, the seizing, and the purging—the three acts the revolution follows—do not usually take place in open-air environments with great concentrations of people, as they did in Russia, Spain, and China, with one recent notable exception: January 6, 2021.

January 6, 2021: America’s Inverse Bastille

After watching radical mobs burn and loot without consequence in the summer of 2020, supporters of then-President Donald J. Trump thought they could freely protest what in their view was a sketchy election result, with Trump losing to former Vice President Joseph Biden following an unprecedented nighttime suspension of vote counting in key states and precincts, with Biden miraculously leapfrogging Trump immediately after vote-counting resumed. Elections in the United States are supposed to be free and fair; these well-meaning patriots believed they could and should speak truth to power on power’s home turf—the US Capitol.

On January 6, 2021, thousands of conservatives walked into a lawfare trap used to destroy them and make them an example to any other Republicans who want to get uppity in the future. Entrapment has been a tool of subversion all the way back to Mao and the Hundred Flowers Campaign. The leftist press officially called the protest an “insurrection.” In terms of the violence on the steps, and there certainly was, it is clear the guards fired on a peaceful crowd with nonlethal munitions and flash-bangs. One of our authors, Jack Posobiec, was an in-person eyewitness to the flashbangs thrown into a peaceful crowd. That is what set everything off. The demonstration was not an insurrection. The insurrection hoax was used to begin a purge of Trump supporters from the military and from public life. This was an inverse Bastille; in the French Revolution, subversives attacked a government building to begin the purge of their enemies. In Washington, the subversives purged their enemies by means of the opposite, by luring them into a government building. It worked; a revolt against a largely law-abiding and nonviolent group of patriotic citizens and influencers on the American right had begun. It has been both brutal and total. Fortunes have been drained, accounts seized, and parents imprisoned away from their children with unusually long sentences for upgraded charges, the penalties for which did not fit the crime, which in most cases consisted of quietly walking around public property taking photos, escorted inside and around by police and other security. The have even convicted a praying grandmother. Going after religious women—sounds familiar.

Remember, the rhetoric is all just window dressing. What they do is what matters. The locking up of patriots was always the goal. This is what they do.

It was all a trap. President Trump possibly saw it for what it was and had preauthorized 10,000 National Guard troops to maintain law and order, protecting peaceful protesters from escalation by possible threats, federal informants, and planned agitators. Washington, DC, Mayor Muriel Bowser rejected the help, accepting only 350 and on a limited capacity.6

National Guard testimony later revealed that on the afternoon of January 6, 2021, the available DC National Guard were still “not allowed” to do their job “due to paralyzed decision-making by acting Secretary of Defense Chris Miller and Secretary of the Army Ryan McCarthy… . This led to a crisis in federal leadership at the Pentagon and delayed the DC response by 3 hours and 19 minutes.”7 The Commander-in-Chief’s wishes had gone unfulfilled to his and to the nation’s detriment; in any other situation, this would be described as a joint political and military coup d’état.

There was indeed an insurrection on January 6, 2021—against President Trump and his supporters. That is not even to mention the questionable election results; three in ten Americans believe Biden won the 2020 election only through fraud, with six in ten Republicans believing such.8, 9 Of course, claims of fraud were roundly rejected by mainstream experts on both sides—before investigations were conducted. Rasmussen Reports has since published extensive research and findings of meaningful anomalies and even “documented fraud.”10 These findings were never given a constitutionally provided hearing in the Senate on Jan 6.

The known facts now exonerate the January 6 protesters. And yet relentless pursuit of nonviolent attendees continues unabated. The federal government has gone full Zimbabwe on President Trump supporters, with political persecution for as many associated with the former president as they could reach. The victims of unseemly, unethical prosecution in Zimbabwe are black; most of those in the United States, white. As the Cultural Marxism frame predicts, Human Rights Watch advocates for the imperiled citizens of Zimbabwe and condemns the political prisoners in America, smearing all January 6 events participants as radical far-right and racist—and well-deserving of persecution.11 Human Rights Watch also falsely accused the majority-peaceful assembly in the US Capitol for “deaths … of security personnel.”12 This is not correct. The only individual killed during the January 6, 2021 protest and specifically as a result of the protest was, in fact, a protester—U.S. Air Force veteran Ashli Babbitt. The alleged insurrection is the prevailing narrative because the protesters were largely white, Christian, and Republican, all deemed oppressor classes through the Cultural Marxist lens. Ergo, all are guilty despite proof of innocence.

The events of January 6, 2021, were a spectacle and yet an anomaly in the revolutionary struggle in the United States.

But not all of the irregular revolution is as big as January 6. Most instances are smaller, yet extremely focused.

Let’s explain.

Microrevolutions

Most other battles against American citizens are more discreet, waged and won far from public view until the victim’s story generates half a day’s outrage on social media (when it’s too late). Why would they be as high-profile as January 6? No need. This is irregular warfare. Low-intensity conflict. The gray zone. Instead of armed mobs charging into government buildings and executing anyone they find … instead of united peasants dragging all landowners in town into the streets to be humiliated and tortured … instead of religious leaders all rounded up, shot, and dumped into mass graves they were forced to dig by hand prior … instead of repeating these obvious tactics, the modern revolutionaries engage in highly targeted “network swarms” of unhuman enemies and their allies targeting one person or one organization.13 Physical weapons are usually not used, but this cyberwarfare has an identical purpose: to neutralize the target. Weaponized social networks and user-generated warfare achieve an acceptable similar result that real-life mobs of ages past did: enemies of the revolution are silenced, and all who sympathize with them self-censor out of terror that they’re next.14 And they do it all without summoning a modern-day White Army or Francoist Nationalists to defend and fight back. Thus, every target is completely isolated from the help they need and totally unaware of what they are really up against. The context of low-intensity conflict makes sense of this; the key to irregular warfare is to wage war without provoking a kinetic response, i.e., military, police, and others authorized to use force.

And so the microrevolutions can come for anyone without those targets being able to fight back or necessarily having the legal backing to muster a response. They may not have literally robbed or killed— but they did enough damage in order to neutralize their target. It’s asymmetric warfare. Compacted, dense, tight period of fighting— without there being any fighting back.

In this way, the decades-long communist revolution taking place in the United States does not feel like a Bolshevik uprising, Chinese farmer revolt, or violent Spanish labor strike. Not unless you or someone you love becomes the target. Consider once again Scott Adams. Following his controversial comments in early 2023, the network of media institutions and social influencers who adhere to the oppressor versus oppressed frame were incited to act. Adams’s successful career as a syndicated cartoonist and traditionally published bestselling author were seized, as was his reputation in popular culture and good name online. All positive mention of Adams and his life’s work was then purged by search engines, most social media companies, and all internet encyclopedias that document the lives of public figures and notable people.

This was a microrevolution. Microuprising. Microrevolt. And the reason the unhumans do it is it works. And it works without provoking an organized response, reprisal, or revenge from any competing institutions or organizations. It’s a war fought in one direction. Conservatives, moderates, and farmers market liberals who are not independently wealthy cannot survive the gray zone gauntlet. Not just on the internet where everything is forever, or in their career where stable corporate employment may no longer be an option, or in their personal life where divorce and loss of their children may be guaranteed. But many made to suffer by the unhumans commit suicide, just as prisoners in the Soviet gulag did decades prior.15 Murder is one thing. Terrorizing you until you long for death is another. This is what they do.

A microrevolution is an important concept, and a weapon of the Cultural Marxist in the irregular revolution. The most common form of microrevolution is cancel culture directed against a single individual over a compressed period of time, which can be defined as a rapid and intense social movement aimed at holding a specific person “accountable” for their perceived transgressions, rooted in Cultural Marxist principles. This phenomenon involves a concentrated effort by a collective group to challenge the individual’s perceived power or privilege, often utilizing social media and online platforms as organizing tools. At its core, this form of microrevolution reflects Marxist ideas of class struggle and the redistribution of power. It manifests as a grassroots movement where the proletariat—the general public or marginalized communities—mobilize to confront and challenge the actions or statements of an individual perceived to represent the bourgeoisie or wield significant influence and privilege.

The process typically begins with the exposure of controversial behavior or statements by the targeted individual, often through social media platforms. As information (usually incorrect information) spreads rapidly, fueled by outrage and shared experiences, it catalyzes a swift and intense response from the community. This response may include calls for boycotts, demands for public apologies or resignations, and the withdrawal of support for the individual in question.

Within the Marxist framework, this microrevolution reflects a challenge to ideological hegemony—the dominant cultural beliefs and values perpetuated by those in power, so they say. Moreover, the compressed timeline of a microrevolution mirrors the urgency and immediacy often associated with Marxist revolutions. Rather than gradual change or reform, the focus is on rapid and decisive action to address perceived injustices and inequalities. The mob will not be satiated until it has consumed its victims. And thus, the instigators of the microrevolution are able to neutralize or purge their targets.

In irregular warfare, combatants engage in whatever they can get away with, even out of typical sequence so long as they get a win. Sometimes these pre-revolutionary and revolutionary events occur in the usual order. In others, out of order. Or the actual revolution happens concurrently or even prior to the OPE activities, with purging of the past followed by new messaging to “pre-suade” disaffected groups, followed by their unleashing to loot without legal consequences. Only those who resist the anarcho-tyranny are punished. This is now the case in major US cities with defunded police and activist district attorneys.

The Scott Adams story is not an isolated incident. His episode is one among many of easy-to-find examples of the low-intensity, highly targeted uprising against normal people the unhumans force on individuals every single day. Consider any canceled or beguiled public figure, any normal one with normal views that even the elite held during the Barack Obama administration. What happened to them? All of them? The mob was incited, the person’s livelihood was seized, and their credibility and opportunities purged. Every. Single. Time.

And so the gray zone revolution marches onward, targeting any of us who dare to refuse a loyalty pledge to the oppression model of reality. This is at great personal risk because the environment has been made unhuman; the presumed-innocent and assumed-guilty have been identified. Accusations and manipulations have already been decided. And all institutions and organizations meant to protect law, order, and human rights have been infiltrated. Dreamers stop dreaming as big. Innovators stop innovating. Investors stop investing. And those who do, keep as low a profile as they can. Big risks that could take civilization forward into a new area of progress and beauty aren’t worth it since they draw the wrong kind of attention from the wrong people. If you are too successful, and you do not pledge support for the revolution in any way it’s demanded of you, then you’re next. This is a problem. And as a result of it, history has paused on us. Civilization is halted. Stagnating. Festering. Existing without really living. Like cancer. Unhuman.

Together with the broken mental model we introduced you to in Chapter 2, this reframe of America’s present crisis as a gray-zone communist revolution makes it all make sense. It maps well, so well that you are now able to predict with confidence which types of people will be targeted by the unhumans, what will be said of them, how they will be exiled to modern-day Siberia—debanked, deplatformed, and otherwise destroyed—and why. A worldview that predicts with accuracy is as good as true.

Now, this new paradigm may not map 100 percent perfectly onto the sociopolitical or economic situation of every person living in a Western country. We get it. There are other issues to contend with. Yes. We understand that. And yet we argue that the Irregular Revolution, a.k.a. “Communist Revolution 2.0” is so likely to be 100 percent real and responsible for the big stuff that our proposition is too high-risk to dismiss. We’re talking about unhumans here. They are real now as they have been for more than a quarter of the last millennium. Wherever and whenever they emerge, they abolish rights to life, liberty, and property. This is what they do. These days, they’re just sneakier about how they do it. Are you cool with that?

We’d ask any normies who disagree with our hypothesis that we are enduring a gray-zone communist revolution the following question: What if there is a 10 percent chance we are correct?

A 10 percent chance. That you, reading this right now, are at moderate to high risk of being targeted for “e-ssassination” by unhumans online or with means far worse in the real world. Anarcho-tyranny already has years of precedent. A 10 percent chance. What if?

We’ll up the ante with another question. Set aside likelihood; consider utility. Does the Irregular Revolution frame predict with meaningful accuracy? Let’s say you had adopted this frame within the last five years. Would you have been able to predict the news with accuracy? You would have predicted that, for example, criminals would be set free and police officers and citizens acting in self-defense would be charged. Has that happened? You’d have predicted that private property of various haves would be seized by have-nots, with that theft made legal. Has that happened? You’d also have predicted politically motivated lawfare and other persecution of the enemies of Cultural Marxism. Has that happened? What about rage mobs incited overnight to destroy the reputations and livelihoods of nonpublic figures over the alleged offense of saying or doing the wrong thing, with that thing being any word or deed deemed that of oppressor harming oppressed?

Yes. All that and more has come to pass. Run that projection into the future. The frame predicts.

Suppose you’re with us. You agree. You assess the risk. You realize the stakes. You know how fragile civilization is and how ruthless are the forces against it. You understand what’s really going on. What then should we do? Well, we’ll tell you what not to do first and foremost. Actually, we’ll let Generalissimo Franco tell you:

We do not believe in government through the voting booth. The Spanish national will was never freely expressed through the ballot box. Spain has no foolish dreams.16

To any good little conservatives reading, please don’t get your polyester made-in-USA panties in knots. Do what radicals don’t and consider the context of the quote. Franco, speaking in 1938 in the midst of brutal war against the unhumans of his day, understood that force must be met with force. Consider that the anarcho-tyrants of Spain had been elected by the people. Then they reigned over them with terror.

Our study of history has brought us to this conclusion: Democracy has never worked to protect innocents from the unhumans. It is time to stop playing by rules they won’t.

Unhumans cannot be shamed into reform either; charging them with hypocrisy for praising a murderer and denigrating the victim as happened in the tragedy of Laken Riley is to wave the white flag of surrender. They know they are holding normal, beautiful people to a different standard than the miserable wretched monsters. This is what they do. Ms. Riley was white and therefore should have known better than to jog alone.
